### [1] [Recursive Language Models Meet Uncertainty: The Surprising Effectiveness of Self-Reflective Program Search for Long Context](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15653)
*Keivan Alizadeh,Parshin Shojaee,Minsik Cho,Mehrdad Farajtabar*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes SRLM, a self-reflective framework that improves long-context reasoning in language models by using uncertainty signals to select better context-interaction programs, outperforming previous recursive approaches like RLM.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although language models have larger context windows, they still struggle to reliably extract, reason over, and utilize information in very long inputs. Recursive Language Models (RLM) address this with recursive programmatic decomposition, but their performance heavily depends on how context-interaction programs are chosen—a problem that has not been systematically studied. The authors aim to understand and improve this program selection process to make long-context handling more robust and effective.

Method: The authors introduce SRLM, which augments programmatic context interaction with uncertainty-aware self-reflection. SRLM generates multiple candidate context-interaction programs and evaluates them using three intrinsic uncertainty signals: (1) self-consistency across multiple reasoning trajectories, (2) reasoning length as a proxy for deliberation, and (3) the model’s own verbalized confidence. These signals are combined to compare and select better programs without requiring explicit recursion or self-query. The framework is tested across diverse benchmarks, context lengths, and backbone models, under comparable time budgets to RLM.

Result: Across a range of datasets, tasks, context lengths, and base models, SRLM consistently outperforms state-of-the-art baselines, including RLM, achieving up to 22% improvement over RLM under the same computational/time budget. The experiments show that recursion alone is not the main factor behind RLM’s gains; instead, better program search and selection via self-reflection can match or exceed RLM’s performance. They also find that, for contexts that fit within the base model’s window, adding recursion in RLM can actually reduce performance compared to the base model, whereas SRLM maintains performance gains. In semantically demanding tasks, where simple heuristic program search fails, SRLM’s semantic, uncertainty-based signals provide a more effective guide for reasoning.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that effective long-context handling depends more on intelligent, uncertainty-aware selection of context-interaction programs than on recursion per se. SRLM’s self-reflective program search can outperform recursive architectures like RLM, especially in semantically complex tasks and across both short and long contexts, all without additional mechanisms such as explicit self-query or recursion. This suggests that embedding self-reflection and intrinsic uncertainty estimation into programmatic interaction frameworks is a promising direction for scaling language model reasoning over long contexts.

Abstract: Long-context handling remains a core challenge for language models: even with extended context windows, models often fail to reliably extract, reason over, and use the information across long contexts. Recent works like Recursive Language Models (RLM) have approached this challenge by agentic way of decomposing long contexts into recursive sub-calls through programmatic interaction at inference. While promising, the success of RLM critically depends on how these context-interaction programs are selected, which has remained largely unexplored. In this paper, we study this problem and introduce SRLM, a framework that augments programmatic context interaction with uncertainty-aware Self-Reflection. SRLM leverages three intrinsic signals: self consistency, reasoning length, and verbalized confidence. These serve as complementary indicators of a model's internal uncertainty, and the model uses them to evaluate and compare candidate context-interaction programs. Extensive experiments across diverse benchmark datasets, context lengths, and backbone models, show that SRLM consistently outperforms state-of-the-art baselines, yielding up to 22% improvement over RLM under the same time budget. Our findings show that recursion itself is not the primary driver of performance in RLM, and a simple self-reflective program search can match or surpass RLM without requiring self-query or explicit recursion mechanisms. We find that for context lengths within the model's window, RLMs with recursion often degrade performance relative to the base model, whereas SRLM yields consistent gains across both short and long contexts. We also find that RLM is less effective in tasks with semantically intensive nature, where heuristic program search is insufficient and broader contextual understanding is required, while self-reflection in SRLM provides a semantic signal that better steers reasoning in these scenarios.

</details>


### [2] [MedArena: Comparing LLMs for Medicine-in-the-Wild Clinician Preferences](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15677)
*Eric Wu,Kevin Wu,Jason Hom,Paul H. Yi,Angela Zhang,Alejandro Lozano,Jeff Nirschl,Jeff Tangney,Kevin Byram,Braydon Dymm,Narender Annapureddy,Eric Topol,David Ouyang,James Zou*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces MedArena, an interactive, clinician-in-the-loop platform for evaluating medical LLMs using real clinical queries and pairwise model comparisons, revealing discrepancies between benchmark performance and real-world clinical utility.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Static, templated benchmarks for medical LLMs (e.g., MedQA-style factual recall) do not reflect the complexity, context, and interaction patterns of real clinical practice. This leads to a mismatch between high benchmark scores and actual usefulness to clinicians, and it obscures what qualities clinicians truly value in model outputs (e.g., depth, clarity, nuance versus just factual correctness). The authors aim to create an evaluation framework grounded in authentic clinician needs and preferences to better guide model development and deployment in healthcare.

Method: They build MedArena, a web-based interactive evaluation system where clinicians submit their own medical questions. For each query, the platform randomly selects two LLMs from a pool of 12 and displays their responses side by side. Clinicians choose which response they prefer and optionally explain why. These pairwise preferences are collected and modeled using the Bradley–Terry framework to derive a global ranking of models. The authors also analyze the distribution and nature of clinician queries (task types, single vs. multi-turn) and conduct analyses to disentangle style factors (e.g., length, formatting) from perceived quality, checking whether rankings are robust when controlling for these factors.

Result: From 1,571 pairwise preferences gathered up to November 1, 2025, across 12 LLMs, Gemini 2.0 Flash Thinking, Gemini 2.5 Pro, and GPT-4o emerge as the top-ranked models by Bradley–Terry scores. Only about one-third of real clinician queries resemble traditional factual recall benchmarks, while most focus on applied, context-rich tasks like treatment decisions, documentation, and patient communication, with about 20% involving multi-turn dialogues. Qualitative feedback indicates clinicians more frequently value depth, detail, clarity, and readability over mere factual accuracy when explaining their preferences. Additional analyses show model rankings remain stable after adjusting for style-related variables such as response length and formatting, suggesting that underlying clinical utility drives preferences more than superficial stylistic differences.

Conclusion: MedArena demonstrates that live, clinician-driven, pairwise comparisons of LLM outputs provide a more realistic and clinically relevant evaluation of medical models than static benchmarks. The findings underscore that real-world clinical queries are more complex and interactional than standard test sets and that clinicians prioritize nuanced, clearly presented reasoning. The platform offers a scalable path to continuously evaluate, compare, and improve medical LLMs based on authentic clinician preferences, helping align model development with real-world clinical needs and utility.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly central to clinician workflows, spanning clinical decision support, medical education, and patient communication. However, current evaluation methods for medical LLMs rely heavily on static, templated benchmarks that fail to capture the complexity and dynamics of real-world clinical practice, creating a dissonance between benchmark performance and clinical utility. To address these limitations, we present MedArena, an interactive evaluation platform that enables clinicians to directly test and compare leading LLMs using their own medical queries. Given a clinician-provided query, MedArena presents responses from two randomly selected models and asks the user to select the preferred response. Out of 1571 preferences collected across 12 LLMs up to November 1, 2025, Gemini 2.0 Flash Thinking, Gemini 2.5 Pro, and GPT-4o were the top three models by Bradley-Terry rating. Only one-third of clinician-submitted questions resembled factual recall tasks (e.g., MedQA), whereas the majority addressed topics such as treatment selection, clinical documentation, or patient communication, with ~20% involving multi-turn conversations. Additionally, clinicians cited depth and detail and clarity of presentation more often than raw factual accuracy when explaining their preferences, highlighting the importance of readability and clinical nuance. We also confirm that the model rankings remain stable even after controlling for style-related factors like response length and formatting. By grounding evaluation in real-world clinical questions and preferences, MedArena offers a scalable platform for measuring and improving the utility and efficacy of medical LLMs.

</details>


### [3] [MiroThinker-1.7 & H1: Towards Heavy-Duty Research Agents via Verification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15726)
*MiroMind Team,S. Bai,L. Bing,L. Lei,R. Li,X. Li,X. Lin,E. Min,L. Su,B. Wang,L. Wang,L. Wang,S. Wang,X. Wang,Y. Zhang,Z. Zhang,G. Chen,L. Chen,Z. Cheng,Y. Deng,Z. Huang,D. Ng,J. Ni,Q. Ren,X. Tang,B. L. Wang,H. Wang,N. Wang,C. Wei,Q. Wu,J. Xia,Y. Xiao,H. Xu,X. Xu,C. Xue,Z. Yang,Z. Yang,F. Ye,H. Ye,J. Yu,C. Zhang,W. Zhang,H. Zhao,P. Zhu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: MiroThinker-1.7 is a research agent for complex long-horizon reasoning, and MiroThinker-H1 is an enhanced version with integrated verification that achieves SOTA on deep research tasks and is released (1.7 and 1.7-mini) as open source.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing language-model-based research agents struggle with reliable, sustained multi-step reasoning over long horizons, especially for complex research, scientific, and financial tasks. The authors aim to improve both the robustness of step-by-step interactions and the trustworthiness of final answers.

Method: They introduce MiroThinker-1.7, trained with an agentic mid-training stage that emphasizes structured planning, contextual reasoning, and tool use to improve per-step reliability. They then extend this to MiroThinker-H1, which builds in verification at both local (intermediate decisions) and global (entire reasoning trajectory) levels, allowing inference-time evaluation, refinement of reasoning, and auditing of chains of evidence.

Result: On benchmarks spanning open-web research, scientific reasoning, and financial analysis, MiroThinker-H1 achieves state-of-the-art results on deep research tasks, while maintaining strong performance in specialized domains. The base and mini versions of MiroThinker-1.7 are efficient, open-source models that deliver competitive research-agent performance.

Conclusion: Structured agentic mid-training plus integrated multi-level verification significantly enhance long-horizon reasoning reliability and performance. The released MiroThinker-1.7 and 1.7-mini models make these research-agent capabilities practically accessible and efficient for broader use.

Abstract: We present MiroThinker-1.7, a new research agent designed for complex long-horizon reasoning tasks. Building on this foundation, we further introduce MiroThinker-H1, which extends the agent with heavy-duty reasoning capabilities for more reliable multi-step problem solving. In particular, MiroThinker-1.7 improves the reliability of each interaction step through an agentic mid-training stage that emphasizes structured planning, contextual reasoning, and tool interaction. This enables more effective multi-step interaction and sustained reasoning across complex tasks. MiroThinker-H1 further incorporates verification directly into the reasoning process at both local and global levels. Intermediate reasoning decisions can be evaluated and refined during inference, while the overall reasoning trajectory is audited to ensure that final answers are supported by coherent chains of evidence. Across benchmarks covering open-web research, scientific reasoning, and financial analysis, MiroThinker-H1 achieves state-of-the-art performance on deep research tasks while maintaining strong results on specialized domains. We also release MiroThinker-1.7 and MiroThinker-1.7-mini as open-source models, providing competitive research-agent capabilities with significantly improved efficiency.

</details>


### [4] [Morphemes Without Borders: Evaluating Root-Pattern Morphology in Arabic Tokenizers and LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15773)
*Yara Alakeel,Chatrine Qwaider,Hanan Aldarmaki,Sawsan Alqahtani*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how well large language models and their tokenizers handle Arabic root-pattern morphology, testing whether they truly learn morphological structure or just memorize surface forms.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Arabic has a complex, non-concatenative root-pattern morphology that is an ideal testbed for understanding whether LLMs actually model morphology or only rely on surface statistics. There is also a widespread assumption that more morphologically aware tokenization should help downstream modeling, but this assumption is not well tested, especially for Arabic.

Method: First, the authors evaluate how closely various Arabic and multilingual tokenizers match gold-standard morphological segmentations (morphological fidelity). Second, they build a new test set to measure LLMs’ ability to productively generate correct Arabic root–pattern combinations. They run experiments on seven Arabic-focused and multilingual LLMs and pair each with its own tokenizer to compare tokenizer–morphology alignment with generative performance.

Result: They find that some tokenizers are much better aligned with gold-standard Arabic morphology than others, but this alignment does not reliably predict the model’s ability to generate correct root–pattern forms. In other words, models with morphologically faithful tokenization are not consistently better at productive morphological generation, and models with weaker morphological tokenization can still perform well.

Conclusion: Tokenizer–morphology alignment is neither necessary nor sufficient for strong morphological generation in Arabic LLMs. This calls into question the common assumption that explicitly morphological tokenization by itself drives better downstream morphological behavior, suggesting that model training and internal representations may play a more important role than tokenization granularity alone.

Abstract: This work investigates how effectively large language models (LLMs) and their tokenization schemes represent and generate Arabic root-pattern morphology, probing whether they capture genuine morphological structure or rely on surface memorization. Arabic morphological system provides a rich testbed for analyzing how LLMs handle complex, non-concatenative forms and how tokenization choices influence this process. Our study begins with an evaluation of morphological fidelity across Arabic and multilingual tokenizers against gold-standard segmentation, followed by an analysis of LLM performance in productive root-pattern generation using a newly developed test set. Our findings across seven Arabic-centric and multilingual LLMs and their respective tokenizers reveal that tokenizer morphological alignment is not necessary nor sufficient for morphological generation, which questions the role of morphological tokenization in downstream performance.

</details>


### [5] [COGNAC at SemEval-2026 Task 5: LLM Ensembles for Human-Level Word Sense Plausibility Rating in Challenging Narratives](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15897)
*Azwad Anjum Islam,Tisa Islam Erana*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents an LLM-based system for rating the plausibility of word senses in short stories, compares prompting strategies, and shows that comparative prompting and LLM ensembling yield strong performance and better alignment with human judgments.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To tackle SemEval-2026 Task 5, which involves grading how plausible specific word senses of homonyms are in narrative contexts, and to understand how different prompting strategies and model ensembling impact LLM performance on subjective semantic evaluation tasks with high annotator disagreement.

Method: The authors experiment with multiple closed-source LLMs under three prompting paradigms: (i) simple zero-shot prompting, (ii) Chain-of-Thought prompting with explicit structured reasoning, and (iii) comparative prompting that jointly evaluates multiple candidate senses. They then build ensembles that average predictions across models and prompting setups to better approximate the distribution of human ratings.

Result: Their official competition system—an ensemble over models and all three prompting strategies—ranked 4th, achieving 0.88 accuracy and 0.83 Spearman correlation. Subsequent post-competition experiments with additional models pushed performance to 0.92 accuracy and 0.85 Spearman’s rho, improving the overall score from 0.86 to 0.89.

Conclusion: Comparative prompting offers consistent gains across different LLMs, and averaging predictions in an ensemble setup notably improves agreement with mean human judgments. This indicates that LLM ensembles are particularly effective for subjective, annotation-variable semantic tasks such as plausibility rating of word senses.

Abstract: We describe our system for SemEval-2026 Task 5, which requires rating the plausibility of given word senses of homonyms in short stories on a 5-point Likert scale. Systems are evaluated by the unweighted average of accuracy (within one standard deviation of mean human judgments) and Spearman Rank Correlation. We explore three prompting strategies using multiple closed-source commercial LLMs: (i) a baseline zero-shot setup, (ii) Chain-of-Thought (CoT) style prompting with structured reasoning, and (iii) a comparative prompting strategy for evaluating candidate word senses simultaneously. Furthermore, to account for the substantial inter-annotator variation present in the gold labels, we propose an ensemble setup by averaging model predictions. Our best official system, comprising an ensemble of LLMs across all three prompting strategies, placed 4th on the competition leaderboard with 0.88 accuracy and 0.83 Spearman's rho (0.86 average). Post-competition experiments with additional models further improved this performance to 0.92 accuracy and 0.85 Spearman's rho (0.89 average). We find that comparative prompting consistently improved performance across model families, and model ensembling significantly enhanced alignment with mean human judgments, suggesting that LLM ensembles are especially well suited for subjective semantic evaluation tasks involving multiple annotators.

</details>


### [6] [Agent-based imitation dynamics can yield efficiently compressed population-level vocabularies](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15903)
*Nathaniel Imel,Richard Futrell,Michael Franke,Noga Zaslavsky*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper unifies evolutionary game theory with the Information Bottleneck framework to show how efficient, information-theoretic vocabularies can emerge through imprecise strategy imitation in signaling games.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While natural language vocabularies appear to be shaped by pressures for efficient information compression, the concrete social and evolutionary mechanisms that could drive this optimization are unclear. Separately, evolutionary game theory models explain how signaling systems and proto-languages might emerge from interactions of agents, but it is unknown whether such dynamics actually yield information-theoretically efficient codes in the Information Bottleneck sense. The authors aim to bridge this gap by providing a mechanistic account of how near-optimal compression might evolve in populations of agents engaged in communication.

Method: The authors build a unified model that embeds signaling games (from evolutionary game theory) within the Information Bottleneck framework. In their population model, agents repeatedly play signaling games and adapt their signaling strategies via an "imprecise imitation" dynamic: they tend to copy more successful strategies but with limited precision and with some probability of confusing similar underlying states. They then analyze the emergent vocabularies in terms of their position on the IB complexity–accuracy tradeoff curve and study how key parameters—precision of imitation, perceptual confusion between states—shape the resulting tradeoffs.

Result: The simulations show that the population dynamics can spontaneously produce vocabularies that lie near the optimal Information Bottleneck tradeoff between complexity (vocabulary size/structure) and accuracy (meaning resolution). Variation in model parameters, such as how precisely agents can imitate strategies and how often they confuse similar states, systematically constrains where on the complexity–accuracy curve the emergent vocabularies fall. Across a range of conditions, the emergent codes approximate information-theoretically efficient compressions and resemble empirically observed properties of natural vocabularies.

Conclusion: The study demonstrates that simple, independently motivated evolutionary game dynamics are sufficient to drive a population toward near-optimally compressed vocabularies in the Information Bottleneck sense. This provides a candidate mechanistic explanation for how natural languages might evolve lexicons that achieve efficient information-theoretic tradeoffs, linking agent-level learning and imitation processes to population-level optimization of vocabulary structure.

Abstract: Natural languages have been argued to evolve under pressure to efficiently compress meanings into words by optimizing the Information Bottleneck (IB) complexity-accuracy tradeoff. However, the underlying social dynamics that could drive the optimization of a language's vocabulary towards efficiency remain largely unknown. In parallel, evolutionary game theory has been invoked to explain the emergence of language from rudimentary agent-level dynamics, but it has not yet been tested whether such an approach can lead to efficient compression in the IB sense. Here, we provide a unified model integrating evolutionary game theory with the IB framework and show how near-optimal compression can arise in a population through an independently motivated dynamic of imprecise strategy imitation in signaling games. We find that key parameters of the model -- namely, those that regulate precision in these games, as well as players' tendency to confuse similar states -- lead to constrained variation of the tradeoffs achieved by emergent vocabularies. Our results suggest that evolutionary game dynamics could potentially provide a mechanistic basis for the evolution of vocabularies with information-theoretically optimal and empirically attested properties.

</details>


### [7] [CTG-DB: An Ontology-Based Transformation of ClinicalTrials.gov to Enable Cross-Trial Drug Safety Analyses](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15936)
*Jeffery L. Painter,François Haguinet,Andrew Bate*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They build an open-source pipeline and database that converts all ClinicalTrials.gov XML data into a standardized, MedDRA-based relational database to enable large-scale, placebo-referenced pharmacovigilance analyses.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: ClinicalTrials.gov contains vast safety information, but its registry-oriented structure and non-standard, text-based adverse event terminology make it hard to perform systematic pharmacovigilance analyses and cross-trial safety aggregation without extensive manual work.

Method: They created CTG-DB, an open-source pipeline that ingests the full ClinicalTrials.gov XML archive and outputs a relational database. The system preserves arm-level denominators and explicitly represents placebo and comparator arms. It normalizes adverse event terminology to MedDRA concepts using deterministic exact and fuzzy matching, providing transparent, reproducible mappings from free-text AEs to standardized identifiers.

Result: The result is a transformed, standardized relational database derived from ClinicalTrials.gov that aligns adverse events with MedDRA terminology and retains trial-arm structure, enabling concept-level retrieval and cross-trial aggregation suitable for scalable pharmacovigilance analyses.

Conclusion: By structurally transforming ClinicalTrials.gov data and standardizing adverse event terminology to MedDRA, CTG-DB makes it possible to perform large-scale, placebo-referenced safety analyses and to integrate clinical trial evidence more directly into downstream pharmacovigilance signal detection workflows.

Abstract: ClinicalTrials.gov (CT.gov) is the largest publicly accessible registry of clinical studies, yet its registry-oriented architecture and heterogeneous adverse event (AE) terminology limit systematic pharmacovigilance (PV) analytics. AEs are typically recorded as investigator-reported text rather than standardized identifiers, requiring manual reconciliation to identify coherent safety concepts. We present the ClinicalTrials.gov Transformation Database (CTG-DB), an open-source pipeline that ingests the complete CT.gov XML archive and produces a relational database aligned to standardized AE terminology using the Medical Dictionary for Regulatory Activities (MedDRA). CTG-DB preserves arm-level denominators, represents placebo and comparator arms, and normalizes AE terminology using deterministic exact and fuzzy matching to ensure transparent and reproducible mappings. This framework enables concept-level retrieval and cross-trial aggregation for scalable placebo-referenced safety analyses and integration of clinical trial evidence into downstream PV signal detection.

</details>


### [8] [BANGLASOCIALBENCH: A Benchmark for Evaluating Sociopragmatic and Cultural Alignment of LLMs in Bangladeshi Social Interaction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15949)
*Tanvir Ahmed Sijan,S. M Golam Rifat,Pankaj Chowdhury Partha,Md. Tanjeed Islam,Md. Musfique Anwar*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces BANGLASOCIALBENCH, a benchmark to test whether LLMs can use Bangla in socially and culturally appropriate ways, not just fluently.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multilingual LLM evaluations mostly focus on grammaticality, fluency, and factual knowledge, overlooking whether models can respect social hierarchies, relational roles, and interactional norms that are crucial in high-context languages like Bangla. The authors want to expose and study these sociopragmatic gaps.

Method: They build BANGLASOCIALBENCH, a 1,719-example benchmark authored and validated by native Bangla speakers, covering three domains: (1) Bangla address terms (e.g., honorific and informal pronouns), (2) kinship reasoning (correctly mapping and using kinship terms), and (3) social customs (appropriate linguistic behavior in culturally specific situations). They then evaluate 12 contemporary LLMs in a zero-shot setting to measure sociopragmatic competence and identify error patterns.

Result: Across models, they find systematic cultural misalignment: LLMs overuse highly formal address forms, miss cases where multiple address pronouns would be acceptable, and confuse kinship terms across religious and cultural contexts. Errors are structured, recurring, and tied to specific sociopragmatic dimensions rather than being random noise.

Conclusion: Current LLMs, despite strong Bangla fluency, exhibit persistent and patterned failures in sociopragmatic competence. BANGLASOCIALBENCH exposes these limitations and provides a targeted benchmark for developing and evaluating models that better capture culturally and socially appropriate language use in Bangladeshi contexts.

Abstract: Large Language Models have demonstrated strong multilingual fluency, yet fluency alone does not guarantee socially appropriate language use. In high-context languages, communicative competence requires sensitivity to social hierarchy, relational roles, and interactional norms that are encoded directly in everyday language. Bangla exemplifies this challenge through its three-tiered pronominal system, kinship-based addressing, and culturally embedded social customs. We introduce BANGLASOCIALBENCH, the first benchmark designed to evaluate sociopragmatic competence in Bangla through context-dependent language use rather than factual recall. The benchmark spans three domains: Bangla Address Terms, Kinship Reasoning, and Social Customs, and consists of 1,719 culturally grounded instances written and verified by native Bangla speakers. We evaluate twelve contemporary LLMs in a zero-shot setting and observe systematic patterns of cultural misalignment. Models frequently default to overly formal address forms, fail to recognize multiple socially acceptable address pronouns, and conflate kinship terminology across religious contexts. Our findings show that sociopragmatic failures are often structured and non-random, revealing persistent limitations in how current LLMs infer and apply culturally appropriate language use in realistic Bangladeshi social interactions.

</details>


### [9] [POLAR:A Per-User Association Test in Embedding Space](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15950)
*Pedro Bento,Arthur Buzelin,Arthur Chagas,Yan Aquino,Victoria Estanislau,Samira Malaquias,Pedro Robles Dutenhefner,Gisele L. Pappa,Virgilio Almeida,Wagner MeiraJr*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Introduces POLAR, a per-user lexical association test in embedding space, to capture author-level linguistic associations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing intrinsic association probes typically work at word, sentence, or corpus level, which hides variation between individual authors. There is a need for a method that can analyze lexical associations at the per-user level to better understand behaviors like bot activity and ideological drift.

Method: POLAR represents each author through private deterministic tokens in a lightly adapted masked language model’s embedding space. It then projects these author vectors onto curated lexical axes corresponding to specific attribute sets. The system computes standardized association effects, estimates permutation-based p-values, and applies Benjamini–Hochberg correction for multiple testing, yielding statistically controlled, per-author association scores.

Result: On a balanced Twitter dataset with human accounts and LLM-based bots, POLAR clearly separates bots from organic users. Applied to an extremist forum, it measures strong association with specific slur lexicons and detects a temporal rightward drift in language use. The method is shown to be modular to new attribute lexicons and to provide compact diagnostics for each author.

Conclusion: POLAR offers a general, modular framework for per-user lexical association testing in embedding space, enabling fine-grained, statistically rigorous diagnostics of author-level linguistic patterns. It is effective for distinguishing bots from humans and for tracking shifts in language alignment over time, making it useful for computational social science. The implementation is publicly available for further use and extension.

Abstract: Most intrinsic association probes operate at the word, sentence, or corpus level, obscuring author-level variation. We present POLAR (Per-user On-axis Lexical Association Re-port), a per-user lexical association test that runs in the embedding space of a lightly adapted masked language model. Authors are represented by private deterministic to-kens; POLAR projects these vectors onto curated lexicalaxes and reports standardized effects with permutation p-values and Benjamini--Hochberg control. On a balanced bot--human Twitter benchmark, POLAR cleanly separates LLM-driven bots from organic accounts; on an extremist forum,it quantifies strong alignment with slur lexicons and reveals rightward drift over time. The method is modular to new attribute sets and provides concise, per-author diagnostics for computational social science. All code is publicly avail-able at https://github.com/pedroaugtb/POLAR-A-Per-User-Association-Test-in-Embedding-Space.

</details>


### [10] [A Family of LLMs Liberated from Static Vocabularies](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15953)
*Aleph Alpha,:,Adnen Abdessaied,Artur Baranowski,Lukas Balles,Michael Barlow,Fabien C. Y. Benureau,Felix Berkenkamp,Lukas Bluebaum,Bastian Boll,Thomas F. Burns,Björn Deiseroth,Constantin Eichenberg,David Friede,Pablo Iyu Guerrero,Ahmed Hammam,Bastian Harren,Johann Higl,Yasser Jadidi,Carina Kauf,Johannes Messner,Jan Hendrik Metzen,Max Meuer,Vedant Nanda,Pit Neitemeier,Koen Oostermeijer,Letitia Parcalabescu,Markus Pernpointner,Felix Reinfurt,Dylan Rodriquez,Grégory Schott,Philipp Siedler,Martin Simonovsky,Till Speicher,Volker Stampa,Stephan Wäldchen,Samuel Weinbach,Gregor Ziegltrum*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose and evaluate a tokenizer-free, byte-level hierarchical autoregressive transformer (HAT) variant of Llama models, showing better robustness and benchmark performance while releasing all models and checkpoints.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLMs rely on learned tokenizers with large, fixed vocabularies that are hard to adapt to new domains and languages and are brittle to spelling and intra-word variation. The authors want a more flexible, tokenizer-free approach that still leverages powerful existing LLM backbones and scales to tens of billions of parameters.

Method: They use the hierarchical autoregressive transformer (HAT) architecture: an encoder transformer reads raw bytes and aggregates them into word-level embeddings, a backbone (standard autoregressive transformer) processes these as a language model, and then a decoder cross-attends to the backbone outputs to convert back into bytes. They adapt existing Llama 3.1 8B and 70B models by removing their embedding and output layers and reusing the transformer blocks as the backbone, while training new byte-level encoders/decoders from scratch, producing Llama-3.1-8B-TFree-HAT and Llama-3.1-70B-TFree-HAT. They also train a separate 7B HAT model from scratch on ~4T words. Pretraining is followed by supervised fine-tuning and direct preference optimization in English and German.

Result: The HAT models operate directly at the byte level while internally using a compressed word-embedding sequence, reducing the number of positions needed versus standard byte models and improving text compression efficiency. They demonstrate improved robustness to spelling and intra-word variations and achieve strong performance in both English and German, outperforming the original Llama 3.1 models on most evaluated benchmarks. They also provide extensive resources by releasing models and 200 pretraining checkpoints on Hugging Face.

Conclusion: Tokenizer-free, byte-level HAT variants of large Llama models can match or exceed the performance of token-based LLMs while being more robust and flexible across languages and spellings, and while using fewer effective sequence positions. The approach allows reusing powerful pretrained backbones, making it a practical path toward tokenizer-free large-scale language modeling.

Abstract: Tokenization is a central component of natural language processing in current large language models (LLMs), enabling models to convert raw text into processable units. Although learned tokenizers are widely adopted, they exhibit notable limitations, including their large, fixed vocabulary sizes and poor adaptability to new domains or languages. We present a family of models with up to 70 billion parameters based on the hierarchical autoregressive transformer (HAT) architecture. In HAT, an encoder transformer aggregates bytes into word embeddings and then feeds them to the backbone, a classical autoregressive transformer. The outputs of the backbone are then cross-attended by the decoder and converted back into bytes. We show that we can reuse available pre-trained models by converting the Llama 3.1 8B and 70B models into the HAT architecture: Llama-3.1-8B-TFree-HAT and Llama-3.1-70B-TFree-HAT are byte-level models whose encoder and decoder are trained from scratch, but where we adapt the pre-trained Llama backbone, i.e., the transformer blocks with the embedding matrix and head removed, to handle word embeddings instead of the original tokens. We also provide a 7B HAT model, Llama-TFree-HAT-Pretrained, trained entirely from scratch on nearly 4 trillion words. The HAT architecture improves text compression by reducing the number of required sequence positions and enhances robustness to intra-word variations, e.g., spelling differences. Through pre-training, as well as subsequent supervised fine-tuning and direct preference optimization in English and German, we show strong proficiency in both languages, improving on the original Llama 3.1 in most benchmarks. We release our models (including 200 pre-training checkpoints) on Hugging Face.

</details>


### [11] [MoLoRA: Composable Specialization via Per-Token Adapter Routing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15965)
*Shrey Shah,Justin Wagle*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose per-token routing across multiple LoRA adapters (MoLoRA), enabling each token to use the most suitable specialized adapter, which yields better performance‑per‑parameter than scaling a single large model.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multi-adapter systems route entire sequences to a single adapter, which fails when one request needs multiple domain or modality specializations (e.g., mixed text–image or mixed math–code tasks). There is a need for modular, composable expertise without retraining large unified models.

Method: Introduce per-token routing that assigns each token to an adapter, using vocabulary-based routing for multimodal tokens and learned gating for semantic specialization. They design MoLoRA (Mixture of LoRA), which loads multiple domain-specific LoRA adapters into a base model and uses a trained router to choose the adapter per token. They analyze the computational complexity, showing per-token routing uses O(N) adapter work for N tokens versus O(KN) for per-sequence routing with K adapters.

Result: Per-token routing is theoretically optimal in adapter usage and, empirically, MoLoRA lets a small base model (Qwen3‑1.7B with multiple LoRAs) outperform a much larger model (Qwen3‑8B) on four reasoning benchmarks, despite being 4.7x smaller in parameter size.

Conclusion: Composable, per-token adapter routing enables modular, domain-specialized expertise at inference time. Independent LoRA experts can be trained separately, combined without joint retraining, and extended by simply adding new adapters, yielding better performance–efficiency tradeoffs than scaling monolithic models.

Abstract: Multi-adapter serving systems route entire sequences to a single adapter, forcing a choice when requests span multiple domains. This assumption fails in two important settings: (1) multimodal generation, where text and image tokens require different adapters within the same sequence, and (2) mixed-capability requests like "write code to solve this equation," which need expertise from multiple specialized adapters. We introduce per-token routing, which routes individual tokens to adapters based on either vocabulary structure (for multimodal models) or learned gating (for semantic specialization). Per-token routing is provably optimal, achieving work N for N tokens versus K \cdot N for per-sequence routing with K adapter types. Our key contribution is MoLoRA (Mixture of LoRA), which enables composable specialization: load multiple domain-specific adapters and let a learned router select the appropriate adapter per-token. We demonstrate that specialization dramatically beats scale: MoLoRA enables Qwen3-1.7B to exceed Qwen3-8B across four reasoning benchmarks while being 4.7x smaller. This enables modular expertise at inference time: train focused LoRAs independently, combine them without retraining, and add new capabilities by simply loading new adapters.

Method: They design PALLM, a paralinguistics-aware speech LLM trained in a two-stage pipeline. The two tasks are: (1) sentiment/emotion classification directly from audio, and (2) paralinguistics-aware response generation. Abstract: Speech large language models (LLMs) observe paralinguistic cues such as prosody, emotion, and non-verbal sounds--crucial for intent understanding. We propose multi-task reinforcement learning (RL) with chain-of-thought prompting that elicits explicit affective reasoning. To address data scarcity, we introduce a paralinguistics-aware speech LLM (PALLM) that jointly optimizes sentiment classification from audio and paralinguistics-aware response generation via a two-stage pipeline. ### [14] [NLP Occupational Emergence Analysis: How Occupations Form and Evolve in Real Time -- A Zero-Assumption Method Demonstrated on AI in the US Technology Workforce, 2022-2026](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15998)
*David Nordfors*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a taxonomy-free method to detect when a real occupation is emerging, using resume data to find mutually reinforcing clusters of people and shared vocabulary. Applied to AI jobs in 2022–2026 US resumes, it finds that while an AI-related vocabulary quickly became cohesive, the people using it did not form a distinct occupational group—suggesting AI is spreading across existing jobs rather than forming a new standalone occupation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional occupational classifications (e.g., SOC, O*NET) update slowly and rely on predefined job titles and taxonomies, making them poor at tracking fast-changing work, especially in areas like AI. The authors want a principled, data-driven way to detect whether something like “AI work” is actually becoming a new occupation, as opposed to just a set of tools diffusing into existing jobs. They also want a concept of “genuine occupation” that is structural and observable in data, not just based on labels.

Method: They define a “genuine occupation” as a bipartite co-attractor: a self-reinforcing structure in which (1) a cohesive professional vocabulary pulls together a set of practitioners, and (2) that cohesive practitioner set in turn stabilizes and reinforces the vocabulary. Using large-scale resume data, they create a zero-assumption pipeline that doesn’t depend on predefined occupational taxonomies or job titles. They separately measure: (a) vocabulary cohesion: how strongly a set of terms co-occur and differentiate a group of resumes from others; and (b) population cohesion: how strongly a set of resumes are tied together by shared terms and similar work history. They then use ablation tests to see whether removing the key vocabulary breaks the population cluster, to verify that the vocabulary is actually the binding mechanism. This is applied to 8.2M US resumes from 2022–2026, focusing especially on AI-related terms and groups.

Result: The method successfully recovers known, established occupations as co-attractors, validating the approach. When applied to AI-related resumes, it uncovers strong vocabulary cohesion emerging rapidly in early 2024—AI tools, concepts, and practices form a clear, shared language. However, there is no corresponding stable, cohesive practitioner population: people who use this vocabulary remain embedded in diverse existing occupations rather than forming a new, distinct AI occupational cluster. The pre-2024 AI community that was more cohesive appears to dissolve as AI tools spread into the broader labor market. The new AI vocabulary is largely absorbed into existing job roles instead of anchoring a new stand-alone occupation.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that AI, at least in the period 2022–2026, behaves more like a general-purpose, diffusing technology than a new occupation. There is an occupationally meaningful vocabulary, but not an occupationally distinct community. This challenges narratives that assume a clear-cut new “AI occupation” is already established. They suggest that creating an explicit “AI Engineer” occupational category might, if adopted, help solidify the practitioner side of the co-attractor (population cohesion) around the already cohesive vocabulary, potentially allowing a true AI occupation to crystallize. More broadly, the paper offers a general framework and operational method for detecting the emergence or dissolution of occupations in real time from resume data.

Abstract: Occupations form and evolve faster than classification systems can track. We propose that a genuine occupation is a self-reinforcing structure (a bipartite co-attractor) in which a shared professional vocabulary makes practitioners cohesive as a group, and the cohesive group sustains the vocabulary. This co-attractor concept enables a zero-assumption method for detecting occupational emergence from resume data, requiring no predefined taxonomy or job titles: we test vocabulary cohesion and population cohesion independently, with ablation to test whether the vocabulary is the mechanism binding the population. Applied to 8.2 million US resumes (2022-2026), the method correctly identifies established occupations and reveals a striking asymmetry for AI: a cohesive professional vocabulary formed rapidly in early 2024, but the practitioner population never cohered. The pre-existing AI community dissolved as the tools went mainstream, and the new vocabulary was absorbed into existing careers rather than binding a new occupation. AI appears to be a diffusing technology, not an emerging occupation. We discuss whether introducing an "AI Engineer" occupational category could catalyze population cohesion around the already-formed vocabulary, completing the co-attractor.

</details>


### [15] [RadAnnotate: Large Language Models for Efficient and Reliable Radiology Report Annotation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16002)
*Saisha Pradeep Shetty,Roger Eric Goldman,Vladimir Filkov*

TL;DR: RadAnnotate is an LLM-based framework that uses retrieval-augmented synthetic radiology reports and confidence-based selective automation to efficiently label entities for RadGraph, reducing expert annotation effort while maintaining high accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Manual annotation of radiology reports for clinical NLP resources like RadGraph is time-consuming and expensive, yet high-quality labeled data is crucial for downstream tasks. There is a need to reduce expert workload without significantly sacrificing annotation quality, especially for challenging categories such as uncertain observations and in low-resource settings where labeled data are scarce.

Method: The paper proposes RadAnnotate, an LLM-based annotation framework focusing on RadGraph-style entity labeling. The approach has three main components: (1) training entity-specific classifiers on gold-standard reports and analyzing their performance across anatomy and observation categories to understand strengths and weaknesses; (2) generating retrieval-augmented (RAG-guided) synthetic radiology reports, training models on these synthetic labels, and comparing them with gold-trained models, including evaluation of synthetic data augmentation benefits, particularly in low-resource conditions; and (3) learning entity-specific confidence thresholds that allow the system to automatically annotate reports when the model is confident, while routing low-confidence cases to human experts for review, implementing selective automation.

Result: Entity-specific classifiers perform differently across categories, with uncertain observations being the most difficult to model. Models trained only on RAG-guided synthetic reports achieve performance close to models trained on gold-standard data, within 1–2 F1 points. In low-resource settings, adding synthetic data particularly improves performance on uncertain observations, raising F1 from 0.61 to 0.70. With learned confidence thresholds, RadAnnotate can automatically annotate between 55% and 90% of reports while maintaining entity match scores between 0.86 and 0.92, passing only the more uncertain cases to experts.

Conclusion: RadAnnotate demonstrates that LLM-based, retrieval-augmented synthetic data and confidence-based selective automation can substantially reduce the need for manual expert labeling of radiology reports for RadGraph-style entity annotation without major loss in quality. Synthetic training data are especially valuable for difficult, low-resource categories like uncertain observations. The framework provides a practical path toward scalable, semi-automated annotation pipelines in clinical NLP, with relation extraction identified as an important direction for future work.

Abstract: Radiology report annotation is essential for clinical NLP, yet manual labeling is slow and costly. We present RadAnnotate, an LLM-based framework that studies retrieval-augmented synthetic reports and confidence-based selective automation to reduce expert effort for labeling in RadGraph. We study RadGraph-style entity labeling (graph nodes) and leave relation extraction (edges) to future work. First, we train entity-specific classifiers on gold-standard reports and characterize their strengths and failure modes across anatomy and observation categories, with uncertain observations hardest to learn. Second, we generate RAG-guided synthetic reports and show that synthetic-only models remain within 1-2 F1 points of gold-trained models, and that synthetic augmentation is especially helpful for uncertain observations in a low-resource setting, improving F1 from 0.61 to 0.70. Finally, by learning entity-specific confidence thresholds, RadAnnotate can automatically annotate 55-90% of reports at 0.86-0.92 entity match score while routing low-confidence cases for expert review.

### [16] [Understanding Moral Reasoning Trajectories in Large Language Models: Toward Probing-Based Explainability](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16017)
*Fan Huang,Haewoon Kwak,Jisun An*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how large language models use and switch between different moral frameworks during multi-step reasoning, and how this affects stability, susceptibility to persuasion, and coherence.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs are increasingly used in morally sensitive decision-making, it is important to understand not just their final answers but the structure and consistency of the ethical reasoning that leads to those answers.

Method: The authors define "moral reasoning trajectories" as sequences of ethical framework invocations across intermediate reasoning steps. They analyze these trajectories across six models and three benchmarks, use linear probes to localize framework-specific representations in model layers, apply lightweight activation steering to adjust framework integration patterns, and introduce a Moral Representation Consistency (MRC) metric that quantifies the consistency of moral representations over a trajectory.

Result: They find that moral reasoning typically involves multi-framework deliberation with frequent switching and that only a small fraction of trajectories maintain a single framework. Trajectories with unstable framework usage are about 1.29x more vulnerable to persuasive attacks. Linear probes can identify layers where moral frameworks are encoded better than a prior baseline. Activation steering reduces framework drift and strengthens the link between stability and accuracy. The proposed MRC metric strongly correlates with human coherence ratings, and its framework attributions are supported by human annotators.

Conclusion: Moral reasoning in LLMs is inherently multi-framework and often unstable, and this instability is linked to susceptibility to persuasion and lower coherence. However, framework representations are localizable and steerable in model activations, and the MRC metric offers a reliable way to assess and potentially improve the consistency and coherence of LLM moral reasoning.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) increasingly participate in morally sensitive decision-making, yet how they organize ethical frameworks across reasoning steps remains underexplored. We introduce \textit{moral reasoning trajectories}, sequences of ethical framework invocations across intermediate reasoning steps, and analyze their dynamics across six models and three benchmarks. We find that moral reasoning involves systematic multi-framework deliberation: 55.4--57.7\% of consecutive steps involve framework switches, and only 16.4--17.8\% of trajectories remain framework-consistent. Unstable trajectories remain 1.29$\times$ more susceptible to persuasive attacks ($p=0.015$). At the representation level, linear probes localize framework-specific encoding to model-specific layers (layer 63/81 for Llama-3.3-70B; layer 17/81 for Qwen2.5-72B), achieving 13.8--22.6\% lower KL divergence than the training-set prior baseline. Lightweight activation steering modulates framework integration patterns (6.7--8.9\% drift reduction) and amplifies the stability--accuracy relationship. We further propose a Moral Representation Consistency (MRC) metric that correlates strongly ($r=0.715$, $p<0.0001$) with LLM coherence ratings, whose underlying framework attributions are validated by human annotators (mean cosine similarity $= 0.859$).

### [18] [ClaimFlow: Tracing the Evolution of Scientific Claims in NLP](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16073)
*Aniket Pramanick,Yufang Hou,Saif M. Mohammad,Iryna Gurevych*

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing citation and claim analysis methods treat citations coarsely and fail to model the fine-grained argumentative relations between specific scientific claims across papers. This prevents a clear understanding of how ideas are supported, reshaped, or contested as the field evolves, and limits the ability to train models that truly interpret scientific argumentation.

Method: The authors manually annotate 304 ACL Anthology papers with 1,084 explicit claims and 832 directed cross-paper claim relations, labeling each relation with one of several stance/role types: supports, extends, qualifies, refutes, or background. On top of this resource (ClaimFlow), they define a new NLP task, Claim Relation Classification, where a model must infer the stance toward a cited claim from textual evidence and citation context. They train and evaluate strong neural models and large language models on this task, then apply the best-performing model to roughly 13k NLP papers to automatically trace how claims are reused, challenged, and transformed over decades.

Result: For the Claim Relation Classification task, the best baseline models reach 0.78 macro-F1, demonstrating that the task is learnable but still difficult. Applying the trained model at scale to ~13k papers, they find that 63.5% of claims are never reused in later work, only 11.1% are ever directly challenged, and that claims that do propagate are more often modified via qualification and extension than simply confirmed or refuted.

Conclusion: ClaimFlow provides a claim-centric, relation-labeled view of the NLP literature that exposes the dynamics of how scientific claims spread and change over time. The findings suggest that while many claims remain isolated, influential ones tend to be reshaped rather than straightforwardly supported or rejected. The resource and task together create a basis for future work on automatic understanding of scientific argumentation and the lifecycle of ideas in NLP research.

Abstract: Scientific papers do more than report results $-$ they advance $\textit{claims}$ that later work supports, extends, or sometimes refutes. Yet existing methods for citation and claim analysis capture only fragments of this dialogue. In this work, we make these interactions explicit at the level of individual scientific claims. We introduce $\texttt{ClaimFlow}$, a claim-centric view of the NLP literature, built from $304$ ACL Anthology papers (1979$-$2025) that are manually annotated with $1{,}084$ claims and $832$ cross-paper claim relations, indicating whether a citing paper $\textit{supports}$, $\textit{extends}$, $\textit{qualifies}$, $\textit{refutes}$, or references a claim as $\textit{background}$. Using $\texttt{ClaimFlow}$, we define a new task $-$ $\textit{Claim Relation Classification}$ $-$ which requires models to infer the scientific stance toward a cited claim from the text and citation context. Evaluating strong neural models and large language models on this task, we report baseline performance of $0.78$ macro-F1, highlighting that claim-relation classification is feasible but challenging. We further apply our model to $\sim$$13k$ NLP papers to analyze how claims evolve across decades of NLP research. Our analysis reveals that $63.5$% claims are never reused; only $11.1$% are ever challenged; meanwhile, widely propagated claims are more often $\textit{reshaped}$ through qualification and extension than directly confirmed or refuted. Overall, $\texttt{ClaimFlow}$ offers a lens for examining how ideas shift and mature within NLP, and a foundation for assessing whether models can interpret scientific argumentation.

### [19] [CounterRefine: Answer-Conditioned Counterevidence Retrieval for Inference-Time Knowledge Repair in Factual Question Answering](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16091)
*Tianyi Huang,Ying Kai Deng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes CounterRefine, an inference-time repair layer that uses retrieval not just to find evidence but to test, validate, and selectively revise provisional QA answers, substantially improving factual QA accuracy over strong RAG and one-shot baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In factual QA, models often have access to the right evidence but still answer incorrectly because they fail to properly commit to or revise their initial answer. Existing retrieval-augmented generation systems mainly use retrieval to expand context, not to critically reassess a drafted answer. The authors aim to address these "failures of commitment" by turning retrieval into a mechanism for checking and repairing provisional answers rather than just informing an initial guess.

Method: CounterRefine is an inference-time, lightweight layer placed on top of a retrieval-grounded QA system. (1) The base system first generates a short draft answer from initial retrieved evidence. (2) Conditioned on this draft answer, the system issues follow-up queries to collect both supporting and conflicting evidence. (3) A restricted refinement step then decides between KEEP and REVISE; any proposed revision must pass deterministic validation rules before being accepted. This design constrains how revisions are made and uses retrieval explicitly to stress-test and potentially repair the draft answer.

Result: On the SimpleQA benchmark, CounterRefine improves over a matched GPT-5 Baseline-RAG system by 5.8 accuracy points, achieving 73.1% correct answers. It also outperforms a reported one-shot GPT-5.4 baseline by roughly 40 points, indicating strong gains from the proposed repair process even compared to more capable base models used in a naive way.

Conclusion: The study shows that substantial QA gains can be achieved not just by better retrieval or larger models, but by explicitly using retrieved evidence to challenge and, when warranted, revise provisional answers. CounterRefine demonstrates that a simple, inference-time repair layer can significantly reduce errors due to "failures of commitment," suggesting that future knowledgeable foundation models should focus on mechanisms for self-checking and repair in addition to evidence access.

Abstract: In factual question answering, many errors are not failures of access but failures of commitment: the system retrieves relevant evidence, yet still settles on the wrong answer. We present CounterRefine, a lightweight inference-time repair layer for retrieval-grounded question answering. CounterRefine first produces a short answer from retrieved evidence, then gathers additional support and conflicting evidence with follow-up queries conditioned on that draft answer, and finally applies a restricted refinement step that outputs either KEEP or REVISE, with proposed revisions accepted only if they pass deterministic validation. In effect, CounterRefine turns retrieval into a mechanism for testing a provisional answer rather than merely collecting more context. On the full SimpleQA benchmark, CounterRefine improves a matched GPT-5 Baseline-RAG by 5.8 points and reaches a 73.1 percent correct rate, while exceeding the reported one-shot GPT-5.4 score by roughly 40 points. These findings suggest a simple but important direction for knowledgeable foundation models: beyond accessing evidence, they should also be able to use that evidence to reconsider and, when necessary, repair their own answers.

</details>


### [20] [Frequency Matters: Fast Model-Agnostic Data Curation for Pruning and Quantization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16105)
*Francesco Pio Monaco,Elia Cunegatti,Flavio Vella,Giovanni Iacca*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes ZipCal, a fast, model-agnostic method to select calibration data for post-training compression of large language models by maximizing lexical diversity using Zipfian power laws.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Post-training compression techniques like pruning and quantization need calibration data to preserve model performance, but current practices often rely on random sampling or expensive model-dependent signals (e.g., perplexity) and under-explore how to systematically choose good calibration sets. There is a need for an efficient, scalable, and model-agnostic way to curate calibration data that preserves capabilities across tasks while remaining computationally tractable for very large models and datasets.

Method: The authors introduce ZipCal, a data curation strategy that selects calibration examples based on intrinsic lexical statistics instead of model outputs. It exploits Zipfian power laws in language by maximizing lexical diversity during sampling. The method has linear complexity in data size, making it substantially faster than approaches that require running the model (e.g., to compute perplexity). They evaluate ZipCal as the calibration selector for both pruning and quantization settings.

Result: Across multiple pruning benchmarks, ZipCal consistently outperforms standard uniform random sampling for calibration set selection. In downstream task performance, it matches a state-of-the-art model-dependent method that selects data based on model perplexity, while avoiding its heavy computational costs. Empirically, ZipCal is about 240× faster on average due to its linear-time complexity and lack of model calls.

Conclusion: Calibration data selection has a significant impact on the quality of compressed LLMs. ZipCal provides an effective, model-agnostic, and computationally efficient way to construct calibration sets by exploiting lexical diversity and Zipfian statistics. It offers comparable downstream performance to strong model-based methods at a fraction of the computational cost, making it especially suitable for large-scale models and datasets.

Abstract: Post-training model compression is essential for enhancing the portability of Large Language Models (LLMs) while preserving their performance. While several compression approaches have been proposed, less emphasis has been placed on selecting the most suitable set of data (the so-called \emph{calibration data}) for finding the compressed model configuration. The choice of calibration data is a critical step in preserving model capabilities both intra- and inter-tasks. In this work, we address the challenge of identifying high-performance calibration sets for both pruning and quantization by analyzing intrinsic data properties rather than model-specific signals. We introduce \texttt{\textbf{ZipCal}}, a model-agnostic data curation strategy that maximizes lexical diversity based on Zipfian power laws. Experiments demonstrate that our method consistently outperforms standard uniform random sampling across various pruning benchmarks. Notably, it also performs on par, in terms of downstream performance, with a state-of-the-art method that relies on model perplexity. The latter becomes prohibitively expensive at large-scale models and datasets, while \texttt{\textbf{ZipCal}} is on average $\sim$240$\times$ faster due to its tractable linear complexity\footnote{We make the code and the experiments available at https://anonymous.4open.science/r/zipcal-71CD/.}.

</details>


### [21] [ASDA: Automated Skill Distillation and Adaptation for Financial Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16112)
*Tik Yu Yim,Wenting Tan,Sum Yee Chan,Tak-Wah Lam,Siu Ming Yiu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes ASDA, a training-free framework that adapts LLMs to financial reasoning by auto-generating and injecting structured skill artifacts, delivering large gains on the FAMMA benchmark without changing model weights.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing ways to adapt LLMs to specialized financial reasoning mostly rely on costly fine-tuning, which locks expertise into specific model weights and is often impractical when weight access is restricted. Recent training-free methods such as GEPA and ACE attempt to optimize prompts or external text, but experiments on the FAMMA benchmark reveal they provide only marginal improvements, especially for complex, multi-step financial reasoning. This exposes a gap: how to achieve strong domain adaptation and robust reasoning without retraining or weight access, and in a way that is auditable and reusable.

Method: The authors introduce Automated Skill Distillation and Adaptation (ASDA), a framework that builds structured, reusable skill artifacts from model errors. A stronger teacher model reviews the failures of a student model on financial reasoning tasks. It then clusters these errors by financial subfield and error type, and automatically synthesizes "skill files" that include reasoning procedures, code templates, and worked examples tailored to each cluster. These skill artifacts are not used to change model weights; instead, they are dynamically injected at inference time when relevant, serving as explicit guidance for the student model. Skill files are organized, human-readable, version-controlled, and aligned with the Agent Skills open standard to ensure interoperability.

Result: On the FAMMA financial reasoning benchmark, ASDA yields substantial improvements over existing training-free baselines. Specifically, it improves arithmetic reasoning performance by up to 17.33 percentage points and non-arithmetic reasoning by up to 5.95 percentage points compared to methods like GEPA and ACE. These results indicate that structured, skill-based intervention outperforms unstructured prompt or text optimization for complex domain reasoning under a no-weight-update constraint.

Conclusion: ASDA demonstrates that large, structured skill artifacts distilled from error analysis can significantly enhance domain-specific financial reasoning of LLMs without any fine-tuning or access to model weights. This training-free, auditable, and standard-compatible approach offers organizations a practical path to adapt general-purpose LLMs to specialized financial tasks using only labeled domain data. The framework challenges the reliance on parameter-level adaptation and suggests that externalized, version-controlled skills can be a powerful and transparent mechanism for domain adaptation.

Abstract: Adapting large language models (LLMs) to specialized financial reasoning typically requires expensive fine-tuning that produces model-locked expertise. Training-free alternatives have emerged, yet our experiments show that leading methods (GEPA and ACE) achieve only marginal gains on the FAMMA financial reasoning benchmark, exposing the limits of unstructured text optimization for complex, multi-step domain reasoning. We introduce Automated Skill Distillation and Adaptation (ASDA), a framework that automatically generates structured skill artifacts through iterative error-corrective learning without modifying model weights. A teacher model analyzes a student model's failures on financial reasoning tasks, clusters errors by subfield and error type, and synthesizes skill files containing reasoning procedures, code templates, and worked examples, which are dynamically injected during inference. Evaluated on FAMMA, ASDA achieves up to +17.33% improvement on arithmetic reasoning and +5.95% on non-arithmetic reasoning, substantially outperforming all training-free baselines. The resulting skill artifacts are human-readable, version-controlled, and compatible with the Agent Skills open standard, offering any organization with a labeled domain dataset a practical and auditable path to domain adaptation without weight access or retraining.

### [22] [Language Models Don't Know What You Want: Evaluating Personalization in Deep Research Needs Real Users](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16120)
*Nishant Balepur,Malachi Hamada,Varsha Kishore,Sergey Feldman,Amanpreet Singh,Pao Siangliulue,Joseph Chee Chang,Eunsol Choi,Jordan Lee Boyd-Graber,Aakanksha Naik*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces MyScholarQA, a personalized deep research tool, shows it outperforms baselines on synthetic benchmarks, then uses real-user studies to uncover subtle personalization failures that LLM-based evaluations miss, arguing that genuine progress in personalized research tools requires evaluation with real users.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Deep research tools can summarize and synthesize large scientific literatures in response to queries, but they treat all users similarly and lack a model of the individual researcher’s interests, workflows, and needs. Current evaluations typically rely on synthetic users and LLM judges, which may miss what actual users care about in personalization. The authors aim to build a system that adapts to individual researchers and to examine whether standard NLP evaluation protocols are sufficient for assessing personalization quality.

Method: The authors design MyScholarQA (MySQA), a personalized deep research system that (1) automatically infers a user profile of research interests; (2) proposes personalized actions or workflows for each query (e.g., what kinds of literature to retrieve or how to structure the response); and (3) generates a multi-section report aligned with the user-approved actions. They evaluate MySQA with a standard NLP-style protocol using synthetic users and LLM judges, comparing against baselines on citation quality and adherence to user-specific actions. Then they deploy an online version to real users, conduct interviews, and perform qualitative analysis to identify types of personalization errors that the automated evaluation failed to detect.

Result: On the synthetic benchmark with LLM judges, MySQA outperforms baseline systems on citation-related metrics and on how well the generated outputs follow the personalized actions inferred from user profiles. However, in the real-user study, interviews reveal nine nuanced categories of personalization errors—subtle mismatches between user needs and system behavior—that are not captured by the LLM-based evaluation protocol. This indicates a gap between benchmark performance and real-world user satisfaction in personalized deep research tools.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that while personalized deep research tools like MySQA can be quantitatively shown to outperform baselines under standard NLP evaluation protocols, these automatic setups—especially those relying on synthetic users and LLM judges—are insufficient to capture the full spectrum of personalization quality that real researchers care about. The authors argue that future work on personalized deep research must incorporate real-user evaluation and qualitative analysis, because key dimensions of personalization only emerge through direct user interaction and feedback. They also offer design lessons for building more effective personalized research assistants based on the uncovered error types.

Abstract: Deep Research (DR) tools (e.g. OpenAI DR) help researchers cope with ballooning publishing counts. Such tools can synthesize scientific papers to answer researchers' queries, but lack understanding of their users. We change that in MyScholarQA (MySQA), a personalized DR tool that: 1) infers a profile of a user's research interests; 2) proposes personalized actions for a user's input query; and 3) writes a multi-section report for the query that follows user-approved actions. We first test MySQA with NLP's standard protocol: we design a benchmark of synthetic users and LLM judges, where MySQA beats baselines in citation metrics and personalized action-following. However, we suspect this process does not cover all aspects of personalized DR users value, so we interview users in an online version of MySQA to unmask them. We reveal nine nuanced errors of personalized DR undetectable by our LLM judges, and we study qualitative feedback to form lessons for future DR design. In all, we argue for a pillar of personalization that easy-to-use LLM judges can lead NLP to overlook: real progress in personalization is only possible with real users.

### [23] [Pre-training LLM without Learning Rate Decay Enhances Supervised Fine-Tuning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16127)
*Kazuki Yano,Shun Kiyono,Sosuke Kobayashi,Sho Takase,Jun Suzuki*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that not decaying the learning rate after warmup (WSO schedule) during large-scale pre-training leads to better downstream performance after supervised fine-tuning, despite sometimes worse pre-training loss.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While decay-based learning rate schedules are standard for pre-training large language models and effective at reducing pre-training loss, their impact on downstream performance after supervised fine-tuning is not well understood. Practitioners usually optimize for pre-training metrics, assuming they translate directly into better downstream performance, but this assumption has not been rigorously tested, especially at scale. The authors aim to clarify whether commonly used learning rate decay actually helps or harms downstream adaptability and to provide guidance for how to schedule learning rates when pre-training models intended for broad downstream use.

Method: The authors compare a Warmup-Stable-Only (WSO) learning rate schedule—warmup followed by a constant learning rate with no decay—against common decay-based schedules in large-scale language model pre-training. They train 1B and 8B parameter models under different training regimes, including mid-training and over-training, then evaluate their downstream performance after supervised fine-tuning (SFT). They analyze both pre-training loss and downstream task metrics, and additionally perform loss landscape analysis to study the sharpness or flatness of the minima reached under different learning rate schedules, connecting geometry of the solution to adaptability after SFT.

Result: WSO schedules consistently yield better downstream performance after SFT than decay-based learning rate schedules for both 1B and 8B models, across mid-training and over-training regimes. This occurs even when decay-based schedulers achieve lower pre-training loss or better pre-training metrics. Loss landscape analysis shows that decay-based schedules tend to converge to sharper minima, while WSO leads to flatter minima, which correlate with improved adaptability under SFT. Thus, aggressively decaying the learning rate can improve pre-training metrics at the cost of downstream flexibility.

Conclusion: Learning rate decay, although beneficial for minimizing pre-training loss, can harm downstream performance after supervised fine-tuning by pushing models into sharper, less adaptable minima. A Warmup-Stable-Only schedule that keeps the learning rate constant after warmup preserves flatter minima and improves downstream adaptability. Therefore, when pre-training large language models intended for broad downstream use, practitioners should prioritize WSO-like schedules rather than aggressive decay, and model providers should consider using such schedules to release more adaptable base models.

Abstract: We investigate the role of learning rate scheduling in the large-scale pre-training of large language models, focusing on its influence on downstream performance after supervised fine-tuning (SFT). Decay-based learning rate schedulers are widely used to minimize pre-training loss. However, despite their widespread use, how these schedulers affect performance after SFT remains underexplored. In this paper, we examine Warmup-Stable-Only (WSO), which maintains a constant learning rate after warmup without any decay. Through experiments with 1B and 8B parameter models, we show that WSO consistently outperforms decay-based schedulers in terms of performance after SFT, even though decay-based schedulers may exhibit better performance after pre-training. The result also holds across different regimes with mid-training and over-training. Loss landscape analysis further reveals that decay-based schedulers lead models into sharper minima, whereas WSO preserves flatter minima that support adaptability. These findings indicate that applying LR decay to improve pre-training metrics may compromise downstream adaptability. Our work also provides practical guidance for training and model release strategies, highlighting that pre-training models with WSO enhances their adaptability for downstream tasks.
