### [24] [Social Simulacra in the Wild: AI Agent Communities on Moltbook](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16128)
*Agam Goyal,Olivia Pal,Hari Sundaram,Eshwar Chandrasekharan,Koustuv Saha*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper empirically compares AI-agent and human online communities, showing that AI-agent platforms have more unequal participation, overlapping authorship across communities, emotionally flatter and more assertive language, and more easily identifiable individual agents, leading to distinct collective communication dynamics.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand how communities composed of autonomous LLM-based agents differ from human communities in structure and language, which is important for communication research and platform governance as AI agents increasingly participate in online discourse.

Method: The authors conduct a large-scale empirical analysis of 73,899 posts from Moltbook (an AI-agent platform) and 189,838 Reddit posts across five carefully matched communities. They compare structural properties (participation inequality, cross-community author overlap) and linguistic attributes (emotionality, cognition, sociality). They further decompose community-level patterns into structural vs. author-level effects, focusing on identifiability and stylistic profiles of individual agents vs. humans.

AI-generated content is more emotionally flat, more assertive than exploratory in cognitive style, and more socially detached. Community-level homogenization appears largely due to structural factors like shared authorship across communities. Individual AI agents are more identifiable than human users because of distinctive stylistic outliers amplified by huge posting volumes.

Conclusion: AI-agent communities exhibit structural and linguistic dynamics that are systematically different from human communities. Apparent homogenization of AI-agent discourse mostly stems from structural features such as overlapping authorship, while at the author level, agents are actually highly distinctive and identifiable. These findings provide a foundation for understanding and governing AI-mediated multi-agent communication as it reshapes online discourse.

Abstract: As autonomous LLM-based agents increasingly populate social platforms, understanding the dynamics of AI-agent communities becomes essential for both communication research and platform governance. We present the first large-scale empirical comparison of AI-agent and human online communities, analyzing 73,899 Moltbook and 189,838 Reddit posts across five matched communities. Structurally, we find that Moltbook exhibits extreme participation inequality (Gini = 0.84 vs. 0.47) and high cross-community author overlap (33.8\% vs. 0.5\%). In terms of linguistic attributes, content generated by AI-agents is emotionally flattened, cognitively shifted toward assertion over exploration, and socially detached. These differences give rise to apparent community-level homogenization, but we show this is primarily a structural artifact of shared authorship. At the author level, individual agents are more identifiable than human users, driven by outlier stylistic profiles amplified by their extreme posting volume. As AI-mediated communication reshapes online discourse, our work offers an empirical foundation for understanding how multi-agent interaction gives rise to collective communication dynamics distinct from those of human communities.

Moreover, since the release of ChatGPT in November 2022, researchers have rapidly adopted LLMs for drafting manuscripts themselves, fundamentally transforming scientific writing, yet no resource exists to analyze how this writing has evolved. SciZoom provides three hierarchical summarization targets (Abstract, Contributions, and TL;DR) achieving compression ratios up to 600:1, enabling both multi-granularity summarization research and temporal mining of scientific writing patterns. Our linguistic analysis reveals striking shifts in phrase patterns (up to 10x for formulaic expressions) and rhetorical style (23% decline in hedging), suggesting that LLM-assisted writing produces more confident yet homogenized prose. SciZoom serves as both a challenging benchmark and a unique resource for mining the evolution of scientific discourse in the generative AI era. Our code and dataset are publicly available on GitHub (https://github.com/janghana/SciZoom) and Hugging Face (https://huggingface.co/datasets/hanjang/SciZoom), respectively.

To address these issues, we propose SI--a Synthesize-Inject-Align framework for building knowledgeable and secure e-commerce search LLMs. Our approach first synthesizes high-quality natural language corpus by combining structured knowledge graphs with unstructured behavioral logs, augmented with reasoning chains and safety-aware data.We then introduce a parameter-efficient pre-training strategy based on Depth Up-Scaling to inject domain knowledge while preserving general capabilities. ### [27] [Parametric Social Identity Injection and Diversification in Public Opinion Simulation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16142)
*Hexi Wang,Yujia Zhou,Bangde Du,Qingyao Ai,Yiqun Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper identifies a “diversity collapse” problem in LLM-based public opinion simulations and proposes a method (PSII) that injects explicit social identity representations into hidden layers to better match real survey distributions and preserve diversity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are being used as synthetic agents to simulate public opinion as a cheaper, faster alternative to human surveys, but current methods fail to capture real social diversity. They blur differences between demographic groups and produce overly homogeneous responses, which undermines their reliability for studying public opinion. The authors are motivated to understand why this happens in the model’s internal representations and to design a way to preserve and control social identity information so simulations can reflect real-world heterogeneity.

Method: The authors first diagnose a “Diversity Collapse” in LLM hidden representations, showing that distinct social identities become less distinguishable as information flows through layers. Based on this, they propose Parametric Social Identity Injection (PSII), a model-agnostic framework that encodes demographic attributes and value orientations as explicit, learnable parameters. These identity vectors are injected directly into intermediate hidden states of the LLM rather than only conditioning via prompts. This representation-level intervention allows fine-grained, controllable modulation of agents’ identities. They test PSII on World Values Survey data and apply it to multiple open-source LLMs, comparing simulated responses to real survey distributions.

Result: PSII significantly improves how well LLM-generated opinion distributions match real survey data, as measured by lower KL divergence. It also increases diversity both across and within demographic groups, avoiding the homogeneous patterns seen with standard prompt-based persona conditioning. These improvements are observed consistently across several open-source LLMs on the World Values Survey benchmark, demonstrating that PSII is effective and relatively general.

Conclusion: The study concludes that diversity collapse in LLM hidden representations is a key reason why current opinion-simulation agents fail to reproduce real social heterogeneity. By intervening directly at the representation level with Parametric Social Identity Injection, LLMs can better model nuanced demographic differences and value orientations. This enables more accurate and diversity-aware public opinion simulations and opens up a general direction for using representation-level control to shape LLM agent behavior. The released code and data aim to support further research in this area.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have recently been adopted as synthetic agents for public opinion simulation, offering a promising alternative to costly and slow human surveys. Despite their scalability, current LLM-based simulation methods fail to capture social diversity, producing flattened inter-group differences and overly homogeneous responses within demographic groups. We identify this limitation as a Diversity Collapse phenomenon in LLM hidden representations, where distinct social identities become increasingly indistinguishable across layers. Motivated by this observation, we propose Parametric Social Identity Injection (PSII), a general framework that injects explicit, parametric representations of demographic attributes and value orientations directly into intermediate hidden states of LLMs. Unlike prompt-based persona conditioning, PSII enables fine-grained and controllable identity modulation at the representation level. Extensive experiments on the World Values Survey using multiple open-source LLMs show that PSII significantly improves distributional fidelity and diversity, reducing KL divergence to real-world survey data while enhancing overall diversity. This work provides new insights into representation-level control of LLM agents and advances scalable, diversity-aware public opinion simulation. Code and data are available at https://github.com/halsayxi/PSII.

### [28] [Polyglot-Lion: Efficient Multilingual ASR for Singapore via Balanced Fine-Tuning of Qwen3-ASR](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16184)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need to understand whether smaller, moderate-scale pretrained models can, with the right training strategy, reach similar performance while dramatically reducing training and inference costs.

Method: Starting from two pretrained Qwen3-ASR models (0.6B and 1.7B parameters), the authors fine-tune them exclusively on public speech datasets for English, Mandarin, Tamil, and Malay. They design a balanced sampling strategy so each language contributes the same number of training utterances, and they intentionally avoid using explicit language tags as input, forcing the models to infer the spoken language directly from audio. They then evaluate the models on 12 benchmarks covering all four target languages and compare their performance, training cost, and inference speed against a much larger 10B-parameter baseline model (MERaLiON-2-10B-ASR).

At inference time, Polyglot-Lion processes audio roughly 20 times faster (0.10 s per sample vs. 2.02 s per sample).

Conclusion: With carefully balanced multilingual fine-tuning and removal of explicit language conditioning, moderately sized pretrained ASR models can deliver competitive multilingual performance across Singapore’s four main languages, while being dramatically cheaper to train and much faster at inference than larger specialist systems. This makes them suitable for practical deployment in resource-constrained environments.

Abstract: We present Polyglot-Lion, a family of compact multilingual automatic speech recognition (ASR) models tailored for the linguistic landscape of Singapore, covering English, Mandarin, Tamil, and Malay. Our models are obtained by fine-tuning Qwen3-ASR-0.6B and Qwen3-ASR-1.7B exclusively on publicly available speech corpora, using a balanced sampling strategy that equalizes the number of training utterances per language and deliberately omits language-tag conditioning so that the model learns to identify languages implicitly from audio. On 12 benchmarks spanning the four target languages, Polyglot-Lion-1.7B achieves an average error rate of 14.85, competitive with MERaLiON-2-10B-ASR (14.32) - a model 6x larger - while incurring a training cost of \$81 on a single RTX PRO 6000 GPU compared to \$18,862 for the 128-GPU baseline. Inference throughput is approximately 20x faster than MERaLiON at 0.10 s/sample versus 2.02 s/sample. These results demonstrate that linguistically balanced fine-tuning of moderate-scale pretrained models can yield deployment-ready multilingual ASR at a fraction of the cost of larger specialist systems.

### [29] [Structured Semantic Cloaking for Jailbreak Attacks on Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16192)
*Xiaobing Sun,Perry Lam,Shaohua Li,Zizhou Wang,Rick Siow Mong Goh,Yong Liu,Liangli Zhen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Structured Semantic Cloaking (S2C), a new jailbreak framework that bypasses advanced LLM safety by restructuring how malicious intent is represented and reconstructed in the model, significantly improving attack success over prior methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing jailbreak attacks mostly manipulate surface text and are increasingly ineffective against modern LLMs, whose safety systems operate on latent semantics and generation-time reasoning. The authors want to understand and exploit how models reconstruct intent internally, creating more robust attacks that can evade such deeper safety mechanisms.

Method: They design S2C, a multi-dimensional attack framework with three mechanisms: (1) Contextual Reframing, which embeds malicious requests into realistic, high-stakes or benign-appearing scenarios to push the model toward helpful behavior; (2) Content Fragmentation, which splits and distributes key malicious semantics across multiple disjoint prompt segments so that the harmful intent is not coherently present at once; and (3) Clue-Guided Camouflage, which hides remaining harmful cues behind benign-looking content while inserting markers that let the model reassemble the intended meaning during generation. Together, these mechanisms delay and complicate semantic consolidation in the model’s latent space, aiming to bypass safety triggers that require explicit or coherent harmful intent at decoding time.

Result: Using HarmBench and JBB-Behaviors on several open-source and proprietary LLMs, they report that S2C increases Attack Success Rate by 12.4% and 9.7% over state-of-the-art attacks, respectively. S2C is particularly effective on GPT-5-mini, where it exceeds the strongest prior baseline by 26% on JBB-Behaviors. They also empirically explore how different combinations of the three mechanisms perform against various model families and how varying the degree of obfuscation versus instruction recoverability affects jailbreak performance.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that safety mechanisms based on latent semantic understanding and generation-time reasoning remain vulnerable to attacks that specifically target semantic reconstruction processes rather than surface text. Structured Semantic Cloaking demonstrates that delaying and distributing malicious intent can significantly degrade these defences while keeping instructions recoverable. This highlights a persistent and evolving vulnerability in LLM safety and suggests that future defences must account for multi-step semantic consolidation and long-range co-reference, not just explicit or localized harmful content.

Abstract: Modern LLMs employ safety mechanisms that extend beyond surface-level input filtering to latent semantic representations and generation-time reasoning, enabling them to recover obfuscated malicious intent during inference and refuse accordingly, and rendering many surface-level obfuscation jailbreak attacks ineffective. We propose Structured Semantic Cloaking (S2C), a novel multi-dimensional jailbreak attack framework that manipulates how malicious semantic intent is reconstructed during model inference. S2C strategically distributes and reshapes semantic cues such that full intent consolidation requires multi-step inference and long-range co-reference resolution within deeper latent representations. The framework comprises three complementary mechanisms: (1) Contextual Reframing, which embeds the request within a plausible high-stakes scenario to bias the model toward compliance; (2) Content Fragmentation, which disperses the semantic signature of the request across disjoint prompt segments; and (3) Clue-Guided Camouflage, which disguises residual semantic cues while embedding recoverable markers that guide output generation. By delaying and restructuring semantic consolidation, S2C degrades safety triggers that depend on coherent or explicitly reconstructed malicious intent at decoding time, while preserving sufficient instruction recoverability for functional output generation. We evaluate S2C across multiple open-source and proprietary LLMs using HarmBench and JBB-Behaviors, where it improves Attack Success Rate (ASR) by 12.4% and 9.7%, respectively, over the current SOTA. Notably, S2C achieves substantial gains on GPT-5-mini, outperforming the strongest baseline by 26% on JBB-Behaviors. We also analyse which combinations perform best against broad families of models, and characterise the trade-off between the extent of obfuscation versus input recoverability on jailbreak success.

</details>


### [30] [SpecSteer: Synergizing Local Context and Global Reasoning for Efficient Personalized Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16219)
*Hang Lv,Sheng Liang,Hao Wang,Yongyue Zhang,Hongchao Gu,Wei Guo,Defu Lian,Yong Liu,Enhong Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: SpecSteer is a collaborative inference framework that combines an on-device small language model with a powerful cloud model to achieve personalized yet private and high-quality generation using a Draft–Verify–Recover pipeline.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is a tension between personalization and privacy in AI assistants: sending personal history to large cloud models risks privacy, but relying only on small on-device models leads to poor reasoning and lower generation quality. The authors’ pilot study indicates that local improvements alone cannot close this performance gap, motivating a new collaborative approach that preserves privacy while leveraging cloud-scale reasoning.

Method: The paper introduces SpecSteer, an asymmetric collaborative inference framework formalized as Bayesian knowledge fusion between a private on-device model and a cloud model. It repurposes speculative decoding as a distributed alignment protocol to coordinate both models without exposing raw user data. The process follows a Draft–Verify–Recover pipeline: (1) Draft: the on-device model generates personalized candidate sequences using private context; (2) Verify: the cloud model checks these drafts through a ratio-based validation mechanism that evaluates reasoning quality while remaining decoupled from the hidden private context; (3) Recover: when the cloud rejects drafts, a steering recovery phase adjusts and corrects them by re-injecting local user intent while still protecting privacy.

Result: Experimental evaluations show that SpecSteer closes the reasoning performance gap between on-device and cloud-only generation, yielding higher-quality personalized outputs compared to standard baselines. It also improves efficiency, achieving a 2.36× speedup over conventional approaches such as direct cloud inference without speculative collaboration.

Conclusion: SpecSteer demonstrates that an asymmetric collaborative inference design can achieve strong personalization, privacy preservation, and high reasoning quality simultaneously. By fusing on-device private context with cloud-scale reasoning through speculative decoding and a Draft–Verify–Recover protocol, the framework offers both accuracy and latency benefits, suggesting a practical path toward private, personalized intelligent assistants on resource-constrained devices.

Abstract: Realizing personalized intelligence faces a core dilemma: sending user history to centralized large language models raises privacy concerns, while on-device small language models lack the reasoning capacity required for high-quality generation. Our pilot study shows that purely local enhancements remain insufficient to reliably bridge this gap. We therefore propose SpecSteer, an asymmetric collaborative inference framework that synergizes private on-device context with cloud-scale reasoning. SpecSteer casts collaboration as Bayesian knowledge fusion and repurposes speculative decoding as a distributed alignment protocol, yielding a Draft--Verify--Recover pipeline: the on-device model drafts personalized sequences; the cloud validates via a ratio-based mechanism that decouples reasoning verification from private context, filtering logical flaws without accessing raw user context; upon rejection, a steering recovery injects local intent during correction. Experiments demonstrate that SpecSteer successfully closes the reasoning gap and achieves superior personalized generation performance, while delivering a 2.36x speedup over standard baselines.

</details>


### [31] [More Rounds, More Noise: Why Multi-Turn Review Fails to Improve Cross-Context Verification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16244)
*Song Tae-Eun*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates whether allowing multi-turn interaction in cross-context review improves LLM verification, and finds that it actually worsens overall error-detection performance compared to single-pass review.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Cross-Context Review (CCR) has been shown to improve verification by separating generation and review, but it is unclear whether adding interactive, multi-turn review—where reviewers can ask questions and get answers—would further enhance error detection. Practitioners might intuitively expect that more dialogue and more review passes should help, so the authors want to rigorously test this assumption.

Method: The authors design a controlled experiment using 30 artifacts with 150 injected errors and compare a baseline single-pass CCR condition to four Dynamic Cross-Context Review (D-CCR) variants that introduce multi-turn elements such as follow-up questions, answers, and additional review rounds. They compute F1, precision, and recall, and analyze error patterns and reviewer behavior to understand performance changes and underlying mechanisms.

Result: Single-pass CCR achieves the best performance (F1 = 0.376) and significantly outperforms all tested D-CCR variants. The D-CCR-2b condition with question-and-answer exchanges reaches only F1 = 0.303, with a statistically significant drop relative to the baseline (p < 0.001, effect size d = -0.59). Multi-turn setups increase recall by about 0.08 but cause a 62% rise in false positives (from 5.2 to 8.5 on average), reducing precision from 0.30 to 0.20. A variant with independent re-review without prior context (D-CCR-2c) performs worst (F1 = 0.263). Within the multi-turn conditions, more information (Q&A) improves performance somewhat, but still does not reach the single-pass baseline.

Conclusion: Multi-turn dynamic cross-context review does not improve LLM verification over single-pass CCR and, in fact, degrades performance due to increased false positives. This degradation comes from behavioral effects such as false positive pressure—where reviewers feel compelled to find new issues in later rounds—and Review Target Drift—where reviewers begin critiquing the dialogue rather than the original artifact. Simply repeating reviews or adding extra rounds invites noise rather than better verification, although within multi-turn setups, providing richer context can mitigate but not eliminate the problem. The key implication is that more review rounds are not inherently better; system designers should favor well-structured single-pass reviews or carefully constrained interactions rather than unconstrained multi-turn reviewing.

Abstract: Cross-Context Review (CCR) improves LLM verification by separating production and review into independent sessions. A natural extension is multi-turn review: letting the reviewer ask follow-up questions, receive author responses, and review again. We call this Dynamic Cross-Context Review (D-CCR). In a controlled experiment with 30 artifacts and 150 injected errors, we tested four D-CCR variants against the single-pass CCR baseline. Single-pass CCR (F1 = 0.376) significantly outperformed all multi-turn variants, including D-CCR-2b with question-and-answer exchange (F1 = 0.303, $p < 0.001$, $d = -0.59$). Multi-turn review increased recall (+0.08) but generated 62% more false positives (8.5 vs. 5.2), collapsing precision from 0.30 to 0.20. Two mechanisms drive this degradation: (1) false positive pressure -- reviewers in later rounds fabricate findings when the artifact's real errors have been exhausted, and (2) Review Target Drift -- reviewers provided with prior Q&A exchanges shift from reviewing the artifact to critiquing the conversation itself. Independent re-review without prior context (D-CCR-2c) performed worst (F1 = 0.263), confirming that mere repetition degrades rather than helps. The degradation stems from false positive pressure in additional rounds, not from information amount -- within multi-turn conditions, more information actually helps (D-CCR-2b > D-CCR-2a). The problem is not what the reviewer sees, but that reviewing again invites noise.

</details>

*Martina Simonotti,Ludovica Pannitto,Eleonora Zucchini,Silvia Ballarè,Caterina Mauri*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates how using Automatic Speech Recognition (ASR) affects the speed and accuracy of creating transcriptions for the KIParla corpus of spoken Italian.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Building large, high-quality spoken corpora is time-consuming and expensive when relying only on manual transcription. The authors want to see whether integrating ASR into the workflow can speed up corpus creation without sacrificing accuracy, and to develop systematic metrics and methods to monitor transcription quality across different annotators and workflows.

Method: They conducted a two-phase experiment where 11 expert and novice transcribers created both fully manual and ASR-assisted transcriptions of the same audio segments, covering three conversation types. They then evaluated the outputs using a mix of statistical modeling, word-level alignment between transcriptions and reference, and several annotation-based metrics to assess speed, accuracy, and patterns of transcription behavior across conditions.

Result: ASR assistance generally increased transcription speed, but it did not reliably improve overall transcription accuracy. The impact of ASR varied depending on workflow setup, type of conversation, and the experience level of the transcriber. The combined use of alignment-based metrics, descriptive statistics, and statistical models successfully captured the differences in performance across annotators and workflows.

Conclusion: ASR-assisted transcription can be incorporated into the KIParla workflow to accelerate corpus building, provided its limitations are recognized. While ASR does not consistently enhance accuracy, with proper configuration and possibly task-specific ASR fine-tuning, it can speed up work without degrading transcription quality. The paper also contributes a systematic evaluation framework for monitoring transcription quality across different annotators and workflow configurations.

Abstract: This paper analyses the implementation of Automatic Speech Recognition (ASR) into the transcription workflow of the KIParla corpus, a resource of spoken Italian. Through a two-phase experiment, 11 expert and novice transcribers produced both manual and ASR-assisted transcriptions of identical audio segments across three different types of conversation, which were subsequently analyzed through a combination of statistical modeling, word-level alignment and a series of annotation-based metrics. Results show that ASR-assisted workflows can increase transcription speed but do not consistently improve overall accuracy, with effects depending on multiple factors such as workflow configuration, conversation type and annotator experience. Analyses combining alignment-based metrics, descriptive statistics and statistical modeling provide a systematic framework to monitor transcription behavior across annotators and workflows. Despite limitations, ASR-assisted transcription, potentially supported by task-specific fine-tuning, could be integrated into the KIParla transcription workflow to accelerate corpus creation without compromising transcription quality.

### [33] [Attention-guided Evidence Grounding for Spoken Question Answering](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16292)
*Ke Yang,Bolin Chen,Yuejie Li,Yueying Hua,Jianhao Nie,Yueping He,Bowen Li,Chengjun Mao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose an end-to-end spoken question answering framework that uses attention in Speech LLMs to explicitly ground evidence segments, fine-tunes attention to focus on relevant audio, and achieves better accuracy, fewer hallucinations, and much lower latency than strong ASR-based cascaded baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Spoken QA requires mapping spoken queries to textual knowledge. Traditional cascaded systems rely on ASR first, which introduces recognition errors and latency, and error propagation can hurt answer quality. End-to-end SpeechLLMs exist but often hallucinate and lack explicit mechanisms to identify which parts of the input audio are evidence for the answer. The authors aim to create a more accurate and efficient system that can explicitly ground and focus on key evidence in speech for QA without heavy cascaded pipelines.

Method: They introduce Attention-guided Evidence Grounding (AEG), an end-to-end framework built on Speech Large Language Models. AEG taps into the model’s internal cross-modal attention maps to locate where in the acoustic representation the evidence for answering the question lies, effectively grounding evidence in the latent space. Because pre-trained SpeechLLMs tend to have diffuse, unfocused attention, they propose Learning to Focus on Evidence (LFE), a supervised fine-tuning strategy that uses labels (or signals) indicating which segments are relevant to the query. LFE trains the attention mechanism to assign higher weights to query-relevant segments and lower weights to irrelevant context, sharpening the evidence localization. The whole system is evaluated on standard QA benchmarks adapted to spoken input.

Result: On spoken versions of SQuAD, HotpotQA, and MuSiQue, AEG with LFE reduces hallucinations and improves efficiency. It outperforms a strong cascaded baseline that uses Whisper-Large-v3 plus a reranker, while cutting inference latency by about 62%, indicating both better answer quality and much faster response, despite using a more compact end-to-end approach.

Conclusion: Explicitly grounding evidence via internal attention in SpeechLLMs and refining that attention through supervised focus training leads to more accurate, less hallucination-prone, and significantly faster spoken QA systems than traditional ASR-based cascades. Attention-guided evidence grounding is a promising direction for cross-modal tasks where speech must be aligned with textual knowledge in an end-to-end manner.

Abstract: Spoken Question Answering (Spoken QA) presents a challenging cross-modal problem: effectively aligning acoustic queries with textual knowledge while avoiding the latency and error propagation inherent in cascaded ASR-based systems. In this paper, we introduce Attention-guided Evidence Grounding (AEG), a novel end-to-end framework that leverages the internal cross-modal attention of Speech Large Language Models (SpeechLLMs) to explicitly locate and ground key evidence in the model's latent space. To address the diffuse attention distribution in pre-trained models, we propose Learning to Focus on Evidence (LFE), a supervised fine-tuning paradigm that calibrates the model's attention mechanism to distinguish query-relevant segments from irrelevant context. Experiments on SQuAD, HotpotQA, and MuSiQue demonstrate that AEG reduces hallucinations and achieves strong efficiency gains, outperforming large-scale cascaded baselines (Whisper-Large-v3 + Reranker) while reducing inference latency by approximately 62%.

Conclusion: PyPhonPlan offers a flexible, open-source, and modular platform for constructing and sharing dynamical models of phonetic planning grounded in neural field theory and task dynamics. Abstract: We introduce PyPhonPlan, a Python toolkit for implementing dynamical models of phonetic planning using coupled dynamic neural fields and task dynamic simulations. The toolkit provides modular components for defining planning, perception and memory fields, as well as between-field coupling, gestural inputs, and using field activation profiles to solve tract variable trajectories. We illustrate the toolkit's capabilities through an example application:~simulating production/perception loops with a coupled memory field, which demonstrates the framework's ability to model interactive speech dynamics using representations that are temporally-principled, neurally-grounded, and phonetically-rich. PyPhonPlan is released as open-source software and contains executable examples to promote reproducibility, extensibility, and cumulative computational development for speech communication research.

However, compared to the world's 7,000 languages, current systems still offer only limited coverage: about 200 languages on the target side, and maybe a few hundreds more on the source side, supported due to cross-lingual transfer. And even these numbers have been hard to evaluate due to the lack of reliable benchmarks and metrics.
  We present Omnilingual Machine Translation (OMT), the first MT system supporting more than 1,600 languages. This scale is enabled by a comprehensive data strategy that integrates large public multilingual corpora with newly created datasets, including manually curated MeDLEY bitext.

Notably, all our 1B to 8B parameter models match or exceed the MT performance of a 70B LLM baseline, revealing a clear specialization advantage and enabling strong translation quality in low-compute settings. Moreover, our evaluation of English-to-1,600 translations further shows that while baseline models can interpret undersupported languages, they frequently fail to generate them with meaningful fidelity; OMT-LLaMA models substantially expand the set of languages for which coherent generation is feasible. Additionally, OMT models improve in cross-lingual transfer, being close to solving the "understanding" part of the puzzle in MT for the 1,600 evaluated. ### [36] [PashtoCorp: A 1.25-Billion-Word Corpus, Evaluation Suite, and Reproducible Pipeline for Low-Resource Language Development](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16354)
*Hanif Rahman*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces PashtoCorp, a very large (1.25B-word) Pashto text corpus and shows it substantially improves Pashto NLP, especially under low-data settings.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Pashto, spoken by ~60 million people, is severely underrepresented in NLP resources and benchmarks. Existing corpora are small (OSCAR subset and a previous dedicated corpus), which limits language model pretraining quality, downstream performance (e.g., NER, reading comprehension), and research on Pashto. The authors aim to fill this resource gap by building a large, high-quality, reproducible corpus and by quantifying its impact on standard NLP tasks.

Method: They construct PashtoCorp by aggregating 39 data sources: seven existing HuggingFace datasets and 32 custom web-scraped sources. The data is passed through a reproducible preprocessing pipeline including Arabic-script tokenization, SHA-256-based deduplication, and quality filtering. They then continue masked language model (MLM) pretraining of XLM-R-base exclusively on PashtoCorp. For evaluation, they measure language modeling perplexity, WikiANN Pashto NER performance in both full-data and low-resource regimes, and Belebele Pashto reading comprehension with Gemma-3n. They also run leave-one-out ablations over data sources to study which sources matter most for downstream NER.

Result: PashtoCorp contains 1.25B words in 2.81M documents, making it 40× larger than the OSCAR Pashto subset and 83× larger than the largest prior Pashto corpus. Continued MLM pretraining on PashtoCorp reduces held-out perplexity by 25.1% (from 8.08 to 6.06). On WikiANN Pashto NER, the pretrained model increases entity F1 from 19.0% to 21.0% (10% relative improvement) and dramatically reduces training variance (~7×). The biggest relative improvement (+27%) occurs when training with only 50 labeled sentences, and PashtoCorp covers 97.9% of WikiANN entity vocabulary. On Belebele Pashto reading comprehension, Gemma-3n achieves 64.6% accuracy, providing the first published LLM baseline for Pashto on this benchmark. Source ablations show that Wikipedia, despite being only 0.7% of the documents, is crucial for NER; removing it alone causes a 47% drop in entity F1.

Conclusion: PashtoCorp is a large, high-quality, and reproducible Pashto corpus that significantly improves language modeling and downstream task performance, particularly NER in low-data settings. The strong coverage of entity vocabulary and the critical role of even small but high-quality sources like Wikipedia underline the importance of targeted data curation. By releasing the corpus, pretrained model, and code, the work substantially advances Pashto NLP and establishes new baselines, enabling further research on low-resource languages more broadly.

Abstract: We present PashtoCorp, a 1.25-billion-word corpus for Pashto, a language spoken by 60 million people that remains severely underrepresented in NLP. The corpus is assembled from 39 sources spanning seven HuggingFace datasets and 32 purpose-built web scrapers, processed through a reproducible pipeline with Arabic-script tokenization, SHA-256 deduplication, and quality filtering. At 1.25B words across 2.81 million documents, PashtoCorp is 40x larger than the OSCAR Pashto subset and 83x larger than the previously largest dedicated Pashto corpus. Continued MLM pretraining of XLM-R-base on PashtoCorp reduces held-out perplexity by 25.1% (8.08->6.06). On WikiANN Pashto NER, the pretrained model improves entity F1 by 10% relative (19.0%->21.0%) and reduces training variance nearly 7x; the largest gain appears at 50 training sentences (+27%), with PashtoCorp covering 97.9% of WikiANN entity vocabulary. On Belebele Pashto reading comprehension, Gemma-3n achieves 64.6% accuracy, the first published LLM baseline for Pashto on this benchmark. A leave-one-out source ablation shows that Wikipedia (0.7% of documents) is the most critical source for NER: removing it alone reduces entity F1 by 47%. Corpus data, trained model, and code are available at https://huggingface.co/datasets/ihanif/pashto-corpus, https://huggingface.co/ihanif/xlmr-pashto, and https://github.com/ihanif/pashto-corpus.

Method: The authors perform a quantitative error analysis comparing different types of benchmark data for Icelandic: human-authored or carefully human-translated items versus synthetic or machine-translated items that were not verified. These flawed items are likely to skew LLM performance scores and reduce the validity of the benchmarks.

Conclusion: Existing LLM benchmarks for Icelandic that rely on unverified synthetic or machine-translated data are not trustworthy indicators of model performance. Abstract: This paper evaluates current Large Language Model (LLM) benchmarking for Icelandic, identifies problems, and calls for improved evaluation methods in low/medium-resource languages in particular. We show that benchmarks that include synthetic or machine-translated data that have not been verified in any way, commonly contain severely flawed test examples that are likely to skew the results and undermine the tests' validity. We warn against the use of such methods without verification in low/medium-resource settings as the translation quality can, at best, only be as good as MT quality for a given language at any given time. Indeed, the results of our quantitative error analysis on existing benchmarks for Icelandic show clear differences between human-authored/-translated benchmarks vs. synthetic or machine-translated benchmarks.

Motivation: Large LLMs are fluent but need expensive preference alignment to perform well on specialized creative tasks like plot generation. This alignment is computationally costly at frontier scale, limiting accessibility. The authors want a resource-efficient way to get high-quality, premise-conditioned plots without relying on very large models.

Method: PlotTwist decomposes plot generation into three components: (1) an Aspect Rating Reward Model, trained with a new Positive-Negative prompting strategy, which scores narratives along five Narrative Quality Dimensions (NQDs); (2) a Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) plot generator aligned with Direct Preference Optimization using high-confidence preference pairs; and (3) an Agentic Evaluation module that simulates human critical review for unbiased post-hoc assessment of generated plots.

Result: Experiments show that small models equipped with PlotTwist outperform much larger frontier models across several narrative quality dimensions, even under tight parameter budgets. The system can also reliably distinguish between plots based on critically acclaimed versus poorly received screenplays, indicating strong sensitivity to narrative quality.

Conclusion: Structured, preference-based alignment—implemented via reward modeling, MoE generation, and agentic evaluation—can enable small language models to match or exceed the creative plot generation quality of much larger systems, offering a more accessible and computationally efficient path to high-quality narrative generation.

Abstract: Creative plot generation presents a fundamental challenge for language models: transforming a concise premise into a coherent narrative that sustains global structure, character development, and emotional resonance. Although recent Large Language Models (LLMs) demonstrate strong fluency across general-purpose tasks, they typically require preference alignment to perform well on specialized domains such as creative plot generation. However, conducting such alignment at the scale of frontier LLMs is computationally prohibitive, significantly limiting accessibility and practical deployment. To address this, we present PlotTwist, a structured framework that enables Small Language Models (SLMs) with $\leq$ 5B active parameters to generate high-quality, premise-conditioned plots competitive with frontier systems up to $200\times$ larger. Our approach decomposes generation into three specialized components: (1) an Aspect Rating Reward Model trained via a novel Positive-Negative prompting strategy to deliver structured narratives across five Narrative Quality Dimensions (NQDs); (2) a Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) plot generator aligned via Direct Preference Optimization on high-confidence preference pairs; and (3) an Agentic Evaluation module that emulates human critical judgment for unbiased post-hoc assessment. Extensive experiments demonstrate that PlotTwist consistently outperforms frontier models across multiple NQDs despite substantially tighter capacity constraints. Further validation confirms strong sensitivity to narrative quality, as the framework reliably distinguishes plots derived from critically acclaimed versus widely panned screenplays. Together, these results establish structured, preference-based alignment as a resource-efficient approach to high-quality creative plot generation.

### [40] [RECOVER: Robust Entity Correction via agentic Orchestration of hypothesis Variants for Evidence-based Recovery](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16411)

Among the strategies, LLM-Select provides the best overall performance on entity correction while preserving the overall word error rate (WER).

Conclusion: RECOVER effectively improves recognition of rare and domain-specific entities in ASR outputs by combining multiple hypotheses, entity retrieval, and LLM-guided correction. The framework substantially reduces entity-specific errors and increases recall without degrading overall transcription quality, with the LLM-Select strategy emerging as the most effective configuration.

Abstract: Entity recognition in Automatic Speech Recognition (ASR) is challenging for rare and domain-specific terms. In domains such as finance, medicine, and air traffic control, these errors are costly. If the entities are entirely absent from the ASR output, post-ASR correction becomes difficult. To address this, we introduce RECOVER, an agentic correction framework that serves as a tool-using agent. It leverages multiple hypotheses as evidence from ASR, retrieves relevant entities, and applies Large Language Model (LLM) correction under constraints. The hypotheses are used using different strategies, namely, 1-Best, Entity-Aware Select, Recognizer Output Voting Error Reduction (ROVER) Ensemble, and LLM-Select. Evaluated across five diverse datasets, it achieves 8-46% relative reductions in entity-phrase word error rate (E-WER) and increases recall by up to 22 percentage points. The LLM-Select achieves the best overall performance in entity correction while maintaining overall WER.

### [41] [IndexRAG: Bridging Facts for Cross-Document Reasoning at Index Time](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16415)
*Zhenghua Bao,Yi Shi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: IndexRAG is a retrieval-augmented generation method for multi-hop QA that pre-computes cross-document reasoning units (bridging facts) during indexing instead of at inference time, improving accuracy and efficiency without extra training.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RAG systems struggle with multi-hop QA because they either use graph-based methods that need expensive online graph construction and traversal, or iterative multi-step reasoning that requires multiple retrieval/LLM calls. The authors want a system that can perform cross-document reasoning efficiently in a single pass, without extra training, while matching or surpassing graph-based methods.

Method: IndexRAG analyzes the document collection offline to detect bridge entities that appear in multiple documents. It then generates explicit bridging facts that connect these documents and stores them as independent retrievable units in the index. At inference time, the system uses only flat, single-pass retrieval to fetch both original passages and relevant bridging facts, and answers the question with a single LLM call. No additional training or fine-tuning is required. The approach can be combined with IRCoT for further gains.

Result: On three multi-hop QA benchmarks (HotpotQA, 2WikiMultiHopQA, MuSiQue), IndexRAG improves F1 over a naive RAG baseline by an average of 4.6 points, while keeping inference to one retrieval step and one LLM call. When combined with IRCoT, IndexRAG outperforms competitive graph-based approaches like HippoRAG and FastGraphRAG on average, despite using only flat retrieval.

Conclusion: Offline construction of bridging facts via entity-based analysis enables efficient and effective multi-hop reasoning in RAG systems. IndexRAG demonstrates that shifting cross-document reasoning from inference time to indexing time can improve accuracy, reduce inference complexity, and even surpass sophisticated graph-based baselines without additional model training.

Abstract: Multi-hop question answering (QA) requires reasoning across multiple documents, yet existing retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) approaches address this either through graph-based methods requiring additional online processing or iterative multi-step reasoning. We present IndexRAG, a novel approach that shifts cross-document reasoning from online inference to offline indexing. IndexRAG identifies bridge entities shared across documents and generates bridging facts as independently retrievable units, requiring no additional training or fine-tuning. Experiments on three widely-used multi-hop QA benchmarks (HotpotQA, 2WikiMultiHopQA, MuSiQue) show that IndexRAG improves F1 over Naive RAG by 4.6 points on average, while requiring only single-pass retrieval and a single LLM call at inference time. When combined with IRCoT, IndexRAG outperforms all graph-based baselines on average, including HippoRAG and FastGraphRAG, while relying solely on flat retrieval. Our code will be released upon acceptance.

### [42] [VQKV: High-Fidelity and High-Ratio Cache Compression via Vector-Quantization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16435)
*Yixuan Wang,Qingyu Shi,Jiayu Zhou,Dianbo Liu,Ziwei He,Zhouhan Lin*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces VQKV, a training-free vector-quantization-based method to heavily compress LLM KV caches while maintaining high performance and extending generation length under fixed memory.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs with long context require large KV caches, which consume significant memory and hinder deployment on resource-constrained devices. Existing training-free KV compression methods (low-rank approximation, scalar quantization) cannot achieve both high compression ratios and high reconstruction fidelity, creating a need for a better compression scheme.

Method: They propose VQKV, a training-free vector quantization approach applied to the KV cache. Instead of storing full-precision KV tensors, VQKV encodes them into compact codebook indices, effectively representing thousands of floating-point values with a few integer indices. This enables substantial memory savings without modifying model weights or requiring retraining.

Result: On LLaMA3.1-8B, VQKV achieves an 82.8% KV cache compression ratio while retaining 98.6% of baseline performance on LongBench and allowing 4.3x longer generations within the same memory budget.

Conclusion: Vector quantization of KV caches is an effective, training-free strategy for LLM memory compression, offering a better trade-off between compression ratio, model fidelity, and context length than prior low-rank or scalar-quantization-based methods.

Abstract: The growing context length of Large Language Models (LLMs) enlarges the Key-Value (KV) cache, limiting deployment in resource-limited environments. Prior training-free approaches for KV cache compression typically rely on low-rank approximation or scalar quantization, which fail to simultaneously achieve high compression ratios and high reconstruction fidelity. We propose VQKV, a novel, training-free method introducing vector quantization (VQ) to obtain highly compressed KV representations while preserving high model fidelity, allowing for the representation of thousands of floating-point values with just a few integer indices. As a result, VQKV achieves an 82.8\% compression ratio on LLaMA3.1-8B while retaining 98.6\% of the baseline performance on LongBench and enabling 4.3x longer generation length on the same memory footprint.

### [43] [DynHD: Hallucination Detection for Diffusion Large Language Models via Denoising Dynamics Deviation Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16459)
*Yanyu Qian,Yue Tan,Yixin Liu,Wang Yu,Shirui Pan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: DynHD is a hallucination detection framework tailored for diffusion large language models that leverages both token-wise semantics and the temporal evolution of uncertainty to more accurately and efficiently identify hallucinated content.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Diffusion large language models generate text through iterative refinement and are a promising alternative to auto-regressive LLMs, but they still suffer from hallucinations that undermine reliability. Existing token-level uncertainty metrics like entropy are used for hallucination detection, yet in diffusion models only a subset of tokens carries useful hallucination signals due to fixed-length generation. Furthermore, prior work typically ignores how uncertainty evolves over diffusion steps, missing potentially rich temporal cues. There is a need for a method that (1) focuses on semantically informative tokens and (2) explicitly models the denoising dynamics of uncertainty for better hallucination detection.

Method: The authors introduce DynHD, a hallucination detection framework operating along spatial (token sequence) and temporal (denoising dynamics) dimensions. Spatially, they design a semantic-aware evidence construction module that filters out non-informative tokens and emphasizes semantically meaningful ones to build stronger hallucination-indicative evidence from token-level uncertainty (e.g., entropy). Temporally, they add a reference evidence generator that learns an expected evolution trajectory of uncertainty evidence over diffusion steps. A deviation-based hallucination detector then compares the observed uncertainty trajectory of a given generation with the learned reference trajectory and predicts hallucinations based on their discrepancy. The approach is model-agnostic over diffusion backbones and leverages uncertainty statistics collected across denoising iterations.

Result: Across multiple hallucination detection benchmarks and different diffusion LLM backbones, DynHD consistently surpasses state-of-the-art baselines in detection performance while also being more efficient. The experiments validate that both components—semantic-aware evidence construction and trajectory-based deviation modeling—contribute to the performance gains.

Conclusion: DynHD demonstrates that hallucination detection for diffusion LLMs benefits from jointly exploiting spatial token semantics and temporal uncertainty dynamics. By focusing on informative tokens and learning typical denoising uncertainty trajectories as references, the method detects hallucinations more accurately and efficiently than prior approaches. This suggests that uncertainty evolution in diffusion processes is a valuable signal for reliability assessment and provides a practical path toward more trustworthy diffusion-based language models.

Abstract: Diffusion large language models (D-LLMs) have emerged as a promising alternative to auto-regressive models due to their iterative refinement capabilities. However, hallucinations remain a critical issue that hinders their reliability. To detect hallucination responses from model outputs, token-level uncertainty (e.g., entropy) has been widely used as an effective signal to indicate potential factual errors. Nevertheless, the fixed-length generation paradigm of D-LLMs implies that tokens contribute unevenly to hallucination detection, with only a small subset providing meaningful signals. Moreover, the evolution trend of uncertainty throughout the diffusion process can also provide important signals, highlighting the necessity of modeling its denoising dynamics for hallucination detection. In this paper, we propose DynHD that bridge these gaps from both spatial (token sequence) and temporal (denoising dynamics) perspectives. To address the information density imbalance across tokens, we propose a semantic-aware evidence construction module that extracts hallucination-indicative signals by filtering out non-informative tokens and emphasizing semantically meaningful ones. To model denoising dynamics for hallucination detection, we introduce a reference evidence generator that learns the expected evolution trajectory of uncertainty evidence, along with a deviation-based hallucination detector that makes predictions by measuring the discrepancy between the observed and reference trajectories. Extensive experiments demonstrate that DynHD consistently outperforms state-of-the-art baselines while achieving higher efficiency across multiple benchmarks and backbone models.

</details>

  <summary>Details</summary>

Result: They observe that existing SER models fail to generalize well from human to synthesized speech. A key factor is a representation mismatch introduced by the speech token prediction process used in modern synthesis pipelines, which alters the acoustic patterns SER models expect. Furthermore, generative speech language models tend to infer emotion mostly from text content (semantics) instead of paralinguistic acoustic cues, indicating a bias toward non-robust or spurious features.

Conclusion: The work concludes that today’s SER models are unreliable as automatic rewards or metrics for emotional speech synthesis, because they exploit dataset- and modality-specific shortcuts rather than robust, fundamental emotion features. It also highlights that achieving robust paralinguistic understanding in speech language models is still an open and challenging problem, calling for new model designs or training strategies that explicitly address these limitations.

Abstract: Emotion is a core paralinguistic feature in voice interaction. It is widely believed that emotion understanding models learn fundamental representations that transfer to synthesized speech, making emotion understanding results a plausible reward or evaluation metric for assessing emotional expressiveness in speech synthesis. In this work, we critically examine this assumption by systematically evaluating Speech Emotion Recognition (SER) on synthesized speech across datasets, discriminative and generative SER models, and diverse synthesis models. We find that current SER models can not generalize to synthesized speech, largely because speech token prediction during synthesis induces a representation mismatch between synthesized and human speech. Moreover, generative Speech Language Models (SLMs) tend to infer emotion from textual semantics while ignoring paralinguistic cues. Overall, our findings suggest that existing SER models often exploit non-robust shortcuts rather than capturing fundamental features, and paralinguistic understanding in SLMs remains challenging.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors design AdaMem, a unified, adaptive memory architecture with four components: working memory for recent context, episodic memory for structured long-term experiences, persona memory for stable user traits, and graph memory for relation-aware connections. At inference, AdaMem first identifies the target participant (e.g., which user or agent the query concerns), then constructs a question-conditioned retrieval route that blends standard semantic retrieval with selective graph expansion only when necessary. Result: On two benchmarks—LoCoMo for long-horizon reasoning and PERSONAMEM for user modeling—AdaMem attains state-of-the-art performance compared with prior memory systems for LLM agents. By combining multiple memory types, relation-aware graph structures, and question-conditioned retrieval strategies, AdaMem addresses key limitations of previous memory systems and delivers SOTA performance on challenging benchmarks. The framework demonstrates that more nuanced memory organization and retrieval can significantly enhance long-term interaction and personalization in dialogue agents.

Abstract: Large language model (LLM) agents increasingly rely on external memory to support long-horizon interaction, personalized assistance, and multi-step reasoning. However, existing memory systems still face three core challenges: they often rely too heavily on semantic similarity, which can miss evidence crucial for user-centric understanding; they frequently store related experiences as isolated fragments, weakening temporal and causal coherence; and they typically use static memory granularities that do not adapt well to the requirements of different questions. We propose AdaMem, an adaptive user-centric memory framework for long-horizon dialogue agents. AdaMem organizes dialogue history into working, episodic, persona, and graph memories, enabling the system to preserve recent context, structured long-term experiences, stable user traits, and relation-aware connections within a unified framework. At inference time, AdaMem first resolves the target participant, then builds a question-conditioned retrieval route that combines semantic retrieval with relation-aware graph expansion only when needed, and finally produces the answer through a role-specialized pipeline for evidence synthesis and response generation. We evaluate AdaMem on the LoCoMo and PERSONAMEM benchmarks for long-horizon reasoning and user modeling. Experimental results show that AdaMem achieves state-of-the-art performance on both benchmarks. The code will be released upon acceptance.

Conclusion: Explicitly modeling recency and stationarity (context dependence of answer changes) is crucial for understanding and improving LLM performance on time-sensitive questions. RecencyQA provides a fine-grained benchmark for analyzing temporal reasoning and lays groundwork for building recency-aware, context-sensitive QA systems that better decide when and how to use up-to-date information.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) often rely on outdated knowledge when answering time-sensitive questions, leading to confident yet incorrect responses. Without explicit signals indicating whether up-to-date information is required, models struggle to decide when to retrieve external evidence, how to reason about stale facts, and how to rank answers by their validity. Existing benchmarks either periodically refresh answers or rely on fixed templates, but they do not reflect on how frequently answers change or whether a question inherently requires up-to-date information. To address this gap, we introduce a recency-stationarity taxonomy that categorizes questions by how often their answers change and whether this change frequency is time-invariant or context-dependent. Building on this taxonomy, we present RecencyQA, a dataset of 4,031 open-domain questions annotated with recency and stationarity labels. Through human evaluation and empirical analysis, we show that non-stationary questions, i.e., those where context changes the recency requirement, are significantly more challenging for LLMs, with difficulty increasing as update frequency rises. By explicitly modeling recency and context dependence, RecencyQA enables fine-grained benchmarking and analysis of temporal reasoning beyond binary notions of freshness, and provides a foundation for developing recency-aware and context-sensitive question answering systems.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Anecdotal media and legal reports describe severe psychological harms from LLM chatbots—such as delusions, self-harm ideation, and so-called "AI psychosis"—but there is little systematic, empirical understanding of how these harmful interactions unfold over time, especially in long, multi-turn conversations. Without such understanding, it is difficult for researchers, developers, and policymakers to design effective safeguards or interventions to reduce harm.

Method: The authors collect and analyze conversation logs between LLM chatbots and 19 users who self-report psychological harm from chatbot use, including users from a dedicated support group and cases previously covered in major media stories. The results highlight systematic patterns in how harmful dynamics develop and escalate over multi-turn interactions.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that empirically documented harmful chatbot-user interactions are real, patterned, and especially pronounced in long conversations where chatbots present themselves as sentient or engage in romantic dynamics. The authors argue that current safeguards may degrade over time in multi-turn settings and provide concrete recommendations and tools—such as their 28-code inventory and conversation analysis framework—for policymakers, LLM developers, and end users to better detect, monitor, and mitigate psychological harms arising from LLM chatbot use.

However, it remains unclear how users and chatbots interact over the course of lengthy delusional ``spirals,'' limiting our ability to understand and mitigate the harm. In our work, we analyze logs of conversations with LLM chatbots from 19 users who report having experienced psychological harms from chatbot use. Many of our participants come from a support group for such chatbot users. We also include chat logs from participants covered by media outlets in widely-distributed stories about chatbot-reinforced delusions. In contrast to prior work that speculates on potential AI harms to mental health, to our knowledge we present the first in-depth study of such high-profile and veridically harmful cases. We develop an inventory of 28 codes and apply it to the $391,562$ messages in the logs. Codes include whether a user demonstrates delusional thinking (15.5% of user messages), a user expresses suicidal thoughts (69 validated user messages), or a chatbot misrepresents itself as sentient (21.2% of chatbot messages). We analyze the co-occurrence of message codes. We find, for example, that messages that declare romantic interest and messages where the chatbot describes itself as sentient occur much more often in longer conversations, suggesting that these topics could promote or result from user over-engagement and that safeguards in these areas may degrade in multi-turn settings. We conclude with concrete recommendations for how policymakers, LLM chatbot developers, and users can use our inventory and conversation analysis tool to understand and mitigate harm from LLM chatbots.
  Warning: This paper discusses self-harm, trauma, and violence.

</details>


### [50] [Diverging Transformer Predictions for Human Sentence Processing: A Comprehensive Analysis of Agreement Attraction Effects](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16574)
*Titus von der Malsburg,Sebastian Padó*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper tests whether current autoregressive transformer language models can serve as cognitively plausible models of human sentence processing, focusing on agreement attraction, and finds they largely fail on key configurations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although transformers drive state-of-the-art performance in NLP, it is unclear whether they capture the mechanisms humans use to process sentences, particularly morphosyntactic agreement under interference (agreement attraction). Prior work has used limited syntactic configurations and few models, risking over-generalized claims about cognitive adequacy. The authors aim to provide a more systematic and comprehensive evaluation of transformers as models of human sentence processing in agreement attraction scenarios.

Method: The authors evaluate eleven autoregressive transformer language models that vary in size and architecture. They apply a surprisal-based linking hypothesis: model surprisals on critical words are compared to human reading time data. The models are tested on an extensive set of English subject–verb agreement attraction configurations, including both prepositional phrase (PP) structures and object-extracted relative clause (ORC) structures, enabling direct comparison between model predictions and established human interference patterns.

Result: For prepositional phrase agreement attraction configurations, model surprisal patterns broadly track human reading time data, indicating partial alignment with human processing. However, for object-extracted relative clause configurations, the models’ performance degrades markedly: predictions diverge sharply across different transformer models, and none captures the characteristic asymmetric interference effects known from human sentence processing experiments. This mixed performance shows both limited successes and clear failures depending on syntactic structure.

Conclusion: Current autoregressive transformer language models do not provide an adequate explanation of human morphosyntactic sentence processing, especially in more complex agreement attraction contexts like object-extracted relative clauses. The authors argue that using transformers as cognitive models requires more rigorous and comprehensive experimental designs, testing a broad range of syntactic configurations and multiple models, to avoid drawing misleading conclusions from narrow or cherry-picked evaluations.

Abstract: Transformers underlie almost all state-of-the-art language models in computational linguistics, yet their cognitive adequacy as models of human sentence processing remains disputed. In this work, we use a surprisal-based linking mechanism to systematically evaluate eleven autoregressive transformers of varying sizes and architectures on a more comprehensive set of English agreement attraction configurations than prior work. Our experiments yield mixed results: While transformer predictions generally align with human reading time data for prepositional phrase configurations, performance degrades significantly on object-extracted relative clause configurations. In the latter case, predictions also diverge markedly across models, and no model successfully replicates the asymmetric interference patterns observed in humans. We conclude that current transformer models do not explain human morphosyntactic processing, and that evaluations of transformers as cognitive models must adopt rigorous, comprehensive experimental designs to avoid spurious generalizations from isolated syntactic configurations or individual models.

### [51] [BATQuant: Outlier-resilient MXFP4 Quantization via Learnable Block-wise Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16590)
*Ji-Fu Li,Manyi Zhang,Xiaobo Xia,Han Bao,Haoli Bai,Zhenhua Dong,Xianzhi Yu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: BATQuant is a post‑training quantization framework tailored to microscaling floating‑point (MXFP) formats that avoids the failure modes of rotation-based PTQ and achieves state-of-the-art performance for 4-bit weight/activation M(LLM)s.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Deploying large and multi-modal language models on modern accelerators requires aggressive low-bit quantization. Microscaling floating-point formats like MXFP4 are promising, but existing PTQ, especially rotation-based methods developed for integer quantization, break down: they cause performance collapse because their global orthogonal rotations interact poorly with MXFP’s block-wise scaling, spreading outlier energy across blocks and producing bimodal distributions that waste the narrow quantization range. A specialized PTQ method that respects MXFP’s structure while still reshaping activation/weight distributions is needed.

Method: The paper introduces BATQuant, a PTQ method that applies Block-wise Affine Transformations rather than global orthogonal rotations. Transformations are confined to the same granularity as MXFP quantization blocks, preventing cross-block outlier propagation. Orthogonality is relaxed so that affine parameters can better shape distributions to fit within the limited MXFP4 range. To keep the method parameter-efficient, the authors propose Global and Private Kronecker (GPK) decomposition, factorizing transformation parameters into shared global and block-private components, reducing storage and runtime cost. Additionally, they add Block-wise Learnable Clipping to explicitly suppress residual outliers in each block. The entire scheme is applied post-training without requiring full model retraining.

Result: On both multimodal LLMs (MLLMs) and standard LLMs under an aggressive quantization setting (W4A4KV16: 4-bit weights, 4-bit activations, and 16-bit key-value for attention), BATQuant sets new state-of-the-art PTQ performance in MXFP4. It recovers up to 96.43% of the full-precision model performance on a range of multimodal benchmarks and consistently outperforms previous PTQ techniques across diverse tasks, resolving the performance collapse seen with rotation-based methods in MXFP.

Conclusion: Rotation-based PTQ designed for integer formats is fundamentally mismatched with microscaling floating-point formats because global rotations conflict with block-wise scaling and limited dynamic range. By respecting MXFP’s block granularity and allowing flexible, parameter-efficient affine transformations plus learnable clipping, BATQuant can effectively reshape activations/weights, contain outliers locally, and make better use of the quantization range. This leads to robust, high-accuracy 4-bit deployment of large and multimodal LLMs on modern accelerators, establishing BATQuant as a strong practical solution for MXFP-based quantization.

Abstract: Microscaling floating-point (MXFP) formats have emerged as a promising standard for deploying Multi-modal Large Language Models (MLLMs) and Large Language Models (LLMs) on modern accelerator architectures. However, existing Post-Training Quantization (PTQ) methods, particularly rotation-based techniques designed for integer formats, suffer from severe performance collapse when applied to MXFP4. Recent studies attribute this failure to a fundamental format mismatch: global orthogonal rotations inadvertently transfer outlier energy across quantization blocks, inducing new outliers that disrupt local block-wise scaling, while often creating bimodal activation distributions that underutilize the limited quantization range. To address these issues, we propose BATQuant (Block-wise Affine Transformation), which restricts transformations to align with MXFP granularity to prevent cross-block outlier propagation, while relaxing orthogonality constraints to optimize distribution shaping. To ensure parameter efficiency, we introduce Global and Private Kronecker (GPK) decomposition to effectively reduces storage and runtime overhead and incorporate Block-wise Learnable Clipping to suppress residual outliers. Extensive experiments on both MLLMs and LLMs demonstrate that BATQuant establishes new state-of-the-art results under aggressive W4A4KV16 configurations, recovering up to 96.43% of full-precision performance on multimodal benchmarks and clearly outperforming existing methods across diverse tasks.

</details>

*Mo El-Haj*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Tarab Corpus is a large, open Arabic corpus of songs and poetry spanning 14+ centuries and multiple dialects, designed for comparative linguistic and cultural analysis.

The authors provide validated, normalised texts with rich metadata and show that the corpus can be used effectively for automatic variety identification and genre differentiation through baseline experiments.

Conclusion: Tarab is, to the authors’ knowledge, the largest open Arabic corpus of creative texts unifying songs and poetry across classical and contemporary periods and dialects. The corpus comprises 2.56 million verses and more than 13.5 million tokens, making it, to our knowledge, the largest open Arabic corpus of creative text spanning both classical and contemporary production. Tarab is broadly balanced between songs and poems and covers Classical Arabic, Modern Standard Arabic (MSA), and six major regional varieties: Egyptian, Gulf, Levantine, Iraqi, Sudanese, and Maghrebi Arabic. The artists and poets represented in the corpus are associated with 28 modern nation states and multiple historical eras, covering over fourteen centuries of Arabic creative expression from the Pre-Islamic period to the twenty-first century. Each verse is accompanied by structured metadata describing linguistic variety, geographic origin, and historical or cultural context, enabling comparative linguistic, stylistic, and diachronic analysis across genres and time. We describe the data collection, normalisation, and validation pipeline and present baseline analyses for variety identification and genre differentiation.
