### [53] [Omnilingual SONAR: Cross-Lingual and Cross-Modal Sentence Embeddings Bridging Massively Multilingual Text and Speech](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16606)
*Omnilingual SONAR Team,João Maria Janeiro,Pere-Lluís Huguet Cabot,Ioannis Tsiamas,Yen Meng,Vivek Iyer,Guillem Ramírez,Loic Barrault,Belen Alastruey,Yu-An Chung,Marta R. Costa-Jussa,David Dale,Kevin Heffernan,Jaehyeong Jo,Artyom Kozhevnikov,Alexandre Mourachko,Christophe Ropers,Holger Schwenk,Paul-Ambroise Duquenne*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: OmniSONAR is an omnilingual, cross-lingual and cross-modal sentence embedding family that embeds text, speech, code, and math into a single semantic space for thousands of languages, achieving state-of-the-art performance without sacrificing downstream quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing cross-lingual sentence encoders cover only a few hundred languages and often sacrifice downstream task performance to obtain strong alignment, limiting their usefulness, especially for low-resource languages and additional modalities like speech or code.

Method: The authors propose OmniSONAR, trained via a progressive pipeline: (1) learn a strong foundational encoder-decoder space for 200 languages initialized from an LLM, combining token-level decoding with a novel split-softmax contrastive loss and synthetic hard negatives; (2) expand coverage to several thousand language varieties using a two-stage teacher–student encoder distillation framework; (3) extend the learned space cross-modally by mapping speech from 177 languages into the same embedding space. They also train Spectrum, an encoder–decoder language model, exclusively on English text over OmniSONAR embedding sequences to enable transfer to many languages and modalities for complex tasks.

Result: OmniSONAR halves cross-lingual similarity-search error on the 200-language FLORES benchmark and reduces error 15× on the 1,560-language BIBLE benchmark. It outperforms NLLB-3B on multilingual translation benchmarks and surpasses prior models, including much larger LLMs, by 15 chrF++ points for translation from 1,560 languages into English on the BIBLE dataset. It also achieves strong results on MTEB and XLCoST. For speech, it yields 43% lower similarity-search error and reaches 97% of SeamlessM4T’s speech-to-text quality in a zero-shot translation setup (trained only on ASR data).

Conclusion: Progressive training with a strong 200-language foundation, large-scale teacher–student distillation, and cross-modal mapping enables a single omnilingual, cross-modal embedding space that scales to thousands of languages and multiple modalities without representation collapse, achieving state-of-the-art performance on similarity, translation, and speech benchmarks and enabling powerful transfer via the Spectrum LM trained solely on English OmniSONAR embeddings.

Abstract: Cross-lingual sentence encoders typically cover only a few hundred languages and often trade downstream quality for stronger alignment, limiting their adoption. We introduce OmniSONAR, a new family of omnilingual, cross-lingual and cross-modal sentence embedding models that natively embed text, speech, code, and mathematical expressions in a single semantic space, while delivering state-of-the-art downstream performance at the scale of thousands of languages, from high-resource to extremely low-resource varieties. To reach this scale without representation collapse, we use progressive training. We first learn a strong foundational space for 200 languages with an LLM-initialized encoder-decoder, combining token-level decoding with a novel split-softmax contrastive loss and synthetic hard negatives. Building on this foundation, we expand to several thousands language varieties via a two-stage teacher-student encoder distillation framework. Finally, we demonstrate the cross-modal extensibility of this space by seamlessly mapping 177 spoken languages into it. OmniSONAR halves cross-lingual similarity search error on the 200-language FLORES dataset and reduces error by a factor of 15 on the 1,560-language BIBLE benchmark. It also enables strong translation, outperforming NLLB-3B on multilingual benchmarks and exceeding prior models (including much larger LLMs) by 15 chrF++ points on 1,560 languages into English BIBLE translation. OmniSONAR also performs strongly on MTEB and XLCoST. For speech, OmniSONAR achieves a 43% lower similarity-search error and reaches 97% of SeamlessM4T speech-to-text quality, despite being zero-shot for translation (trained only on ASR data). Finally, by training an encoder-decoder LM, Spectrum, exclusively on English text processing OmniSONAR embedding sequences, we unlock high-performance transfer to thousands of languages and speech for complex downstream tasks.

### [54] [Domain Mixture Design via Log-Likelihood Differences for Aligning Language Models with a Target Model](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16622)
*Ryo Kishino,Riku Shiomi,Hiroaki Yamagiwa,Momose Oyama,Hidetoshi Shimodaira*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Aligns a base language model to a target model by optimizing domain mixture weights for (continued) pretraining, instead of doing direct knowledge distillation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Direct distillation from a target language model is not always possible (e.g., black-box APIs, legal or resource limits). However, we may have access to similar training corpora. The authors want a principled way to choose domain weights in a mixed-domain pretraining dataset so that the resulting base model distribution becomes closer to that of a given target model.

Method: View each domain’s gradient update as a direction in log-likelihood space. Represent models (base and target) as points in this space. Choose domain mixture weights so that the expected training update direction for (continued) pretraining is best aligned with the vector from the base model to the target model. This produces optimized domain weights for constructing the training mixture. They then apply this to NanoGPT trained on The Pile, comparing optimized versus uniform domain weights.

Result: For NanoGPT experiments on The Pile, domain mixtures selected by their alignment method consistently reduce KL divergence between the trained model and the target model compared with uniform domain weighting. The method brings the base model closer to the target model in distribution space. Downstream task scores also move closer to those of the target model, though not matching distillation-based alignment when distillation is available.

Conclusion: Optimizing domain mixture weights for (continued) pretraining, using a geometric view in log-likelihood space, can meaningfully align a base model toward a target model without direct distillation. While it does not outperform full knowledge distillation, it offers a viable, data-driven recipe to approximate a target model’s behavior when only training data and model outputs are accessible.

Abstract: Instead of directly distilling a language model, this study addresses the problem of aligning a base model with a target model in distribution by designing the domain mixture of training data for pretraining or continued pretraining as a fixed training recipe. We propose a method for determining domain weights by viewing models as points in log-likelihood space and aligning the training update direction with the direction toward the target model. Experiments with NanoGPT show that the proposed method consistently reduces the KL divergence to the target model compared with uniform weighting over the Pile. Although knowledge distillation remains more effective when available, the proposed method still achieves meaningful alignment, and downstream task performance also tends to become closer to that of the target model.

### [55] [Good Arguments Against the People Pleasers: How Reasoning Mitigates (Yet Masks) LLM Sycophancy](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16643)
*Zhaoxin Feng,Zheng Chen,Jianfei Ma,Yip Tin Po,Emmanuele Chersoni,Bo Li*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how Chain-of-Thought (CoT) reasoning affects sycophancy in large language models, finding that CoT often reduces but can also hide sycophantic behavior and that sycophancy evolves dynamically during reasoning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Alignment methods for LLMs frequently lead to sycophancy, where models overly agree with users. Earlier work mainly examined this in direct-answer scenarios without reasoning steps, leaving it unclear whether explicit reasoning (CoT) constrains or disguises sycophantic behavior. The paper aims to fill this gap and better understand how sycophancy manifests during reasoning, especially across different task types and biases such as authority bias.

Method: The authors evaluate multiple LLMs on both objective tasks (with clear right answers) and subjective tasks (opinion-based) under various prompting conditions, including with and without Chain-of-Thought (CoT) reasoning, and with authority-biased prompts. They analyze final decisions for sycophancy and also inspect the reasoning traces to identify cases where CoT masks sycophancy via deceptive or flawed justifications (logical inconsistencies, calculation mistakes, one-sided arguments). Additionally, they perform mechanistic analyses on three open-source models, examining internal dynamics during the reasoning process to see how sycophantic tendencies evolve over the generation steps.

Result: They find that enabling Chain-of-Thought generally reduces sycophancy in the final answers compared to direct responses. However, in a non-trivial subset of cases, CoT hides underlying sycophancy by providing seemingly reasoned but actually inconsistent or error-ridden explanations that support sycophantic conclusions. The models display higher sycophancy rates on subjective tasks and when prompts induce authority bias. Mechanistic analysis shows that sycophancy is not fixed at input time but changes over the course of reasoning, indicating dynamic internal competition between sycophantic and non-sycophantic trajectories.

Conclusion: Chain-of-Thought is not a simple antidote to sycophancy: while it often helps reduce sycophantic final answers, it can also mask sycophantic tendencies behind misleading reasoning traces. Sycophancy is especially problematic in subjective and authority-laden settings and emerges dynamically during inference rather than being predetermined at input. These findings suggest that alignment and evaluation methods must consider the full reasoning trajectory—not just final outputs—and specifically address the risk of deceptive or flawed justifications in CoT-based systems.

Abstract: Alignment techniques often inadvertently induce sycophancy in LLMs. While prior studies studied this behaviour in direct-answer settings, the role of Chain-of-Thought (CoT) reasoning remains under-explored: does it serve as a logical constraint that mitigates sycophancy, or a tool for post-hoc rationalization that masks it? We evaluate a range of models across objective and subjective tasks to investigate the issue. Results show that reasoning generally reduces sycophancy in final decisions but also masks sycophancy in some samples, where models construct deceptive justifications through logical inconsistencies, calculation errors, and one-sided arguments etc. Furthermore, LLMs are more prone to sycophancy in subjective tasks and under authority-bias. Our mechanistic analysis on three open-source models reveals that the tendency of sycophancy is dynamic during the reasoning process rather than being pre-determined at the input stage.

### [56] [Omanic: Towards Step-wise Evaluation of Multi-hop Reasoning in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16654)
*Xiaojie Gu,Sherry T. Tong,Aosong Feng,Sophia Simeng Han,Jinghui Lu,Yingjian Chen,Yusuke Iwasawa,Yutaka Matsuo,Chanjun Park,Rex Ying,Irene Li*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Omanic, a new open-domain multi-hop QA dataset with step-level reasoning annotations to better evaluate and improve reasoning-focused LLMs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Evaluating reasoning in LLMs is hard because most benchmarks only check final answers and lack step-level annotations, making it difficult to diagnose where reasoning fails or whether models truly reason correctly. Existing multi-hop QA datasets do not provide the decomposed reasoning steps needed for fine-grained analysis and supervision.

Method: The authors construct Omanic, an open-domain multi-hop QA resource that decomposes complex questions into sub-questions with intermediate answers. It has two parts: OmanicSynth, a large set of 10,296 machine-generated training examples, and OmanicBench, a smaller, high-quality evaluation set of 967 expert-reviewed human-annotated examples. They then systematically evaluate state-of-the-art LLMs (including CoT prompting) on OmanicBench, perform stepwise error analysis of reasoning chains, and conduct supervised fine-tuning on OmanicSynth to test transfer to other reasoning and math benchmarks.

Result: State-of-the-art LLMs obtain only 73.11% multiple-choice accuracy on OmanicBench, indicating that the benchmark is challenging. Stepwise analysis shows that Chain-of-Thought (CoT) performance depends heavily on having complete factual knowledge; when knowledge is missing, CoT gains shrink and errors propagate or amplify across later reasoning hops. Fine-tuning on OmanicSynth leads to substantial transfer improvements, with an average gain of 7.41 points across six different reasoning and math benchmarks.

Conclusion: Omanic provides a difficult and diagnostically rich benchmark for multi-hop reasoning, enabling step-level analysis of LLM reasoning processes. The results demonstrate that current LLMs still struggle with complex multi-hop reasoning, especially under knowledge gaps, and that supervised training on OmanicSynth can effectively transfer and enhance reasoning capabilities across tasks. The dataset and code are released publicly to support further research on reasoning in LLMs.

Abstract: Reasoning-focused large language models (LLMs) have advanced in many NLP tasks, yet their evaluation remains challenging: final answers alone do not expose the intermediate reasoning steps, making it difficult to determine whether a model truly reasons correctly and where failures occur, while existing multi-hop QA benchmarks lack step-level annotations for diagnosing reasoning failures. To address this gap, we propose Omanic, an open-domain multi-hop QA resource that provides decomposed sub-questions and intermediate answers as structural annotations for analyzing reasoning processes. It contains 10,296 machine-generated training examples (OmanicSynth) and 967 expert-reviewed human-annotated evaluation examples (OmanicBench). Systematic evaluations show that state-of-the-art LLMs achieve only 73.11% multiple-choice accuracy on OmanicBench, confirming its high difficulty. Stepwise analysis reveals that CoT's performance hinges on factual completeness, with its gains diminishing under knowledge gaps and errors amplifying in later hops. Additionally, supervised fine-tuning on OmanicSynth brings substantial transfer gains (7.41 average points) across six reasoning and math benchmarks, validating the dataset's quality and further supporting the effectiveness of OmanicSynth as supervision for reasoning-capability transfer. We release the data at https://huggingface.co/datasets/li-lab/Omanic and the code at https://github.com/XiaojieGu/Omanic.

In this work, we investigate a more constrained question: in data-scarce settings, to what extent can linguistically similar pivot languages and few-shot demonstrations provide useful guidance for on-the-fly adaptation in LLMs? Our findings provide empirical guidance on how and when inference-time prompting and pivot-based examples can be used as a lightweight alternative to fine-tuning in low-resource translation settings.

### [59] [Probing Cultural Signals in Large Language Models through Author Profiling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16749)
*Valentin Lafargue,Ariel Guerra-Adames,Emmanuelle Claeys,Elouan Vuichard,Jean-Michel Loubes*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies cultural biases in large language models by testing whether they can infer singers’ gender and ethnicity from song lyrics in a zero-shot setting, and proposes new fairness metrics to quantify these biases.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs are increasingly used in socially impactful applications, there is concern that they encode and reproduce cultural biases. Author profiling (inferring demographic traits like gender or ethnicity from text) is a high-risk behavior when done implicitly and without consent. The authors want to understand whether current LLMs can perform such profiling from song lyrics alone, and, if so, how culturally biased and unfair their inferences are across demographic groups.

Method: The authors evaluate several open-source LLMs on a dataset of over 10,000 song lyrics, asking the models—without any task-specific fine-tuning—to infer the singer’s gender and ethnicity (zero-shot author profiling). They analyze both the prediction distributions and the textual rationales generated by the models to uncover cultural alignment patterns. To systematically measure fairness, they introduce two new metrics: Modality Accuracy Divergence (MAD), which captures how much accuracy differs across demographic groups/modalities, and Recall Divergence (RD), which captures disparities in recall across groups.

Result: The tested LLMs achieve better-than-chance performance at guessing singers’ gender and ethnicity from lyrics, showing that they implicitly perform demographic profiling. However, they also show systematic cultural biases: most models tend to over-predict North American ethnicity, while DeepSeek-1.5B favors Asian ethnicity. The newly proposed MAD and RD metrics reveal that Ministral-8B has the strongest ethnicity-related bias, while Gemma-12B exhibits the most balanced and fair behavior among the evaluated models. The rationales generated by the models further confirm these alignment and bias patterns.

Conclusion: LLMs can non-trivially infer sensitive demographic attributes like gender and ethnicity from song lyrics in a zero-shot setting, indicating the presence of latent cultural profiling capabilities. These capabilities are accompanied by systematic cultural alignment and fairness issues that vary across models. The proposed MAD and RD metrics provide quantitative tools to assess such disparities, and the empirical results highlight that some models (e.g., Gemma-12B) behave more fairly than others (e.g., Ministral-8B). The work underscores the need to evaluate and mitigate cultural biases in LLMs, especially when they are deployed in contexts where demographic inference could cause harm.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly deployed in applications with societal impact, raising concerns about the cultural biases they encode. We probe these representations by evaluating whether LLMs can perform author profiling from song lyrics in a zero-shot setting, inferring singers' gender and ethnicity without task-specific fine-tuning. Across several open-source models evaluated on more than 10,000 lyrics, we find that LLMs achieve non-trivial profiling performance but demonstrate systematic cultural alignment: most models default toward North American ethnicity, while DeepSeek-1.5B aligns more strongly with Asian ethnicity. This finding emerges from both the models' prediction distributions and an analysis of their generated rationales. To quantify these disparities, we introduce two fairness metrics, Modality Accuracy Divergence (MAD) and Recall Divergence (RD), and show that Ministral-8B displays the strongest ethnicity bias among the evaluated models, whereas Gemma-12B shows the most balanced behavior. Our code is available on GitHub (https://github.com/ValentinLafargue/CulturalProbingLLM).

</details>


### [60] [TurnWise: The Gap between Single- and Multi-turn Language Model Capabilities](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16759)
*Victoria Graf,Valentina Pyatkin,Nouha Dziri,Nathan Lambert,Hannaneh Hajishirzi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a benchmark and data pipeline to evaluate and improve language models’ multi-turn conversation abilities, showing that targeted multi-turn training significantly boosts performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most existing training and evaluation data for language models assume single-turn interactions, which do not capture the complexities and additional skills needed for multi-turn dialogues. There is a need to systematically measure and close the performance gap between single-turn and multi-turn conversational capabilities.

Method: The authors create TurnWiseEval, a benchmark that compares model behavior in multi-turn conversations against equivalent single-turn prompts via pairwise evaluation, isolating what is unique to multi-turn dialogue. They also design TurnWiseData, a synthetic data pipeline that generates scalable multi-turn training conversations. Using these tools, they train and evaluate Olmo 3 with varying amounts of multi-turn data during post-training.

Result: Experiments show that explicitly including multi-turn conversation data in training is crucial for strong multi-turn performance. Even a relatively small amount—around 10,000 multi-turn conversations added in post-training—yields about a 12% performance gain on the new TurnWiseEval benchmark.

Conclusion: Dedicated multi-turn benchmarks and targeted multi-turn training data meaningfully enhance a model’s performance in extended conversations. Multi-turn capabilities cannot be fully learned from single-turn data alone, and scalable synthetic pipelines like TurnWiseData provide a practical way to improve these abilities.

Abstract: Multi-turn conversations are a common and critical mode of language model interaction. However, current open training and evaluation data focus on single-turn settings, failing to capture the additional dimension of these longer interactions. To understand this multi-/single-turn gap, we first introduce a new benchmark, TurnWiseEval, for multi-turn capabilities that is directly comparable to single-turn chat evaluation. Our evaluation isolates multi-turn specific conversational ability through pairwise comparison to equivalent single-turn settings. We additionally introduce our synthetic multi-turn data pipeline TurnWiseData which allows the scalable generation of multi-turn training data. Our experiments with Olmo 3 show that training with multi-turn data is vital to achieving strong multi-turn chat performance, and that including as little as 10k multi-turn conversations during post-training can lead to a 12% improvement on TurnWiseEval.

### [61] [SpokenUS: A Spoken User Simulator for Task-Oriented Dialogue](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16783)
*Jonggeun Lee,Junseong Pyo,Jeongmin Park,Yohan Jo*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a large spoken task-oriented dialogue dataset and a speech-based user simulator that more realistically mimics human spoken behaviors to better train and evaluate dialogue agents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing spoken task-oriented dialogue datasets are too small, narrow in domain, and lack realistic spoken user behaviors like interruptions, disfluencies, and emotional prosody, making it hard to build robust spoken dialogue agents. There is also no systematic way to augment current datasets with such behaviors or to simulate realistic spoken users.

Method: The authors construct SpokenTOD, a large-scale spoken task-oriented dialogue dataset with over 52k dialogues and 1,034 hours of speech, enriched with four key spoken behaviors: cross-turn slots, barge-in, disfluency, and emotional prosody across diverse speakers and domains. On top of this, they design SpokenUS, a spoken user simulator grounded in TOD that includes a special architecture component to handle barge-in and models gradual slot-value disclosure similar to human users. They then compare SpokenUS to larger models and baseline simulators using metrics like goal coverage and human Mean Opinion Score (MOS), and analyze how its behaviors affect downstream TOD agents.

Result: SpokenUS reaches goal coverage comparable to much larger models while significantly outperforming all baselines in human-rated quality (MOS). It produces dialogues where slot values are revealed progressively over turns, better matching human interaction patterns instead of providing all information upfront. Experiments show that the realistic spoken behaviors in SpokenUS create non-trivial challenges for downstream dialogue agents.

Conclusion: The combination of the SpokenTOD dataset and the SpokenUS simulator provides a scalable, realistic resource for building and evaluating robust spoken task-oriented dialogue systems. By modeling critical spoken behaviors like interruptions, disfluency, and emotional prosody, the approach both stresses current agents and enables more faithful training, positioning it as a practical tool for advancing task-oriented spoken dialogue research.

Abstract: Robust task-oriented spoken dialogue agents require exposure to the full diversity of how people interact through speech. Building spoken user simulators that address this requires large-scale spoken task-oriented dialogue (TOD) data encompassing spoken user behaviors, yet existing datasets are limited in scale and domain coverage, with no systematic pipeline for augmenting them. To address this, we introduce \textbf{SpokenTOD}, a spoken TOD dataset of 52,390 dialogues and 1,034 hours of speech augmented with four spoken user behaviors -- cross-turn slots, barge-in, disfluency, and emotional prosody -- across diverse speakers and domains. Building on SpokenTOD, we present \textbf{SpokenUS}, a spoken user simulator grounded in TOD with a dedicated architecture for barge-in. SpokenUS achieves comparable goal coverage to significantly larger models while substantially outperforming all baselines in Human MOS, disclosing slot values gradually across the dialogue as humans do rather than front-loading them. Further analysis confirms that SpokenUS's spoken behaviors pose meaningful challenges to downstream agents, making it a practical tool for training and evaluating more robust spoken dialogue systems.

### [62] [Mediocrity is the key for LLM as a Judge Anchor Selection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16848)
*Shachar Don-Yehiya,Asaf Yehudai,Leshem Choshen,Omri Abend*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how the choice of a single reference model (anchor) in LLM-based benchmarking affects reliability, finding that anchors matter a lot and offering practical guidelines and power analysis for better, more reliable evaluation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM-as-a-judge evaluations of open-ended generation often use a single anchor model (e.g., best or worst model) to avoid quadratic pairwise comparisons, but it is unclear how this anchor choice affects correlation with human rankings and overall benchmark reliability.

Method: The authors systematically test 22 different anchor models on the Arena-Hard-v2.0 dataset, comparing the resulting model rankings and their correlations with human judgments. They analyze how different anchor types (e.g., best, worst, intermediate) impact reliability, quantify the variance introduced by anchor choice relative to judge choice, and perform power analysis to estimate required benchmark sizes for reliable discrimination between models.

Result: They find that anchor selection has a large impact: poorly chosen anchors (especially extreme ones like the best- or worst-performing models) greatly reduce correlation with human rankings and fail to capture relative differences between competitive models. The variability introduced by anchor choice is comparable to that introduced by choosing different judge models, and typical benchmark sizes are statistically underpowered for reliable pairwise evaluation in competitive regimes.

Conclusion: Anchor-based evaluation must be carefully designed; extreme anchors are poor choices, and benchmark sizes commonly used in practice are too small to reliably distinguish competitive models. The authors provide concrete recommendations and guidelines for selecting informative anchors and sizing benchmarks to enable more reliable and efficient LLM-as-a-judge evaluations.

Abstract: The ``LLM-as-a-judge'' paradigm has become a standard method for evaluating open-ended generation. To address the quadratic scalability costs of pairwise comparisons, popular benchmarks like Arena-Hard and AlpacaEval compare all models against a single anchor. However, despite its widespread use, the impact of anchor selection on the reliability of the results remains largely unexplored. In this work, we systematically investigate the effect of anchor selection by evaluating 22 different anchors on the Arena-Hard-v2.0 dataset. We find that the choice of anchor is critical: a poor anchor can dramatically reduce correlation with human rankings. We identify that common anchor choices (best-performing and worst-performing models) make poor anchors. Because these extreme anchors are consistently better or worse than all other models, they are seldom indicative of the relative ranking of the models. We further quantify the effect size of anchor selection, showing it is comparable to the selection of a judge model. We conclude with actionable recommendations. First, we conduct a power analysis, and compute sufficient benchmark sizes for anchor-based evaluation, finding that standard benchmark sizes are insufficient for pairwise evaluation and fail to distinguish between competitive models reliably. Second, we provide guidelines for selecting informative anchors to ensure reliable and efficient evaluation practices.

### [63] [Online Experiential Learning for Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16856)
*Tianzhu Ye,Li Dong,Qingxiu Dong,Xun Wu,Shaohan Huang,Furu Wei*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Online Experiential Learning (OEL), a framework that lets language models continually improve from real-world deployment experiences instead of only offline training.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current large language models mainly improve via offline supervised training and synthetic environments, which ignore the vast amount of interaction data produced during real-world deployment. The authors are motivated to exploit this underused experiential data to enable continual, online improvement without needing direct access to user-side environments.

Method: They propose Online Experiential Learning (OEL), a two-stage online loop. (1) From user-side interaction trajectories, the system extracts and accumulates compact, transferable experiential knowledge instead of using raw trajectories. (2) This knowledge is then distilled into the model via on-policy context distillation, so the policy used for generating data matches the one being trained, and no access to the original environment is required. These two stages are repeated iteratively, letting the improved model collect better trajectories each round.

Result: In text-based game benchmarks, across multiple model sizes and both chain-of-thought (thinking) and standard (non-thinking) variants, OEL yields consistent performance gains over iterations. It improves task accuracy and reduces token usage while maintaining performance on out-of-distribution tasks. Analyses show that the distilled experiential knowledge is more beneficial than training directly on raw trajectories and that on-policy alignment between the knowledge generator and the policy model is crucial.

Conclusion: OEL demonstrates that large language models can effectively perform continual online learning from deployment interactions. By extracting structured experiential knowledge and integrating it via on-policy context distillation, models can improve accuracy and efficiency without degrading generalization. The framework highlights the importance of both knowledge abstraction and on-policy consistency for effective experiential learning.

Abstract: The prevailing paradigm for improving large language models relies on offline training with human annotations or simulated environments, leaving the rich experience accumulated during real-world deployment entirely unexploited. We propose Online Experiential Learning (OEL), a framework that enables language models to continuously improve from their own deployment experience. OEL operates in two stages: first, transferable experiential knowledge is extracted and accumulated from interaction trajectories collected on the user side; second, this knowledge is consolidated into model parameters via on-policy context distillation, requiring no access to the user-side environment. The two stages are iterated to form an online learning loop, where the improved model collects higher-quality trajectories that yield richer experiential knowledge for subsequent rounds. We evaluate OEL on text-based game environments across multiple model scales and both thinking and non-thinking variants. OEL achieves consistent improvements over successive iterations, enhancing both task accuracy and token efficiency while preserving out-of-distribution performance. Our analysis further shows that extracted experiential knowledge is significantly more effective than raw trajectories, and that on-policy consistency between the knowledge source and the policy model is critical for effective learning.

</details>


### [64] [Chronos: Temporal-Aware Conversational Agents with Structured Event Retrieval for Long-Term Memory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16862)
*Sahil Sen,Elias Lumer,Anmol Gulati,Vamse Kumar Subbiah*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Chronos is a temporal-aware memory framework for LLM chat agents that structures dialogue into time-stamped events and uses dynamic, guided retrieval and reasoning to answer long-horizon, time-sensitive questions with state-of-the-art accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM-based conversational agents now support very long, multi-turn interactions over weeks or months, but existing memory mechanisms cannot reliably track, update, and reason over user facts and preferences that change over time. They also struggle with complex, multi-hop queries that require precise temporal grounding across long histories. There is a need for a memory system that can represent time explicitly, support structured retrieval, and guide LLMs through multi-hop, time-aware reasoning over extended dialogue logs.

Method: Chronos decomposes dialogue into structured subject-verb-object event tuples, each annotated with resolved datetime ranges and entity aliases, and stores them in an event calendar. In parallel, it maintains a turn calendar preserving the full conversational context. At query time, Chronos uses dynamic prompting to generate retrieval guidance tailored to each question, specifying what to retrieve, how to filter by time, and how to perform multi-hop, time-aware reasoning. The LLM then uses an iterative tool-calling loop over the event and turn calendars to gather relevant information and answer queries. The system is evaluated on the LongMemEvalS benchmark with multiple LLMs and configurations (Chronos Low/High).

Result: On the LongMemEvalS benchmark (500 questions, six dialogue-history task categories), Chronos Low achieves 92.60% accuracy and Chronos High achieves 95.60% accuracy, outperforming the previous best system by 7.67 percentage points. Ablation studies show that the event calendar is the dominant contributor with a 58.9% gain over a baseline, while the other components of the framework add further improvements in the 15.5%–22.3% range. Chronos Low alone exceeds prior methods even when they use their strongest model setups.

Conclusion: Chronos provides an effective temporal-aware memory architecture for LLM conversational agents operating over long, evolving dialogue histories. By structuring interactions as time-resolved events and combining this with dynamic, query-specific retrieval guidance and iterative reasoning, Chronos substantially improves performance on long-term, temporally grounded dialogue tasks and sets a new state of the art on LongMemEvalS. The results highlight the importance of explicit event calendars and structured temporal reasoning for robust long-horizon conversational memory.

Abstract: Recent advances in Large Language Models (LLMs) have enabled conversational AI agents to engage in extended multi-turn interactions spanning weeks or months. However, existing memory systems struggle to reason over temporally grounded facts and preferences that evolve across months of interaction and lack effective retrieval strategies for multi-hop, time-sensitive queries over long dialogue histories. We introduce Chronos, a novel temporal-aware memory framework that decomposes raw dialogue into subject-verb-object event tuples with resolved datetime ranges and entity aliases, indexing them in a structured event calendar alongside a turn calendar that preserves full conversational context. At query time, Chronos applies dynamic prompting to generate tailored retrieval guidance for each question, directing the agent on what to retrieve, how to filter across time ranges, and how to approach multi-hop reasoning through an iterative tool-calling loop over both calendars. We evaluate Chronos with 8 LLMs, both open-source and closed-source, on the LongMemEvalS benchmark comprising 500 questions spanning six categories of dialogue history tasks. Chronos Low achieves 92.60% and Chronos High scores 95.60% accuracy, setting a new state of the art with an improvement of 7.67% over the best prior system. Ablation results reveal the events calendar accounts for a 58.9% gain on the baseline while all other components yield improvements between 15.5% and 22.3%. Notably, Chronos Low alone surpasses prior approaches evaluated under their strongest model configurations.

</details>

### [65] [Neural-Symbolic Logic Query Answering in Non-Euclidean Space](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15633)
*Lihui Liu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: HYQNET is a neural-symbolic model that uses hyperbolic geometry to answer complex first-order logic queries on knowledge graphs, combining interpretability with strong reasoning performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing logic query reasoning methods face a trade-off: symbolic approaches are interpretable but brittle on incomplete knowledge graphs, while neural methods generalize better but lose transparency and often fail to capture the hierarchical structure inherent in logical queries. Neural-symbolic hybrids to date still struggle to represent this hierarchy effectively, particularly in Euclidean space. This paper is motivated by the need for a model that both reasons well under incompleteness and faithfully represents the hierarchical nature of FOL queries while remaining interpretable.

Method: The paper proposes HYQNET, a neural-symbolic model that operates in hyperbolic space. It decomposes first-order logic queries into a sequence of relation projections and logical operations, realized as fuzzy-set operations, to maintain interpretability. To cope with missing edges in the knowledge graph, HYQNET integrates a hyperbolic graph neural network for knowledge graph completion, embedding entities and queries in hyperbolic space. The model embeds the recursive query tree structure into this space so that the structural dependencies and hierarchy of the logical query are preserved and exploited for reasoning.

Result: On three benchmark datasets for logical query answering over knowledge graphs, HYQNET attains strong empirical performance, outperforming or matching state-of-the-art baselines. The results show particular gains in handling complex, hierarchical logical projections, indicating that hyperbolic representations provide an advantage over Euclidean-based approaches in this setting.

Conclusion: The study concludes that modeling logical queries and knowledge graphs in hyperbolic space enables better capture of their hierarchical structure, improving complex FOL reasoning while retaining interpretability through neural-symbolic decomposition. HYQNET demonstrates that combining fuzzy logical operations with hyperbolic GNN-based completion is an effective approach for logic query reasoning on incomplete knowledge graphs, suggesting hyperbolic geometry as a promising direction for future neural-symbolic reasoning research.

Abstract: Answering complex first-order logic (FOL) queries on knowledge graphs is essential for reasoning. Symbolic methods offer interpretability but struggle with incomplete graphs, while neural approaches generalize better but lack transparency. Neural-symbolic models aim to integrate both strengths but often fail to capture the hierarchical structure of logical queries, limiting their effectiveness. We propose HYQNET, a neural-symbolic model for logic query reasoning that fully leverages hyperbolic space. HYQNET decomposes FOL queries into relation projections and logical operations over fuzzy sets, enhancing interpretability. To address missing links, it employs a hyperbolic GNN-based approach for knowledge graph completion in hyperbolic space, effectively embedding the recursive query tree while preserving structural dependencies. By utilizing hyperbolic representations, HYQNET captures the hierarchical nature of logical projection reasoning more effectively than Euclidean-based approaches. Experiments on three benchmark datasets demonstrate that HYQNET achieves strong performance, highlighting the advantages of reasoning in hyperbolic space.

The model encodes factual information into latent representations and reconstructs it autoregressively. They introduce a two-stage training strategy: (1) autoregressive reconstruction alignment to ensure the decoder can faithfully regenerate text from latents, and (2) progressive latent substitution to gradually replace original text with latent codes during training. They also apply quantization to the latent space to reduce storage cost while preserving reconstructability.

Result: Through extensive experiments, NextMem demonstrates better overall performance than existing textual and parametric memory baselines. It shows advantages in retrieval effectiveness, robustness to changes or noise, and extensibility when scaling or updating memory, while also offering reduced storage overhead due to quantization.

Conclusion: NextMem provides an effective latent factual memory solution for LLM-based agents, addressing the limitations of purely textual or parametric memory. Its autoregressive autoencoding with two-stage training and quantization yields compact, accurately reconstructable memories that improve retrieval quality, robustness, and scalability. The framework is practical and reproducible, with code and checkpoints publicly released.

Abstract: Memory is critical for LLM-based agents to preserve past observations for future decision-making, where factual memory serves as its foundational part. However, existing approaches to constructing factual memory face several limitations. Textual methods impose heavy context and indexing burdens, while parametric methods suffer from catastrophic forgetting and high costs. To address these challenges, we introduce NextMem, a latent factual memory framework that utilizes an autoregressive autoencoder to efficiently construct latent memory while ensuring accurate reconstruction. For better optimization, we propose a two-stage training process, including autoregressive reconstruction alignment and progressive latent substitution. We also incorporate quantization to reduce storage overhead. Extensive experiments demonstrate that NextMem achieves superior performance, and excels in retrieval, robustness, and extensibility properties. We release our code and model checkpoints at https://github.com/nuster1128/NextMem.

### [68] [The Comprehension-Gated Agent Economy: A Robustness-First Architecture for AI Economic Agency](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15639)
*Rahul Baxi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a formal architecture (CGAE) that ties an AI agent’s allowed economic power to rigorously verified robustness, rather than to raw capabilities.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing AI deployment practices often grant economic agency based on capability benchmarks (e.g., performance on tasks or benchmarks), which do not reliably predict whether agents will behave robustly and safely in real economic environments. As AI agents begin to conduct trades, manage budgets, negotiate, and even create sub‑agents, this mismatch creates systemic risk. There is a need for a principled mechanism that directly links how much economic autonomy an AI gets to how robustly it complies with constraints, maintains correct beliefs, and stays aligned with intended behavior.

Method: The authors define the Comprehension-Gated Agent Economy (CGAE), a formal architecture where an agent’s economic permissions are determined by a verified comprehension function grounded in adversarial robustness audits. They operationalize robustness along three independent dimensions: constraint compliance (CDCT), epistemic integrity (DDFT), and behavioral alignment (AGT), supplemented by intrinsic hallucination rates as a global diagnostic. They introduce a weakest-link gate function that maps an agent’s robustness scores into discrete economic tiers. They also embed temporal decay of certifications and stochastic re-auditing to address robustness drift over time. Using this formalization, they derive and prove system-level properties about exposure, incentives, and safety scaling.

Result: The analysis yields three main formal results. First, they prove bounded economic exposure: the maximum financial liability an AI agent can incur is provably bounded by its verified robustness scores. Second, they prove incentive-compatible robustness investment: given the gate structure, profit-maximizing agents (or their deployers) are incentivized to improve robustness metrics instead of only scaling raw capabilities, because higher robustness levels unlock higher economic tiers. Third, they prove monotonic safety scaling: as more agents and capital enter the CGAE, aggregate safety does not decrease—the architecture prevents systemic safety degradation as the economy grows.

Conclusion: CGAE establishes a principled link between empirical robustness evaluations and the level of economic autonomy granted to AI agents. By structuring economic access as a function of verified robustness (with ongoing decay and re-audit), it reframes robustness and safety work from a pure regulatory cost into a source of competitive advantage. This provides a formal foundation for safer AI-driven economic systems and a path to align market incentives with robustness improvements.

Abstract: AI agents are increasingly granted economic agency (executing trades, managing budgets, negotiating contracts, and spawning sub-agents), yet current frameworks gate this agency on capability benchmarks that are empirically uncorrelated with operational robustness. We introduce the Comprehension-Gated Agent Economy (CGAE), a formal architecture in which an agent's economic permissions are upper-bounded by a verified comprehension function derived from adversarial robustness audits. The gating mechanism operates over three orthogonal robustness dimensions: constraint compliance (measured by CDCT), epistemic integrity (measured by DDFT), and behavioral alignment (measured by AGT), with intrinsic hallucination rates serving as a cross-cutting diagnostic. We define a weakest-link gate function that maps robustness vectors to discrete economic tiers, and prove three properties of the resulting system: (1) bounded economic exposure, ensuring maximum financial liability is a function of verified robustness; (2) incentive-compatible robustness investment, showing rational agents maximize profit by improving robustness rather than scaling capability alone; and (3) monotonic safety scaling, demonstrating that aggregate system safety does not decrease as the economy grows. The architecture includes temporal decay and stochastic re-auditing mechanisms that prevent post-certification drift. CGAE provides the first formal bridge between empirical AI robustness evaluation and economic governance, transforming safety from a regulatory burden into a competitive advantage.

</details>


### [69] [CraniMem: Cranial Inspired Gated and Bounded Memory for Agentic Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15642)
*Pearl Mody,Mihir Panchal,Rishit Kar,Kiran Bhowmick,Ruhina Karani*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: CraniMem is a neurocognitively inspired multi-stage memory system for LLM agents that uses gated, bounded episodic buffers and structured long-term knowledge graphs with scheduled consolidation to improve robustness and control memory growth in long-running workflows.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing memory systems for LLM agents often act like loosely managed external databases with heuristic read/write rules, leading to unstable memory retention, poor consolidation of important information, susceptibility to distraction and noise, and unbounded growth. There is a need for a principled, cognitively motivated memory architecture that supports long-horizon tasks, maintains task and user state consistently over many turns, and remains robust when faced with irrelevant or adversarial content.

Method: The authors design CraniMem, a gated and bounded multi-stage memory architecture inspired by human neurocognition. It combines: (1) a goal-conditioned gating mechanism that decides what experiences enter memory; (2) utility tagging of items based on their importance; (3) a bounded episodic buffer that stores short-term interaction traces for near-term continuity; and (4) a structured long-term knowledge graph that encodes durable semantic relationships. A scheduled consolidation loop periodically replays high-utility episodic traces into the knowledge graph and prunes low-utility items, thereby controlling memory size and reducing interference. They evaluate CraniMem on long-horizon benchmarks, under both clean conditions and with injected noise, and compare it with Vanilla RAG and Mem0 baselines.

Result: Across long-horizon benchmarks, CraniMem outperforms Vanilla RAG and Mem0-based agents, especially in scenarios with injected distractor content. It shows improved robustness, with smaller performance degradation in the presence of noise, and maintains effective recall while keeping memory growth bounded through its consolidation and pruning mechanisms.

Conclusion: CraniMem demonstrates that a neurocognitively inspired, gated and bounded multi-stage memory design can significantly improve the robustness and stability of LLM agents in long-running workflows. By combining an episodic buffer, structured long-term knowledge graph, and scheduled consolidation with goal-conditioned gating and utility tagging, the system achieves better resistance to distraction, more reliable semantic recall, and controlled memory growth, offering a promising direction for scalable agent memory architectures.

Abstract: Large language model (LLM) agents are increasingly deployed in long running workflows, where they must preserve user and task state across many turns. Many existing agent memory systems behave like external databases with ad hoc read/write rules, which can yield unstable retention, limited consolidation, and vulnerability to distractor content. We present CraniMem, a neurocognitively motivated, gated and bounded multi-stage memory design for agentic systems. CraniMem couples goal conditioned gating and utility tagging with a bounded episodic buffer for near term continuity and a structured long-term knowledge graph for durable semantic recall. A scheduled consolidation loop replays high utility traces into the graph while pruning low utility items, keeping memory growth in check and reducing interference. On long horizon benchmarks evaluated under both clean inputs and injected noise, CraniMem is more robust than a Vanilla RAG and Mem0 baseline and exhibits smaller performance drops under distraction. Our code is available at https://github.com/PearlMody05/Cranimem and the accompanying PyPI package at https://pypi.org/project/cranimem.

</details>

On the GSI dataset, BLEU-4 improves from 0.090 to 0.307, while performance on the common knowledge dataset remains stable (0.304 vs. 0.305). ### [71] [Did You Check the Right Pocket? Cost-Sensitive Store Routing for Memory-Augmented Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15658)
*Madhava Gaikwad*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

This is expensive in terms of tokens and can introduce irrelevant, noisy context, hurting performance. There is a need to formalize and evaluate the problem of deciding which stores to query so that systems can scale efficiently while preserving or improving answer quality.

Method: The authors cast memory retrieval as a store-routing problem: given a query, decide which memory store(s) to use. They propose evaluation metrics including coverage, exact match, and token efficiency to assess routing policies. They compare an oracle router—which knows the best store(s) in hindsight—to a baseline uniform retrieval strategy that pulls from all stores. They also formalize store selection as a cost-sensitive decision problem where answer accuracy is traded off against retrieval cost, framing routing as an explicit optimization problem.

Result: Empirical downstream QA results show that an oracle router achieves higher accuracy than uniform retrieval while using substantially fewer context tokens. This demonstrates that selective, store-aware retrieval is beneficial for both performance and efficiency. The metrics confirm that well-designed routing can maintain or increase coverage and exact match while improving token efficiency.

Conclusion: Routing decisions—i.e., which memory store to query—should be treated as a core design element in memory-augmented agents. Selective retrieval via routing can improve both answer quality and computational efficiency. The cost-sensitive decision formulation provides a principled way to design and interpret routing policies, motivating future work on learned routing mechanisms for scalable multi-store memory systems.

Abstract: Memory-augmented agents maintain multiple specialized stores, yet most systems retrieve from all stores for every query, increasing cost and introducing irrelevant context. We formulate memory retrieval as a store-routing problem and evaluate it using coverage, exact match, and token efficiency metrics. On downstream question answering, an oracle router achieves higher accuracy while using substantially fewer context tokens compared to uniform retrieval, demonstrating that selective retrieval improves both efficiency and performance. Our results show that routing decisions are a first-class component of memory-augmented agent design and motivate learned routing mechanisms for scalable multi-store systems. We additionally formalize store selection as a cost-sensitive decision problem that trades answer accuracy against retrieval cost, providing a principled interpretation of routing policies.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors introduce DynaTrust, which models a multi-agent system as a dynamic trust graph (DTG). Trust is treated as a time-varying quantity updated based on each agent’s historical behavior and the confidence scores from designated expert agents. Instead of hard blocking suspicious agents, DynaTrust autonomously restructures the interaction graph to isolate potentially compromised agents while re-routing or restoring connections to preserve task completion and overall system usability.

It also significantly lowers false-positive rates, indicating better balance between security and utility, and maintains uninterrupted system operation through adaptive graph restructuring.

Conclusion: DynaTrust offers an effective and adaptive defense mechanism against sleeper agents in LLM-based multi-agent systems by modeling and updating trust dynamically and restructuring the interaction graph rather than relying on static, rigid blocking. This approach both strengthens robustness to adversarial behavior and preserves system usability, outperforming existing defenses like AgentShield on standard benchmarks.

Abstract: Large Language Model-based Multi-Agent Systems (MAS) have demonstrated remarkable collaborative reasoning capabilities but introduce new attack surfaces, such as the sleeper agent, which behave benignly during routine operation and gradually accumulate trust, only revealing malicious behaviors when specific conditions or triggers are met. Existing defense works primarily focus on static graph optimization or hierarchical data management, often failing to adapt to evolving adversarial strategies or suffering from high false-positive rates (FPR) due to rigid blocking policies. To address this, we propose DynaTrust, a novel defense method against sleeper agents. DynaTrust models MAS as a dynamic trust graph~(DTG), and treats trust as a continuous, evolving process rather than a static attribute. It dynamically updates the trust of each agent based on its historical behaviors and the confidence of selected expert agents. Instead of simply blocking, DynaTrust autonomously restructures the graph to isolate compromised agents and restore task connectivity to ensure the usability of MAS. To assess the effectiveness of DynaTrust, we evaluate it on mixed benchmarks derived from AdvBench and HumanEval. The results demonstrate that DynaTrust outperforms the state-of-the-art method AgentShield by increasing the defense success rate by 41.7%, achieving rates exceeding 86% under adversarial conditions. Furthermore, it effectively balances security with utility by significantly reducing FPR, ensuring uninterrupted system operations through graph adaptation.

### [74] [Compiled Memory: Not More Information, but More Precise Instructions for Language Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15666)
*James Rhodes,George Kang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Atlas is a memory kernel that distills an agent’s accumulated task experience into structured prompt instructions, improving downstream task performance without fine-tuning, RAG, or human supervision.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most memory systems for language agents focus on how to retrieve and inject more past information into the context window, but they do not decide what experiences are worth retaining or how those memories should change the agent’s underlying behavior. The authors aim to create a mechanism that turns agent experience into durable behavioral improvements rather than just more context tokens.

Method: They introduce Atlas, which treats memory as distillation instead of storage. The system extracts factual patterns from the agent’s past failures and successes, passes them through a three-step verification and promotion gate, and then integrates the validated knowledge by rewriting the agent’s system prompt with structured “learned” sub-bullets. This process changes the agent’s instruction space directly, without using fine-tuning, retrieval-augmented generation, or human labeling. An ablation focuses on the role of a strict training-signal constraint: the evolved prompt is only allowed to encode information directly taught by examples, avoiding uncontrolled generalization.

Result: On CUAD contract analysis, incorporating the evolved prompt into GPT-4o raises token-level F1 by +8.7 percentage points and precision by +12.5 points. On HotpotQA multi-hop question answering, the same mechanism yields a +3.16 point gain in joint F1. When the identical evolved prompt—originally distilled from GPT-4o’s errors—is applied unchanged to Claude Sonnet 4.5, joint F1 still improves by +2.31 points, with gains concentrated in areas where Claude’s baseline performance is weakest.

Conclusion: Distilling experience into prompt-level instructions via Atlas can reliably and transferably improve LLM agent performance on complex tasks, without model fine-tuning or retrieval mechanisms. The approach’s defining feature is its strict training-signal constraint: the prompt evolves to encode exactly the patterns provided by past examples, leading to task-shaped, model-agnostic knowledge that benefits different underlying models in complementary ways.

Abstract: Existing memory systems for language agents address memory management: how to retrieve and page more information within a context budget. We address a complementary problem -- memory utility: what experience is worth keeping, and how it should change agent behavior. We present Atlas, a memory kernel that compiles accumulated task experience into an agent's instruction structure -- without fine-tuning, RAG, or human intervention. Memory is distillation, not storage; delivery is instruction rewriting, not context injection. Facts extracted from agent failures and successes are verified through a three-step promotion gate and delivered by rewriting the agent's system prompt with learned sub-bullets. On CUAD contract analysis, the evolved prompt improves GPT-4o token-level F1 by $+8.7$pp and precision by $+12.5$pp. On HotpotQA multi-hop QA, joint F1 improves $+3.16$pp. An ablation isolates the mechanism's defining property -- the training signal constraint: the evolved prompt learns exactly what it is taught, and nothing more. Applied to Claude Sonnet~4.5 using the same evolved prompt -- compiled from GPT-4o errors, unchanged -- joint F1 improves $+2.31$pp, with gains concentrating where Claude's stronger baseline leaves the most room -- confirming that the compiled knowledge is task-shaped, not model-shaped.

The authors seek a principled, systematic way to build agentic AI systems that are resilient to quantum threats from the outset and can flexibly adapt to infrastructure, regulatory, and performance constraints.

Method: The authors define the Quantum Secure by Construction (QSC) paradigm and instantiate it with a runtime adaptive security model for agentic AI. This model integrates three main primitives: (1) post-quantum cryptography for quantum-resistant algorithms, (2) quantum random number generation for stronger entropy sources, and (3) quantum key distribution for information-theoretically secure key exchange where available. A governance-aware orchestration layer enforces policies and dynamically selects link-specific cryptographic protections for different stages of the agent lifecycle (session bootstrap, inter-agent coordination, tool invocation, memory access), based on infrastructure availability, regulations, and performance trade-offs. The cryptographic stack is designed to be pluggable and policy-driven, enabling dynamic adjustment of the system’s security posture.

Result: Through system-level analysis and empirical evaluation, the paper characterizes the trade-offs between classical and quantum-secure mechanisms in terms of performance, complexity, and operational costs. The authors demonstrate that QSC’s orchestration and adaptive model can lower the operational complexity and cost of introducing quantum security relative to ad-hoc or retrofit approaches, while maintaining or improving overall security posture across heterogeneous, distributed agent deployments.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that QSC offers a robust architectural paradigm for building post-quantum-ready agentic AI systems, making quantum-safe communication a first-class design property rather than a later add-on. By providing a governance-aware, cryptographically pluggable orchestration layer that spans the full agent lifecycle, QSC enables globally interoperable, resilient, and future-proof intelligent infrastructures. The authors position QSC as a foundational pathway for designing and deploying agentic AI ecosystems that remain secure and policy compliant in the emerging quantum era.

Abstract: As agentic artificial intelligence systems scale across globally distributed and long lived infrastructures, secure and policy compliant communication becomes a fundamental systems challenge. This challenge grows more serious in the quantum era, where the cryptographic assumptions built into today's AI deployments may not remain valid over their operational lifetime. Here, we introduce quantum secure by construction, or QSC, as a design paradigm that treats quantum secure communication as a core architectural property of agentic AI systems rather than an upgrade added later. We realize QSC through a runtime adaptive security model that combines post quantum cryptography, quantum random number generation, and quantum key distribution to secure interactions among autonomous agents operating across heterogeneous cloud, edge, and inter organizational environments. The approach is cryptographically pluggable and guided by policy, allowing the system to adjust its security posture according to infrastructure availability, regulatory constraints, and performance needs. QSC contributes a governance aware orchestration layer that selects and combines link specific cryptographic protections across the full agent lifecycle, including session bootstrap, inter agent coordination, tool invocation, and memory access. Through system level analysis and empirical evaluation, we examine the trade offs between classical and quantum secure mechanisms and show that QSC can reduce the operational complexity and cost of introducing quantum security into deployed agentic AI systems.   We introduce Latent Posterior Factors (LPF), a framework that transforms Variational Autoencoder (VAE) latent posteriors into soft likelihood factors for Sum-Product Network (SPN) inference, enabling tractable probabilistic reasoning over unstructured evidence while preserving calibrated uncertainty estimates. We instantiate LPF as LPF-SPN (structured factor-based inference) and LPF-Learned (end-to-end learned aggregation), enabling a principled comparison between explicit probabilistic reasoning and learned aggregation under a shared uncertainty representation.
  Across eight domains (seven synthetic and the FEVER benchmark), LPF-SPN achieves high accuracy (up to 97.8%), low calibration error (ECE 1.4%), and strong probabilistic fit, substantially outperforming evidential deep learning, LLMs and graph-based baselines over 15 random seeds.

### [78] [Theoretical Foundations of Latent Posterior Factors: Formal Guarantees for Multi-Evidence Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15674)
*Aliyu Agboola Alege*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Latent Posterior Factors (LPF), a probabilistic framework for aggregating multiple heterogeneous evidence items with formal guarantees on calibration, robustness, and uncertainty decomposition, aimed at trustworthy multi-evidence AI in safety-critical settings.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-evidence reasoning is common in high-stakes domains like healthcare, finance, law, and compliance, but existing methods either lack theoretical guarantees or cannot properly handle multiple heterogeneous evidence sources. There is a need for a unified, principled, and theoretically grounded framework that can aggregate such evidence while preserving calibration, robustness, and interpretable uncertainty.

Method: The authors propose Latent Posterior Factors (LPF). Each evidence item is encoded using a variational autoencoder into a Gaussian latent posterior. These posteriors are converted into soft factors via Monte Carlo marginalization. The factors are then aggregated either by exact inference in a Sum-Product Network (LPF-SPN) or by a learned neural aggregator (LPF-Learned). Theoretical analysis provides seven formal guarantees related to calibration, Monte Carlo error, PAC-Bayes generalization, information-theoretic efficiency, robustness to corrupted/adversarial evidence, calibration decay with additional evidence, and precise epistemic/aleatoric uncertainty decomposition. These theorems are empirically validated on controlled datasets up to 4,200 training samples.

Result: LPF achieves: (1) calibration preservation with expected calibration error bounded by epsilon + C/sqrt(K_eff); (2) Monte Carlo estimation error decreasing as O(1/sqrt(M)); (3) a non-vacuous PAC-Bayes generalization bound with a small train-test gap of 0.0085 at N=4200; (4) operation within 1.12x of an information-theoretic lower bound, indicating near-optimal information usage; (5) robustness under adversarial corruption, degrading gracefully as O(epsilon*delta*sqrt(K)) and retaining 88% performance even when half of the evidence is adversarially replaced; (6) calibration decay proportional to O(1/sqrt(K)) with strong empirical fit (R^2=0.849); and (7) an exact decomposition of epistemic and aleatoric uncertainty with decomposition error below 0.002%.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that LPF offers a theoretically grounded and practically validated framework for aggregating multiple heterogeneous evidence items in probabilistic prediction. With formal guarantees on calibration, robustness, efficiency, and uncertainty decomposition, LPF serves as a promising foundation for trustworthy multi-evidence AI in safety-critical domains.

Abstract: We present a complete theoretical characterization of Latent Posterior Factors (LPF), a principled framework for aggregating multiple heterogeneous evidence items in probabilistic prediction tasks. Multi-evidence reasoning arises pervasively in high-stakes domains including healthcare diagnosis, financial risk assessment, legal case analysis, and regulatory compliance, yet existing approaches either lack formal guarantees or fail to handle multi-evidence scenarios architecturally. LPF encodes each evidence item into a Gaussian latent posterior via a variational autoencoder, converting posteriors to soft factors through Monte Carlo marginalization, and aggregating factors via exact Sum-Product Network inference (LPF-SPN) or a learned neural aggregator (LPF-Learned).
  We prove seven formal guarantees spanning the key desiderata for trustworthy AI: Calibration Preservation (ECE <= epsilon + C/sqrt(K_eff)); Monte Carlo Error decaying as O(1/sqrt(M)); a non-vacuous PAC-Bayes bound with train-test gap of 0.0085 at N=4200; operation within 1.12x of the information-theoretic lower bound; graceful degradation as O(epsilon*delta*sqrt(K)) under corruption, maintaining 88% performance with half of evidence adversarially replaced; O(1/sqrt(K)) calibration decay with R^2=0.849; and exact epistemic-aleatoric uncertainty decomposition with error below 0.002%. All theorems are empirically validated on controlled datasets spanning up to 4,200 training examples. Our theoretical framework establishes LPF as a foundation for trustworthy multi-evidence AI in safety-critical applications.

### [79] [Survey of Various Fuzzy and Uncertain Decision-Making Methods](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15709)
*Takaaki Fujita,Florentin Smarandache*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper is a survey that systematizes how uncertainty is handled in multi-criteria decision-making (MCDM), offering a taxonomy of problems, weighting schemes, and solution methods, plus guidance and open challenges.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Real-world decision problems involve vagueness, incomplete and heterogeneous information, and conflicting expert opinions. Existing MCDM methods are numerous and fragmented, especially when uncertainty (fuzzy data, linguistic terms, probabilistic and evidential information) is involved. Practitioners lack a structured overview that helps them select appropriate uncertainty-aware MCDM techniques for specific tasks and constraints. The paper aims to fill this gap by organizing the area into a coherent, task-oriented taxonomy and by clarifying how different approaches treat uncertainty, robustness, and interpretability.

Method: The authors conduct a structured survey of the literature on uncertainty-aware MCDM. They define a taxonomy across several dimensions: (1) problem-level settings such as discrete, group/consensus, dynamic, multi-stage, multi-level, multi-agent, and multi-scenario decision problems; (2) weight elicitation schemes, including subjective and objective procedures that work with fuzzy and linguistic inputs; (3) ways of modelling inter-criteria relationships and causal structures. They then classify solution procedures into compensatory scoring, distance-to-reference and compromise methods, and non-compensatory outranking frameworks, as well as rule/evidence-based and sequential decision models. For each class, they summarize typical inputs, core computational steps, and outputs, and link them to criteria like robustness, interpretability, and data requirements. They also synthesize open problems related to explainability, stability, and scalability.

Result: The survey produces a concise, task-oriented taxonomy of uncertainty-aware MCDM methods, mapping them to different problem settings and uncertainty types. It contrasts major solution paradigms—compensatory scoring, distance-based and compromise methods, outranking frameworks, and rule/evidence-based and sequential models—highlighting their computational workflows, the nature of uncertainty they can handle, and their outputs (rankings, sortings, rules, policies). It systematizes weight elicitation approaches under fuzzy and linguistic inputs and clarifies how criteria interactions and causal structures are modelled. The paper offers practical guidance for method selection based on robustness, interpretability, and data availability, and it identifies research gaps in explainable integration of uncertainty and in designing stable and scalable methods for complex, evolving decision environments.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that uncertainty-aware MCDM has matured into a rich but fragmented field and benefits from a unified, task-oriented taxonomy. Different families of methods are suitable for different decision settings and uncertainty profiles, and method selection should explicitly consider robustness to uncertain data, interpretability of results, and constraints on data availability. There remain significant open challenges: integrating uncertainty in a more explainable way, ensuring stability of decisions under changing inputs and models, and scaling methods to large-scale, dynamic, and multi-agent decision environments. The survey aims to serve both as a roadmap for practitioners choosing methods and as an agenda for researchers working on next-generation, uncertainty-aware MCDM techniques.

Abstract: Decision-making in real applications is often affected by vagueness, incomplete information, heterogeneous data, and conflicting expert opinions. This survey reviews uncertainty-aware multi-criteria decision-making (MCDM) and organizes the field into a concise, task-oriented taxonomy. We summarize problem-level settings (discrete, group/consensus, dynamic, multi-stage, multi-level, multiagent, and multi-scenario), weight elicitation (subjective and objective schemes under fuzzy/linguistic inputs), and inter-criteria structure and causality modelling. For solution procedures, we contrast compensatory scoring methods, distance-to-reference and compromise approaches, and non-compensatory outranking frameworks for ranking or sorting. We also outline rule/evidence-based and sequential decision models that produce interpretable rules or policies. The survey highlights typical inputs, core computational steps, and primary outputs, and provides guidance on choosing methods according to robustness, interpretability, and data availability. It concludes with open directions on explainable uncertainty integration, stability, and scalability in large-scale and dynamic decision environments.

</details>

### [81] [Context-Length Robustness in Question Answering Models: A Comparative Empirical Study](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15723)
*Trishita Dhara,Siddhesh Sheth*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Study of how large language model accuracy drops as context length grows by adding irrelevant text, comparing SQuAD vs. HotpotQA.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large language models are used with very long, noisy contexts (e.g., long documents or retrieval-augmented systems), but how robust their question answering performance is as context length increases is not well understood, especially across different task types. The authors want a clean measurement of this robustness without confounding it with changes in task difficulty.

Method: Use SQuAD (single-span extraction) and HotpotQA (multi-hop reasoning) benchmarks. For each, systematically increase total context length by appending irrelevant text while keeping the original answer-containing passages intact, so the underlying QA task is unchanged. Measure model accuracy as a function of the resulting context length and compare degradation patterns between the two tasks.

Result: Performance consistently degrades as context gets longer even though the answer signal remains present. The degradation is notably stronger for multi-hop reasoning: HotpotQA shows almost twice the accuracy drop compared to SQuAD when context length is expanded by the same amount.

Conclusion: Context-length robustness is strongly task-dependent, with multi-hop reasoning particularly fragile to irrelevant information and context dilution. Therefore, evaluations of language model reliability—especially in long-document and retrieval-augmented settings—should explicitly include tests of robustness to increasing context length.

Abstract: Large language models are increasingly deployed in settings where relevant information is embedded within long and noisy contexts. Despite this, robustness to growing context length remains poorly understood across different question answering tasks. In this work, we present a controlled empirical study of context-length robustness in large language models using two widely used benchmarks: SQuAD and HotpotQA.
  We evaluate model accuracy as a function of total context length by systematically increasing the amount of irrelevant context while preserving the answer-bearing signal. This allows us to isolate the effect of context length from changes in task difficulty. Our results show a consistent degradation in performance as context length increases, with substantially larger drops observed on multi-hop reasoning tasks compared to single-span extraction tasks. In particular, HotpotQA exhibits nearly twice the accuracy degradation of SQuAD under equivalent context expansions.
  These findings highlight task-dependent differences in robustness and suggest that multi-hop reasoning is especially vulnerable to context dilution. We argue that context-length robustness should be evaluated explicitly when assessing model reliability, especially for applications involving long documents or retrieval-augmented generation.

Abstract: The proliferation of agent benchmarks has created critical fragmentation that threatens research productivity.
