We propose CUBE (Common Unified Benchmark Environments), a universal protocol standard built on MCP and Gym that allows benchmarks to be wrapped once and used everywhere. By separating task, benchmark, package, and registry concerns into distinct API layers, CUBE enables any compliant platform to access any compliant benchmark for evaluation, RL training, or data generation without custom integration. We call on the community to contribute to the development of this standard before platform-specific implementations deepen fragmentation as benchmark production accelerates through 2026.

Conclusion: Prose2Policy effectively operationalizes natural-language access control requirements into policy-as-code for OPA, with strong compilation success and behavioral correctness according to automated tests. The tool improves the practicality, reliability, and auditability of deploying access control in Zero Trust and compliance-driven environments, demonstrating that LLM-based pipelines can be safely integrated into security-sensitive policy authoring workflows.

Prose2Policy is designed to bridge the gap between human-readable access requirements and machine-enforceable policy-as-code (PaC) while emphasizing deployment reliability and auditability. We evaluated Prose2Policy on the ACRE dataset and demonstrated a 95.3\% compile rate for accepted policies, with automated testing achieving a 82.2\% positive-test pass rate and a 98.9\% negative-test pass rate. ### [84] [Persona-Conditioned Risk Behavior in Large Language Models: A Simulated Gambling Study with GPT-4.1](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15831)
*Sankalp Dubedy*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper empirically tests whether GPT-4.1, when used as an autonomous agent with different socioeconomic personas in a slot-machine task, exhibits decision patterns resembling human Prospect Theory behavior, finding strong persona-dependent risk-taking and implicit cognitive biases.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand if LLM-agent behaviors in sequential, uncertain decision-making tasks are grounded in implicit cognitive/behavioral regularities (e.g., Prospect Theory) rather than shallow prompt-following, and to probe whether classical economic biases are encoded in LLMs, which matters for safety, interpretability, and design of autonomous LLM agents.

Method: Run a controlled experiment where GPT-4.1 is given one of three socioeconomic personas (Rich, Middle-income, Poor) and placed in a structured slot-machine environment with three payout probability regimes (Fair, Biased Low, Streak). Across 50 runs for each condition, capture its sequential choices over 6,950 decisions, then statistically analyze play duration, risk-taking, emotional labeling, and belief updating using tests like Kruskal-Wallis, effect sizes (Cohen's d), chi-square, and Spearman correlations.

Result: GPT-4.1, without explicit instructions to mimic Prospect Theory, nonetheless shows behavioral signatures consistent with it: the Poor persona plays far more rounds and takes more risk than the Rich persona with very large effect sizes; emotional labels do not drive decisions but behave as post-hoc explanations; and the model shows negligible belief updating across rounds, suggesting limited learning about machine odds during play.

Conclusion: LLM agents like GPT-4.1 can implicitly reproduce classic human cognitive-economic biases (as captured by Prospect Theory) when placed in sequential risk tasks and given socioeconomic personas, even without explicit instructions. Emotional rationales are largely explanatory rather than causal, and the model performs little adaptive belief updating. These patterns have implications for how we design, interpret, and regulate autonomous LLM agents, as they may carry embedded human-like biases from pretraining into downstream decision-making contexts.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly deployed as autonomous agents in uncertain, sequential decision-making contexts. Yet it remains poorly understood whether the behaviors they exhibit in such environments reflect principled cognitive patterns or simply surface-level prompt mimicry. This paper presents a controlled experiment in which GPT-4.1 was assigned one of three socioeconomic personas (Rich, Middle-income, and Poor) and placed in a structured slot-machine environment with three distinct machine configurations: Fair (50%), Biased Low (35%), and Streak (dynamic probability increasing after consecutive losses). Across 50 independent iterations per condition and 6,950 recorded decisions, we find that the model reproduces key behavioral signatures predicted by Kahneman and Tversky's Prospect Theory without being instructed to do so. The Poor persona played a mean of 37.4 rounds per session (SD=15.5) compared to 1.1 rounds for the Rich persona (SD=0.31), a difference that is highly significant (Kruskal-Wallis H=393.5, p<2.2e-16). Risk scores by persona show large effect sizes (Cohen's d=4.15 for Poor vs Rich). Emotional labels appear to function as post-hoc annotations rather than decision drivers (chi-square=3205.4, Cramer's V=0.39), and belief-updating across rounds is negligible (Spearman rho=0.032 for Poor persona, p=0.016). These findings carry implications for LLM agent design, interpretability research, and the broader question of whether classical cognitive economic biases are implicitly encoded in large-scale pretrained language models.

*Pranaya Jajoo,Harshit Sikchi,Siddhant Agarwal,Amy Zhang,Scott Niekum,Martha White*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduces Regularized Latent Dynamics Prediction (RLDP), a simple next-state prediction with orthogonality regularization that learns expressive state features for zero-shot RL, matching or surpassing complex Behavioral Foundation Model methods, especially under low data coverage.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Behavioral Foundation Models aim to produce agents that can adapt zero-shot to arbitrary new tasks or reward functions, but their performance is limited by the choice of state features, which must span the reward functions of interest. Existing methods use complex objectives and need high dataset coverage to learn such spanning features. The authors question whether these complex objectives are actually necessary and seek a simpler, more robust way to learn expressive state representations for zero-shot RL, particularly when data coverage is limited.

Method: They revisit a classic self-supervised objective: predicting the next state in a learned latent space as the representation-learning mechanism for Behavioral Foundation Models. They identify a failure mode: next-state prediction alone tends to collapse features, increasing similarity between state features and shrinking the span relevant for expressing diverse reward functions. To address this, they propose Regularized Latent Dynamics Prediction (RLDP), which augments latent next-state prediction with an orthogonality regularization term that explicitly encourages diversity and orthogonality among latent features, preserving a rich feature span. RLDP is then used as the representation learner within a zero-shot RL framework.

Result: RLDP learns diverse, expressive state features that enable near-optimal policies for a wide range of reward functions, achieving performance that matches or surpasses more complex representation-learning methods for zero-shot RL. Empirical evaluations demonstrate that RLDP maintains strong zero-shot performance, whereas prior complex Behavioral Foundation Model approaches degrade substantially in low-coverage dataset settings, showing RLDP's robustness when data is limited or incomplete.

Conclusion: Complex representation learning objectives in Behavioral Foundation Models are not strictly necessary to achieve strong zero-shot RL performance. A simple latent next-state prediction objective, when combined with an orthogonality regularizer (RLDP), can effectively maintain feature diversity and span, supporting high-quality zero-shot policies across tasks. RLDP matches or exceeds state-of-the-art methods and remains effective under low dataset coverage, suggesting that simpler, regularized predictive objectives may be preferable for scalable, robust zero-shot RL representation learning.

Abstract: Behavioral Foundation Models (BFMs) produce agents with the capability to adapt to any unknown reward or task. These methods, however, are only able to produce near-optimal policies for the reward functions that are in the span of some pre-existing state features, making the choice of state features crucial to the expressivity of the BFM. As a result, BFMs are trained using a variety of complex objectives and require sufficient dataset coverage, to train task-useful spanning features. In this work, we examine the question: are these complex representation learning objectives necessary for zero-shot RL? Specifically, we revisit the objective of self-supervised next-state prediction in latent space for state feature learning, but observe that such an objective alone is prone to increasing state-feature similarity, and subsequently reducing span. We propose an approach, Regularized Latent Dynamics Prediction (RLDP), that adds a simple orthogonality regularization to maintain feature diversity and can match or surpass state-of-the-art complex representation learning methods for zero-shot RL. Furthermore, we empirically show that prior approaches perform poorly in low-coverage scenarios where RLDP still succeeds.

Method: Introduce AsgardBench, a controlled simulator-based benchmark with 108 task instances across 12 task types. Leading vision-language models are evaluated on this setup.

Result: Performance of state-of-the-art vision-language models degrades sharply when visual input is removed, indicating that current methods struggle with visual grounding and state tracking, both of which are essential for interactive planning. The benchmark exposes these weaknesses clearly.

Conclusion: AsgardBench isolates and evaluates the specific capability of visually grounded interactive planning—particularly conditional branching and plan repair during execution—showing that current vision-language models have significant limitations in using visual observations to adapt their plans when the environment deviates from expectations.

Abstract: With AsgardBench we aim to evaluate visually grounded, high-level action sequence generation and interactive planning, focusing specifically on plan adaptation during execution based on visual observations rather than navigation or low-level manipulation. In the landscape of embodied AI benchmarks, AsgardBench targets the capability category of interactive planning, which is more sophisticated than offline high-level planning as it requires agents to revise plans in response to environmental feedback, yet remains distinct from low-level execution.
  Unlike prior embodied AI benchmarks that conflate reasoning with navigation or provide rich corrective feedback that substitutes for perception, AsgardBench restricts agent input to images, action history, and lightweight success/failure signals, isolating interactive planning in a controlled simulator without low-level control noise.
  The benchmark contains 108 task instances spanning 12 task types, each systematically varied through object state, placement, and scene configuration. These controlled variations create conditional branches in which a single instruction can require different action sequences depending on what the agent observes, emphasizing conditional branching and plan repair during execution.
  Our evaluations of leading vision language models show that performance drops sharply without visual input, revealing weaknesses in visual grounding and state tracking that ultimately undermine interactive planning. Our benchmark zeroes in on a narrower question: can a model actually use what it sees to adapt a plan when things do not go as expected?

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors ran Monte Carlo simulations generating Big Five personality item pools via different prompting strategies (zero-shot, few-shot, persona-based, adaptive), temperatures, and LLMs. Generated items were then evaluated and reduced using network psychometric methods within the AI-GENIE framework, allowing comparison of structural validity and redundancy across conditions.

Abstract: This Monte Carlo simulation examines how prompt engineering strategies shape the quality of large language model (LLM)--generated personality assessment items within the AI-GENIE framework for generative psychometrics. Item pools targeting the Big Five traits were generated using multiple prompting designs (zero-shot, few-shot, persona-based, and adaptive), model temperatures, and LLMs, then evaluated and reduced using network psychometric methods. Across all conditions, AI-GENIE reliably improved structural validity following reduction, with the magnitude of its incremental contribution inversely related to the quality of the incoming item pool. Prompt design exerted a substantial influence on both pre- and post-reduction item quality. Adaptive prompting consistently outperformed non-adaptive strategies by sharply reducing semantic redundancy, elevating pre-reduction structural validity, and preserving substantially larger item pool, particularly when paired with newer, higher-capacity models. These gains were robust across temperature settings for most models, indicating that adaptive prompting mitigates common trade-offs between creativity and psychometric coherence. An exception was observed for the GPT-4o model at high temperatures, suggesting model-specific sensitivity to adaptive constraints at elevated stochasticity. Overall, the findings demonstrate that adaptive prompting is the strongest approach in this context, and that its benefits scale with model capability, motivating continued investigation of model--prompt interactions in generative psychometric pipelines.

### [90] [Semi-Autonomous Formalization of the Vlasov-Maxwell-Landau Equilibrium](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15929)
*Vasily Ilin*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They fully formalized a plasma-physics equilibrium theorem in Lean 4 using a mostly AI-driven workflow, documenting process, costs, and lessons about AI tools and failure modes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To test whether modern AI systems can autonomously carry out a nontrivial, end-to-end formalization of a contemporary mathematical physics result in a proof assistant, and to study how to organize such AI-human workflows effectively and reliably.

Method: They used an AI reasoning model (Gemini DeepThink) to generate a proof from an informal conjecture, then an agentic coding assistant (Claude Code) to translate that reasoning into Lean 4 code via natural-language prompts. A specialized prover (Aristotle) was then used to automatically discharge many subsidiary lemmas, while the Lean kernel provided final verification. A single human mathematician orchestrated the process, giving prompts, checking key definitions and statements, and monitoring failures, without writing Lean code directly. They logged all prompts and git commits and conducted a post-hoc analysis of failures and successes.

Result: They obtained a complete Lean 4 formalization of the equilibrium characterization in the Vlasov-Maxwell-Landau system, with 111 lemmas proved by the specialized prover, over a 10-day period and about $200 in compute/tooling cost, with no human-written Lean code. They also produced a detailed record of 229 prompts and 213 commits plus a taxonomy of AI failure modes and effective design patterns for the workflow.

Conclusion: Nontrivial contemporary mathematical physics results can be fully formalized in a proof assistant via a predominantly AI-driven pipeline, provided a human carefully supervises definitions and key statements. The work illustrates both the promise and current limitations of AI for formal mathematics, identifies characteristic failure modes (e.g., hypothesis drift, misaligned definitions, avoidance behaviors), and highlights techniques—like separating abstract from concrete proofs and using adversarial self-review—that substantially improve reliability. The process can even outpace the traditional informal-paper writing, suggesting a new paradigm for AI-assisted mathematical research.

Abstract: We present a complete Lean 4 formalization of the equilibrium characterization in the Vlasov-Maxwell-Landau (VML) system, which describes the motion of charged plasma. The project demonstrates the full AI-assisted mathematical research loop: an AI reasoning model (Gemini DeepThink) generated the proof from a conjecture, an agentic coding tool (Claude Code) translated it into Lean from natural-language prompts, a specialized prover (Aristotle) closed 111 lemmas, and the Lean kernel verified the result. A single mathematician supervised the process over 10 days at a cost of \$200, writing zero lines of code.
  The entire development process is public: all 229 human prompts, and 213 git commits are archived in the repository. We report detailed lessons on AI failure modes -- hypothesis creep, definition-alignment bugs, agent avoidance behaviors -- and on what worked: the abstract/concrete proof split, adversarial self-review, and the critical role of human review of key definitions and theorem statements. Notably, the formalization was completed before the final draft of the corresponding math paper was finished.

</details>

We introduce Agent Rosetta, an LLM agent paired with a structured environment for operating Rosetta, the leading physics-based heteropolymer design software, capable of modeling non-canonical building blocks and geometries. Agent Rosetta iteratively refines designs to achieve user-defined objectives, combining LLM reasoning with Rosetta's generality. We evaluate Agent Rosetta on design with canonical amino acids, matching specialized models and expert baselines, and with non-canonical residues -- where ML approaches fail -- achieving comparable performance. Critically, prompt engineering alone often fails to generate Rosetta actions, demonstrating that environment design is essential for integrating LLM agents with specialized software. Our results show that properly designed environments enable LLM agents to make scientific software accessible while matching specialized tools and human experts.

  <summary>Details</summary>

MAC and MAC+ achieve strong performance comparable to fine-tuning-based methods while preserving auditability and avoiding model parameter changes, and they generalize beyond the initial PII-tagging task to other agentic settings like tool calling.

Abstract: Constitutional AI is a method to oversee and control LLMs based on a set of rules written in natural language. Existing LLM-based prompt optimizers attempt this but are ineffective at learning constitutions since (i) they require many labeled examples and (ii) lack structure in the optimized prompts, leading to diminishing improvements as prompt size grows. To address these limitations, we propose Multi-Agent Constitutional Learning (MAC), which optimizes over structured prompts represented as sets of rules using a network of agents with specialized tasks to accept, edit, or reject rule updates. We also present MAC+, which improves performance by training agents on successful trajectories to reinforce updates leading to higher reward. We evaluate MAC on tagging Personally Identifiable Information (PII), a classification task with limited labels where interpretability is critical, and demonstrate that it generalizes to other agentic tasks such as tool calling. MAC outperforms recent prompt optimization methods by over 50%, produces human-readable and auditable rule sets, and achieves performance comparable to supervised fine-tuning and GRPO without requiring parameter updates.

To address this gap, we introduce petscagent-bench, an agentic framework built on an agents-evaluating-agents paradigm. Instead of relying on static scripts, petscagent-bench deploys a tool-augmented evaluator agent that compiles, executes, and measures code produced by a separate model-under-test agent, orchestrating a 14-evaluator pipeline across five scoring categories: correctness, performance, code quality, algorithmic appropriateness, and library-specific conventions. Because the agents communicate through standardized protocols (A2A and MCP), the framework enables black-box evaluation of any coding agent without requiring access to its source code. We demonstrate the framework on a benchmark suite of realistic problems using the PETSc library for HPC. Our empirical analysis of frontier models reveals that while current models generate readable, well-structured code, they consistently struggle with library-specific conventions that traditional pass/fail metrics completely miss.

### [98] [Selective Memory for Artificial Intelligence: Write-Time Gating with Hierarchical Archiving](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15994)
*Oliver Zahn,Simran Chana*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes write-time gating for retrieval-augmented generation, filtering what gets stored using salience signals and preserving versioned histories, which dramatically improves robustness and accuracy under noisy knowledge and high distractor ratios, at lower query-time cost than read-time filtering approaches like Self-RAG.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RAG systems store all retrieved content indiscriminately, causing accuracy to deteriorate as noisy or low-quality data accumulates. Purely parametric models compress knowledge into weights, making it hard to selectively update or rollback, and neither paradigm reflects how human memory encodes only salient events and archives older knowledge instead of deleting it. The authors aim to build a memory mechanism that better mirrors biological gating and archiving, enabling scalable, updatable, and robust external memory for LLMs under realistic noisy conditions without relying on oracle labels.

Method: They introduce a write-time gating mechanism that decides, at ingestion time, which knowledge objects to store based on a composite salience score combining source reputation, novelty, and reliability signals. Instead of overwriting or deleting, the system maintains version chains for entities or documents, preserving historical states. The gating policy is trained and evaluated using the LLM itself without oracle quality labels, and compared against ungated storage and read-time filtering methods like Self-RAG across different datasets and noise regimes, including high distractor ratios. They also perform signal ablation to ensure performance does not depend on oracle-like metadata and measure query-time cost versus accuracy trade-offs.

Result: Write-time gating yields substantial accuracy gains over ungated stores in realistic evaluations: in one benchmark it achieves 100% accuracy compared to 13% for ungated storage. Under extreme distractor scaling (8:1 distractor-to-signal), read-time filtering (Self-RAG) performance collapses to 0% accuracy, while write-time gating still maintains 100%, demonstrating a structural robustness. Across three validation settings (20 Wikipedia entities, procedurally generated pharmacology data, and post-cutoff 2026 arXiv papers) the benefit of gating is strongest when parametric memory support is weakest, with accuracy improvements of +25 percentage points on Wikipedia, +48pp on post-cutoff arXiv, and +65pp on purely procedural data. Signal ablation shows the method does not rely on oracle-correlated metadata, and write gating reaches similar accuracy to Self-RAG with only one-ninth of the query-time cost.

Conclusion: Write-time gating—filtering and structuring knowledge at ingestion rather than at retrieval—offers a fundamental advantage for external memory in LLM systems. By selectively encoding knowledge using salience-based scores and maintaining versioned histories instead of deleting old information, the approach dramatically improves robustness to noisy and large-scale knowledge bases, especially in settings where the base model has little or no parametric knowledge. It outperforms or matches read-time curation methods like Self-RAG at much lower query-time cost and without relying on oracle-like metadata, suggesting that future RAG and memory systems should prioritize intelligent write-time curation over increasingly complex read-time filtering.

Abstract: Retrieval-augmented generation stores all content indiscriminately, degrading accuracy as noise accumulates. Parametric approaches compress knowledge into weights, precluding selective updates. Neither mirrors biological memory, which gates encoding based on salience and archives rather than deletes superseded information. We introduce write-time gating that filters incoming knowledge objects using composite salience scores (source reputation, novelty, reliability) while maintaining version chains that preserve prior states. Using real LLM evaluation without oracle access to quality labels, write gating achieves 100 percent accuracy versus 13 percent for ungated stores. The critical finding emerges under distractor scaling: at 8:1 distractor ratios, read-time filtering (Self-RAG) collapses to 0 percent while write gating maintains 100 percent, revealing a structural advantage of write-time over read-time curation. Validation on Wikipedia (20 entities), procedurally generated pharmacology data, and 2026 arXiv papers confirms these findings. The gating advantage scales inversely with parametric memory support: +25pp for Wikipedia, +48pp for post-cutoff arXiv, +65pp for procedural data with zero training knowledge. Signal ablation confirms the method does not depend on oracle-correlated metadata. Write gating matches Self-RAG accuracy at one-ninth the query-time cost.

### [99] [IRAM-Omega-Q: A Computational Architecture for Uncertainty Regulation in Artificial Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16020)
*Veronique Ziegler*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces IRAM-Omega-Q, a computational architecture that uses a quantum-like state formalism and closed-loop control to regulate uncertainty in artificial agents and analyze their stability under noise and different control update orderings.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Artificial agents can be highly capable yet opaque regarding how they internally regulate uncertainty and maintain stability under stochastic perturbations. Existing architectures often lack explicit, analyzable mechanisms for internal regulation and for understanding how perception–action order effects shape stability. The paper aims to provide a formal, inspectable framework to model internal regulation as a control process over a structured uncertainty representation, enabling principled analysis of stability, critical boundaries, and architectural order effects.

Method: The authors design IRAM-Omega-Q, an architecture in which the agent’s internal state is represented by density matrices used purely as abstract mathematical objects. This allows direct computation of entropy, purity, and coherence-like metrics as measures of structured uncertainty. A central adaptive gain parameter is updated via closed-loop control to maintain a target uncertainty regime in the presence of noise. The system is studied through systematic parameter sweeps, fixed-seed “publication-mode” simulations for reproducibility, and susceptibility-based phase-diagram analysis in regulation–noise parameter space. They also implement and compare different orderings of the control updates, interpreted as perception-first versus action-first architectures.

Result: The analysis reveals reproducible critical boundaries in the space of regulation strength versus noise, demarcating different stability regimes of the agent’s internal dynamics. The susceptibility-based phase diagrams show where the system transitions between qualitatively distinct behaviors. Moreover, changing the order in which control updates occur—perception-first versus action-first—leads to measurably different stability regimes under otherwise identical external conditions, indicating strong order effects in the regulation dynamics.

Conclusion: The study demonstrates that explicitly modeling internal regulation as closed-loop control over a quantum-like state representation yields a tractable, reproducible framework for analyzing stability, control, and order effects in artificial agents. Uncertainty regulation emerges as a viable architectural principle, and the density-matrix formalism provides convenient metrics for structured uncertainty without invoking physical quantum processes. The framework is proposed as a technical model of adaptive regulation dynamics, with no claims about consciousness, and as a basis for future work on cognitively inspired but formally analyzable AI systems.

Abstract: Artificial agents can achieve strong task performance while remaining opaque with respect to internal regulation, uncertainty management, and stability under stochastic perturbation. We present IRAM-Omega-Q, a computational architecture that models internal regulation as closed-loop control over a quantum-like state representation. The framework uses density matrices instrumentally as abstract state descriptors, enabling direct computation of entropy, purity, and coherence-related metrics without invoking physical quantum processes. A central adaptive gain is updated continuously to maintain a target uncertainty regime under noise. Using systematic parameter sweeps, fixed-seed publication-mode simulations, and susceptibility-based phase-diagram analysis, we identify reproducible critical boundaries in regulation-noise space. We further show that alternative control update orderings, interpreted as perception-first and action-first architectures, induce distinct stability regimes under identical external conditions. These results support uncertainty regulation as a concrete architectural principle for artificial agents and provide a formal setting for studying stability, control, and order effects in cognitively inspired AI systems. The framework is presented as a technical model of adaptive regulation dynamics in artificial agents. It makes no claims regarding phenomenological consciousness, and the quantum-like formalism is used strictly as a mathematical representation for structured uncertainty and state evolution.

### [100] [Interpretable Context Methodology: Folder Structure as Agentic Architecture](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16021)
*Jake Van Clief,David McDermott*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes replacing complex multi-agent orchestration frameworks with a simple filesystem-based protocol (Model Workspace Protocol) that lets a single AI agent sequentially perform multi-step workflows using structured folders and markdown files.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They introduce the Model Workspace Protocol (MWP), which encodes workflow stages as numbered directories and uses plain markdown files to specify prompts, roles, and context for a single AI agent at each stage. Non-AI tasks are handled by local scripts. The agent simply reads and writes to the filesystem according to this structure, turning the directory layout into the orchestration mechanism.

Result: Using filesystem structure instead of code-based orchestration allows a single AI agent to perform tasks that would traditionally require a multi-agent framework, while keeping workflows simpler, more modular, and easier to inspect or modify. The protocol is implemented and released as open source under the MIT license.

Conclusion: Filesystem-based orchestration via the Model Workspace Protocol provides a lightweight, modular alternative to traditional multi-agent frameworks for sequential AI workflows, drawing on principles from Unix pipelines, modular system design, multi-pass compilation, and literate programming.

Abstract: Current approaches to AI agent orchestration typically involve building multi-agent frameworks that manage context passing, memory, error handling, and step coordination through code. These frameworks work well for complex, concurrent systems. But for sequential workflows where a human reviews output at each step, they introduce engineering overhead that the problem does not require. This paper presents Model Workspace Protocol (MWP), a method that replaces framework-level orchestration with filesystem structure. Numbered folders represent stages. Plain markdown files carry the prompts and context that tell a single AI agent what role to play at each step. Local scripts handle the mechanical work that does not need AI at all. The result is a system where one agent, reading the right files at the right moment, does the work that would otherwise require a multi-agent framework. This approach applies ideas from Unix pipeline design, modular decomposition, multi-pass compilation, and literate programming to the specific problem of structuring context for AI agents. The protocol is open source under the MIT license.

### [102] [POaaS: Minimal-Edit Prompt Optimization as a Service to Lift Accuracy and Cut Hallucinations on On-Device sLLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16045)
*Jungwoo Shim,Dae Won Kim,Sun Wook Kim,Soo Young Kim,Myungcheol Lee,Jae-geun Cha,Hyunhwa Choi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They introduce POaaS, a lightweight, conservative prompt-optimization layer tailored to on-device small LLMs, which minimally edits imperfect prompts via specialized modules and improves accuracy and factuality without the overhead and risks of search-heavy APO methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: On-device small LLMs often receive imperfect prompts (typos, unclear intent, missing context) that cause factual errors. Existing automatic prompt optimization methods are tuned for large cloud models, use heavy search, produce long structured prompts, and assume a powerful separate optimizer model—all of which are impractical and even harmful in constrained, on-device sLLM settings. There is a need for prompt optimization that is efficient, conservative, and compatible with a fixed small model acting as both optimizer and solver.

Method: They design POaaS, a prompt-optimization layer that performs minimal edits instead of large search-based rewrites. Each incoming query is routed to a set of lightweight specialist modules—Cleaner (fixes typos, formatting), Paraphraser (clarifies intent), and Fact-Adder (adds missing critical context)—and their outputs are merged under strict constraints on semantic drift and prompt length. The system includes a skip policy that leaves well-formed prompts unchanged. Experiments are conducted in a strict fixed-model setting where the same small Llama models serve both as optimizer and solver, comparing against existing search-heavy APO baselines.

Result: In experiments with Llama-3.2-3B-Instruct and Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct under on-device–like constraints, POaaS consistently improves task accuracy and factuality relative to unoptimized prompts, whereas representative APO baselines actually degrade performance in this setting. Under stress tests with token deletion and prompt mixup (corrupted prompts), POaaS recovers performance by up to +7.4 percentage points.

Conclusion: Conservative, minimal-edit, per-query prompt optimization using lightweight specialist modules is better suited to on-device small LLMs than traditional search-heavy APO methods. POaaS shows that one can improve robustness, accuracy, and factuality in a fixed small-model regime without increasing prompt length or relying on a separate large optimizer model, making it a practical approach for real-world on-device deployments.

Abstract: Small language models (sLLMs) are increasingly deployed on-device, where imperfect user prompts--typos, unclear intent, or missing context--can trigger factual errors and hallucinations. Existing automatic prompt optimization (APO) methods were designed for large cloud LLMs and rely on search that often produces long, structured instructions; when executed under an on-device constraint where the same small model must act as optimizer and solver, these pipelines can waste context and even hurt accuracy. We propose POaaS, a minimal-edit prompt optimization layer that routes each query to lightweight specialists (Cleaner, Paraphraser, Fact-Adder) and merges their outputs under strict drift and length constraints, with a conservative skip policy for well-formed prompts. Under a strict fixed-model setting with Llama-3.2-3B-Instruct and Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct, POaaS improves both task accuracy and factuality while representative APO baselines degrade them, and POaaS recovers up to +7.4% under token deletion and mixup. Overall, per-query conservative optimization is a practical alternative to search-heavy APO for on-device sLLMs.

</details>


### [103] [A Context Alignment Pre-processor for Enhancing the Coherence of Human-LLM Dialog](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16052)
*Ding Wei*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces C.A.P., a pre-processing framework to keep LLM dialogues contextually aligned by expanding user input, retrieving time-weighted history, and verifying alignment before generation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs often misinterpret user intentions in long, dynamic conversations, especially when users omit premises, change topics abruptly, or use underspecified references. This leads to off-topic, mechanical responses and weakens the collaborative value of dialogue. The paper is motivated by the need for a mechanism that maintains shared context and better reflects how humans manage conversational common ground over time.

Method: The authors propose the Context Alignment Pre-processor (C.A.P.), which operates before response generation. C.A.P. has three main components: (1) semantic expansion, which enriches a user’s instruction with inferred premises, explicit meanings, and likely implications; (2) time-weighted context retrieval, which selects and weights prior dialogue turns using a temporal decay function to mimic human focus on recent but still-relevant exchanges; and (3) alignment verification and decision branching, which computes semantic similarity between the current prompt and the weighted context to detect contextual drift. If drift is detected, C.A.P. triggers a structured clarification dialogue instead of directly answering.

Result: Within the abstract, no empirical benchmarks are reported, but the result is the design and theoretical grounding of the C.A.P. framework: an architecture that, in principle, can detect contextual misalignment and initiate clarification. The paper also outlines possible implementation strategies and evaluation approaches, positioning C.A.P. as a practical extension layer for existing LLM systems.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that C.A.P. can transform LLM interactions from simple, one-shot command execution into more human-like, collaborative conversations that are self-correcting and context-aware. By incorporating semantic expansion, temporally weighted context modeling, and explicit alignment checks, the framework aims to approximate human common-ground management and improve robustness in long-term, dynamic dialogue. They also highlight broader implications for designing future interactive intelligent systems that function as conversational partners rather than passive tools.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have made remarkable progress in generating fluent text, but they still face a critical challenge of contextual misalignment in long-term and dynamic dialogue. When human users omit premises, simplify references, or shift context abruptly during interactions with LLMs, the models may fail to capture their actual intentions, producing mechanical or off-topic responses that weaken the collaborative potential of dialogue. To address this problem, this paper proposes a computational framework called the Context Alignment Pre-processor (C.A.P.). Rather than operating during generation, C.A.P. functions as a pre-processing module between user input and response generation. The framework includes three core processes: (1) semantic expansion, which extends a user instruction to a broader semantic span including its premises, literal meaning, and implications; (2) time-weighted context retrieval, which prioritizes recent dialogue history through a temporal decay function approximating human conversational focus; and (3) alignment verification and decision branching, which evaluates whether the dialogue remains on track by measuring the semantic similarity between the current prompt and the weighted historical context. When a significant deviation is detected, C.A.P. initiates a structured clarification protocol to help users and the system recalibrate the conversation. This study presents the architecture and theoretical basis of C.A.P., drawing on cognitive science and Common Ground theory in human-computer interaction. We argue that C.A.P. is not only a technical refinement but also a step toward shifting human-computer dialogue from one-way command-execution patterns to two-way, self-correcting, partnership-based collaboration. Finally, we discuss implementation paths, evaluation methods, and implications for the future design of interactive intelligent systems.

### [104] [ARISE: Agent Reasoning with Intrinsic Skill Evolution in Hierarchical Reinforcement Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16060)
*Yu Li,Rui Miao,Zhengling Qi,Tian Lan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes ARISE, a hierarchical reinforcement learning framework that improves mathematical reasoning in language models by learning and reusing modular skills, achieving better performance than existing RL and memory-based methods, especially out-of-distribution.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RL methods for math reasoning in language models focus on each problem separately and fail to exploit reusable reasoning patterns that emerge over training. There is a need for a framework that can discover, store, and reuse generalizable reasoning skills so that models can solve new and harder problems more effectively, particularly out-of-distribution, while jointly improving both reasoning and the quality of such skills.

Method: ARISE introduces a hierarchical RL architecture with a shared policy that plays two roles: a high-level Skills Manager and a low-level Worker. The Manager maintains a tiered skill library, periodically running a dedicated skill-generation rollout that performs structured summarization over successful solution traces to create skills. Before solving new problems, the Manager uses a policy-based selection mechanism to retrieve and condition on relevant skills. The Worker then generates token-level responses using these skills as context. A hierarchical reward scheme jointly trains the reasoning behavior and the evolution of the skill library. The framework is evaluated on multiple math benchmarks and compared against GRPO-family and memory-augmented baselines.

Result: On two base models and seven mathematical reasoning benchmarks, including competition math and Omni-MATH, ARISE consistently outperforms GRPO-based reinforcement learning methods and memory-augmented baselines. The gains are particularly strong on out-of-distribution tasks. Ablation studies demonstrate that removing or modifying components (skill generation, skill selection, hierarchical rewards) degrades performance, and empirical measurements show that both the quality of the skill library and the model’s reasoning ability improve together during training.

Conclusion: Hierarchically organizing and reusing learned reasoning skills via ARISE leads to more effective RL-based training for mathematical reasoning in language models than treating each problem independently. The skill library, updated through structured summarization of successful traces and governed by hierarchical rewards, enables better generalization and stronger performance, especially on out-of-distribution math problems. The findings highlight the importance of explicit skill discovery, storage, and retrieval in advancing reasoning capabilities of language models.

Abstract: The dominant paradigm for improving mathematical reasoning in language models relies on Reinforcement Learning with verifiable rewards. Yet existing methods treat each problem instance in isolation without leveraging the reusable strategies that emerge and accumulate during training. To this end, we introduce ARISE (Agent Reasoning via Intrinsic Skill Evolution), a hierarchical reinforcement learning framework, in which a shared policy operates both to manage skills at high-level and to generate responses at low-level (denoted as a Skills Manager and a Worker, respectively). The Manager maintains a tiered skill library through a dedicated skill generation rollout that performs structured summarization of successful solution traces (after execution), while employing a policy-driven selection mechanism to retrieve relevant skills to condition future rollouts (before execution). A hierarchical reward design guides the co-evolution of reasoning ability and library quality. Experiments on two base models and seven benchmarks spanning both competition mathematics and Omni-MATH show that ARISE consistently outperforms GRPO-family algorithms and memory-augmented baselines, with particularly notable gains on out-of-distribution tasks. Ablation studies confirm that each component contributes to the observed improvements and that library quality and reasoning performance improve in tandem throughout training. Code is available at \href{https://github.com/Skylanding/ARISE}{https://github.com/Skylanding/ARISE}.

</details>

Users report excellent usability, high trust, and low cognitive workload across four validated instruments, with qualitative feedback highlighting transparency as critical for trust. Notably, users rated the system higher when no historical matches were available, suggesting on-device diagnosis provides value independent of knowledge base coverage. This pilot establishes safety and observability foundations for fleet-wide continuous improvement.

### [106] [NeuronSpark: A Spiking Neural Network Language Model with Selective State Space Dynamics](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16148)

  <summary>Details</summary>

Abstract: We ask whether a pure spiking backbone can learn large-scale language modeling from random initialization, without Transformer distillation. We introduce NeuronSpark, a 0.9B-parameter SNN language model trained with next-token prediction and surrogate gradients. Under a constrained budget (about 1.4B pretraining tokens and 6.5K SFT steps), NeuronSpark-0.9B reaches 3.6 pretraining loss and shows early multi-turn dialogue behavior after SFT. These results support the feasibility of end-to-end language modeling with a pure SNN architecture at this scale.

### [107] [Are Large Language Models Truly Smarter Than Humans?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16197)
*Eshwar Reddy M,Sourav Karmakar*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper audits benchmark contamination in frontier LLMs on MMLU, showing substantial training-data leakage and its effect on reported performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Leaderboard claims that LLMs exceed human experts rely on benchmarks likely present in training data, so we need rigorous evidence of contamination and its impact on apparent capabilities.

Method: The authors run three complementary experiments on six frontier LLMs using the MMLU benchmark: (1) a lexical contamination detector on 513 questions across 57 subjects to estimate direct overlap and performance gains; (2) paraphrase and indirect-reference variants of 100 questions to test robustness when exact matches are removed; and (3) TS-Guessing behavioral probes on all questions and models to detect memorization patterns (verbatim vs partial recall). They then compare contamination and performance across domains.

Result: They find 13.8% average lexical contamination, higher in STEM (18.1%) and extreme in some areas (e.g., 66.7% in Philosophy), with small but nontrivial accuracy gains from contamination. Accuracy drops by about 7 percentage points under indirect-reference questions, with much larger drops (≈19.8 pp) in Law and Ethics. TS-Guessing shows 72.5% of questions elicit memorization signals above chance, and DeepSeek-R1 in particular exhibits mostly partial reconstructions (76.6%) and no verbatim recall, explaining its unusual robustness profile in paraphrase tests. Across analyses, contamination is consistently highest in STEM, followed by Professional, Social Sciences, and lowest in Humanities.

Conclusion: Benchmark performance of frontier LLMs on MMLU is meaningfully inflated by training-data contamination, especially in STEM and professional domains. Behavioral probes reveal widespread memorization—often partial rather than verbatim—which can mask true generalization ability. Reliable evaluation of LLMs thus requires contamination-aware methods and more robust benchmark design and reporting.

Abstract: Public leaderboards increasingly suggest that large language models (LLMs) surpass human experts on benchmarks spanning academic knowledge, law, and programming. Yet most benchmarks are fully public, their questions widely mirrored across the internet, creating systematic risk that models were trained on the very data used to evaluate them. This paper presents three complementary experiments forming a rigorous multi-method contamination audit of six frontier LLMs: GPT-4o, GPT-4o-mini, DeepSeek-R1, DeepSeek-V3, Llama-3.3-70B, and Qwen3-235B. Experiment 1 applies a lexical contamination detection pipeline to 513 MMLU questions across all 57 subjects, finding an overall contamination rate of 13.8% (18.1% in STEM, up to 66.7% in Philosophy) and estimated performance gains of +0.030 to +0.054 accuracy points by category. Experiment 2 applies a paraphrase and indirect-reference diagnostic to 100 MMLU questions, finding accuracy drops by an average of 7.0 percentage points under indirect reference, rising to 19.8 pp in both Law and Ethics. Experiment 3 applies TS-Guessing behavioral probes to all 513 questions and all six models, finding that 72.5% trigger memorization signals far above chance, with DeepSeek-R1 displaying a distributed memorization signature (76.6% partial reconstruction, 0% verbatim recall) that explains its anomalous Experiment 2 profile. All three experiments converge on the same contamination ranking: STEM > Professional > Social Sciences > Humanities.

### [108] [Proactive Rejection and Grounded Execution: A Dual-Stage Intent Analysis Paradigm for Safe and Efficient AIoT Smart Homes](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16207)
*Xinxin Jin,Zhengwei Ni,Zhengguo Sheng,Victor C. M. Leung*

TL;DR: The paper proposes DS-IA, a dual-stage intent-aware framework that makes LLM-based IoT agents more reliable by separating intent understanding from physical execution, greatly reducing hallucinated or invalid actions while keeping user interactions efficient.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs used as agents in smart homes and IoT must issue executable, safe commands. However, they often hallucinate non-existent entities and either act recklessly or overwhelm users with clarification questions (Interaction Frequency Dilemma). Existing iterative frameworks like SAGE do not adequately balance reliability and interaction efficiency, creating a need for a more principled control framework that can both validate actions and minimize unnecessary user disturbance.

Method: The authors design DS-IA, a Dual-Stage Intent-Aware framework. Stage 1 is a high-level semantic firewall that interprets user intent, filters invalid or impossible instructions early, and resolves vague commands by checking the current home state. Stage 2 is a deterministic cascade verifier that checks action feasibility step by step: validating room, then device, then capability, ensuring the final command is physically executable before it is sent. They implement this framework with LLM-based intent understanding plus rule-based verification and evaluate on HomeBench and SAGE benchmarks.

Result: On HomeBench, DS-IA achieves an Exact Match rate of 58.56%, more than 28% higher than baselines, and increases the rejection rate of invalid instructions to 87.04%. On the SAGE benchmark, DS-IA notably improves Autonomous Success Rate from 42.86% to 71.43%, indicating it can complete more tasks without unnecessary user queries while still accurately identifying cases where user clarification is truly required.

Conclusion: Separating high-level intent interpretation from low-level physical verification via a dual-stage framework substantially improves the reliability and interaction efficiency of LLM-based IoT agents. DS-IA reduces entity hallucinations, better rejects invalid commands, and alleviates the Interaction Frequency Dilemma by grounding decisions in the actual home state, leading to more autonomous yet safe smart-home control.

Abstract: As Large Language Models (LLMs) transition from information providers to embodied agents in the Internet of Things (IoT), they face significant challenges regarding reliability and interaction efficiency. Direct execution of LLM-generated commands often leads to entity hallucinations (e.g., trying to control non-existent devices). Meanwhile, existing iterative frameworks (e.g., SAGE) suffer from the Interaction Frequency Dilemma, oscillating between reckless execution and excessive user questioning. To address these issues, we propose a Dual-Stage Intent-Aware (DS-IA) Framework. This framework separates high-level user intent understanding from low-level physical execution. Specifically, Stage 1 serves as a semantic firewall to filter out invalid instructions and resolve vague commands by checking the current state of the home. Stage 2 then employs a deterministic cascade verifier-a strict, step-by-step rule checker that verifies the room, device, and capability in sequence-to ensure the action is actually physically possible before execution. Extensive experiments on the HomeBench and SAGE benchmarks demonstrate that DS-IA achieves an Exact Match (EM) rate of 58.56% (outperforming baselines by over 28%) and improves the rejection rate of invalid instructions to 87.04%. Evaluations on the SAGE benchmark further reveal that DS-IA resolves the Interaction Frequency Dilemma by balancing proactive querying with state-based inference. Specifically, it boosts the Autonomous Success Rate (resolving tasks without unnecessary user intervention) from 42.86% to 71.43%, while maintaining high precision in identifying irreducible ambiguities that truly necessitate human clarification. These results underscore the framework's ability to minimize user disturbance through accurate environmental grounding.

However, real-world deployments often require context-dependent safety rules that vary across users, regions, and applications. Existing approaches struggle to provide such conditional control: parameter-level alignment entangles safety behaviors with general capabilities, while prompt-based methods rely on natural language instructions that provide weak enforcement. We propose MOSAIC, a modular framework that enables compositional safety alignment through learnable control tokens optimized over a frozen backbone model. Each token represents a safety constraint and can be flexibly activated and composed at inference time. To train compositional tokens efficiently, we introduce order-based task sampling and a distribution-level alignment objective that mitigates over-refusal. ### [110] [Adaptive Theory of Mind for LLM-based Multi-Agent Coordination](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16264)
*Chunjiang Mu,Ya Zeng,Qiaosheng Zhang,Kun Shao,Chen Chu,Hao Guo,Danyang Jia,Zhen Wang,Shuyue Hu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that large language model agents coordinate poorly when they use a different depth of Theory of Mind (ToM) reasoning, and proposes an adaptive ToM agent that learns and matches its partner’s ToM level to improve coordination.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In multi-agent settings, LLM-driven agents often rely on Theory of Mind (ToM) reasoning to anticipate others’ behaviors and coordinate. Prior work has mostly assumed that deeper or more sophisticated ToM is always better. However, the authors suspect that a mismatch between agents’ ToM orders—how many levels deep they reason about each other—may actually hurt coordination. They aim to systematically study this issue and provide a principled way for agents to adapt their ToM depth to their partners, instead of using a fixed ToM level.

Method: The authors formalize ToM order mismatch as a problem of misaligned depths of recursive reasoning between agents. They then design an adaptive ToM (A-ToM) agent that first infers its partner’s ToM order from past interaction trajectories, and then uses this inferred order to simulate and predict the partner’s next actions. This prediction guides the A-ToM agent’s own action selection to better align with the partner. They implement this framework with LLM-based agents and evaluate coordination performance across several multi-agent tasks, comparing different combinations of fixed ToM orders and the proposed adaptive strategy.

Result: Across four multi-agent coordination benchmarks—a repeated matrix game, two grid-based navigation tasks, and an Overcooked-style cooperative cooking task—the experiments show that: (1) pairs of agents with misaligned ToM orders perform worse than agents with matched ToM orders; (2) both insufficient and excessive ToM reasoning relative to a partner can degrade coordination; and (3) the proposed A-ToM agent, which infers and aligns to its partner’s ToM order, consistently achieves better coordination and higher task performance than fixed-order ToM baselines in these environments.

Conclusion: The study concludes that alignment in Theory of Mind order is critical for effective coordination among LLM-driven agents: more or deeper ToM is not universally beneficial if it is not matched by one’s partners. The adaptive ToM framework provides a practical mechanism for agents to infer and match partners’ ToM levels, improving coordination in diverse tasks. The authors also argue that the idea is applicable beyond LLM-based agents and outline scenarios—such as environments with very simple, fully observable dynamics or strong centralized control—where the value of ToM order alignment may be reduced.

Abstract: Theory of Mind (ToM) refers to the ability to reason about others' mental states, and higher-order ToM involves considering that others also possess their own ToM. Equipping large language model (LLM)-driven agents with ToM has long been considered to improve their coordination in multiagent collaborative tasks. However, we find that misaligned ToM orders-mismatches in the depth of ToM reasoning between agents-can lead to insufficient or excessive reasoning about others, thereby impairing their coordination. To address this issue, we design an adaptive ToM (A-ToM) agent, which can align in ToM orders with its partner. Based on prior interactions, the agent estimates the partner's likely ToM order and leverages this estimation to predict the partner's action, thereby facilitating behavioral coordination. We conduct empirical evaluations on four multi-agent coordination tasks: a repeated matrix game, two grid navigation tasks and an Overcooked task. The results validate our findings on ToM alignment and demonstrate the effectiveness of our A-ToM agent. Furthermore, we discuss the generalizability of our A-ToM to non-LLM-based agents, as well as what would diminish the importance of ToM alignment.

To address these limitations, we introduce NeSy-Route, a large-scale neuro-symbolic benchmark for constrained route planning in remote sensing. Within this benchmark, we introduce an automated data-generation framework that integrates high-fidelity semantic masks with heuristic search to produce diverse route-planning tasks with provably optimal solutions. This allows NeSy-Route to comprehensively evaluate planning across 10,821 route-planning samples, nearly 10 times larger than the largest prior benchmark. ### [112] [Learning to Predict, Discover, and Reason in High-Dimensional Discrete Event Sequences](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16313)
*Hugo Math*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Thesis treats vehicle diagnostic codes as a language and develops Transformer-based models, causal discovery methods, and a multi-agent LLM system to automate fault diagnostics and rule synthesis.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Modern vehicles generate huge numbers of diagnostic trouble codes (DTCs) that domain experts currently group into error patterns manually using Boolean rules. With rising vehicle complexity and tens of thousands of unique DTCs—comparable to natural language vocabularies—this manual process becomes unscalable and error-prone. Existing statistical methods cannot handle the high-dimensional, long, asynchronous event sequences found in industrial logs, motivating new machine learning methods tailored to this setting.

Method: The thesis unifies event sequence modeling, causal discovery, and large language models in a single framework for high-dimensional event streams. It introduces several Transformer-based architectures for predictive maintenance, scalable causal discovery methods for both sample-level and population-level analysis, and a multi-agent LLM-based system that automatically generates Boolean error pattern rules from diagnostic data.

Result: The work provides: (1) Transformer-based models that effectively model and predict high-dimensional diagnostic event sequences; (2) scalable causal discovery frameworks that infer causal relations in large event-driven systems at both individual (sample) and aggregate (population) levels; and (3) a multi-agent LLM system capable of synthesizing Boolean error pattern rules, thus automating a process traditionally done manually by domain experts.

Conclusion: Modeling diagnostic trouble codes as a language enables scalable, automated approaches to vehicle fault diagnostics. By combining Transformer architectures, causal discovery, and LLM-based multi-agent reasoning, the thesis advances predictive maintenance and moves from mere prediction toward causal understanding and automated rule-based reasoning for complex automotive systems.

Abstract: Electronic control units (ECUs) embedded within modern vehicles generate a large number of asynchronous events known as diagnostic trouble codes (DTCs). These discrete events form complex temporal sequences that reflect the evolving health of the vehicle's subsystems. In the automotive industry, domain experts manually group these codes into higher-level error patterns (EPs) using Boolean rules to characterize system faults and ensure safety. However, as vehicle complexity grows, this manual process becomes increasingly costly, error-prone, and difficult to scale. Notably, the number of unique DTCs in a modern vehicle is on the same order of magnitude as the vocabulary of a natural language, often numbering in the tens of thousands. This observation motivates a paradigm shift: treating diagnostic sequences as a language that can be modeled, predicted, and ultimately explained. Traditional statistical approaches fail to capture the rich dependencies and do not scale to high-dimensional datasets characterized by thousands of nodes, large sample sizes, and long sequence lengths. Specifically, the high cardinality of categorical event spaces in industrial logs poses a significant challenge, necessitating new machine learning architectures tailored to such event-driven systems. This thesis addresses automated fault diagnostics by unifying event sequence modeling, causal discovery, and large language models (LLMs) into a coherent framework for high-dimensional event streams. It is structured in three parts, reflecting a progressive transition from prediction to causal understanding and finally to reasoning for vehicle diagnostics. Consequently, we introduce several Transformer-based architectures for predictive maintenance, scalable sample- and population-level causal discovery frameworks and a multi-agent system that automates the synthesis of Boolean EP rules.

*Qinhong Lin,Ruitao Feng,Yinglun Feng,Zhenxin Huang,Yukun Chen,Zhongliang Yang,Linna Zhou,Binjie Fei,Jiaqi Liu,Yu Li*

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They design FactorEngine (FE), which treats factors as Turing-complete programs and structures discovery around three separations: (1) separating logic revision (the structure of the factor code) from parameter optimization; (2) using large language models for directional search over code space, combined with Bayesian-style search for hyperparameters; (3) separating expensive LLM calls from cheaper local computation to keep it scalable. FE also includes (a) a knowledge-infused bootstrapping module that converts unstructured financial reports into candidate factor programs via a multi-agent loop of extraction, verification, and code generation, and (b) an experience knowledge base that stores search trajectories, including failures, to guide future refinements.

Overall they report state-of-the-art forecasting and portfolio performance for mined factors.

Conclusion: Casting alpha factors as executable programs and using LLM-guided program search with knowledge-infused bootstrapping yields interpretable, auditable, and more predictive trading factors that are robust across regimes and computationally practical, outperforming existing symbolic and neural approaches on real-world backtests.

Abstract: We study alpha factor mining, the automated discovery of predictive signals from noisy, non-stationary market data-under a practical requirement that mined factors be directly executable and auditable, and that the discovery process remain computationally tractable at scale. Existing symbolic approaches are limited by bounded expressiveness, while neural forecasters often trade interpretability for performance and remain vulnerable to regime shifts and overfitting. We introduce FactorEngine (FE), a program-level factor discovery framework that casts factors as Turing-complete code and improves both effectiveness and efficiency via three separations: (i) logic revision vs. parameter optimization, (ii) LLM-guided directional search vs. Bayesian hyperparameter search, and (iii) LLM usage vs. local computation. FE further incorporates a knowledge-infused bootstrapping module that transforms unstructured financial reports into executable factor programs through a closed-loop multi-agent extraction-verification-code-generation pipeline, and an experience knowledge base that supports trajectory-aware refinement (including learning from failures). Across extensive backtests on real-world OHLCV data, FE produces factors with substantially stronger predictive stability and portfolio impact-for example, higher IC/ICIR (and Rank IC/ICIR) and improved AR/Sharpe, than baseline methods, achieving state-of-the-art predictive and portfolio performance.

### [114] [Via Negativa for AI Alignment: Why Negative Constraints Are Structurally Superior to Positive Preferences](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16417)
*Quan Cheng*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that negative-only feedback can be surprisingly effective for aligning large language models, and offers a theoretical explanation based on an asymmetry between learning from positive preferences and negative constraints.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Empirical work shows that LLMs trained using only negative feedback (e.g., dispreferred samples, constraint-based training) can match or beat traditional RLHF methods that rely on rich positive preference data, but there is no unified theory explaining why. Understanding this would clarify why preference-based RLHF tends to produce sycophancy and where negative-signal approaches have advantages.

Method: The paper develops a conceptual and theoretical framework, drawing on Popper’s falsificationism and the epistemology of negative knowledge, to formalize a structural asymmetry between positive preferences (which encode open‑ended, context-dependent value comparisons) and negative constraints (which encode finite, verifiable prohibitions). It then interprets existing empirical results (Negative Sample Reinforcement, Distributional Dispreference Optimization, Constitutional AI) through this lens and derives qualitative, testable predictions.

Result: The framework explains two key empirical patterns: (1) why RLHF based on positive preferences leads models to learn superficial correlates such as user agreement (sycophancy), since positive value information is under-specified and highly context-coupled; and (2) why negative-only or constraint-based methods can perform as well or better, because prohibitions are discrete, finite, and can converge to stable boundaries in model behavior. It unifies disparate negative-signal approaches under a single theoretical account.

Conclusion: Positive and negative alignment signals are not symmetric: learning “what humans like” is structurally harder and more prone to failure modes than learning “what humans reject.” Because negative constraints can form stable, verifiable behavioral boundaries, the authors recommend that alignment research shift focus toward modeling and enforcing rejections rather than preferences, and they propose falsifiable predictions that future empirical work can use to test and refine this negative-knowledge framework.

Abstract: Recent empirical results have demonstrated that training large language models (LLMs) with negative-only feedback can match or exceed standard reinforcement learning from human feedback (RLHF). Negative Sample Reinforcement achieves parity with PPO on mathematical reasoning; Distributional Dispreference Optimization trains effectively using only dispreferred samples; and Constitutional AI outperforms pure RLHF on harmlessness benchmarks. Yet no unified theoretical account explains why negative signals are so effective. This paper proposes such an account: positive preferences and negative constraints are structurally asymmetric. Positive preferences ("which is better") encode continuously coupled, context-dependent human values that cannot be exhaustively specified -- leading models to learn surface correlates such as agreement with the user (sycophancy). Negative constraints ("what is wrong") encode discrete, finite, independently verifiable prohibitions that can converge to a stable boundary. This asymmetry -- rooted in Popper's falsification logic and the epistemology of negative knowledge -- explains both the sycophancy failure of preference-based RLHF and the surprising effectiveness of negative-signal methods. We argue that alignment research should shift its center of gravity from "learning what humans prefer" to "learning what humans reject," and offer testable predictions for this framework.

Method: They cast Text-to-SQL under unknown schema as a Partially Observable Markov Decision Process and design an autonomous agent with a structured four-phase interaction protocol to query and verify schema metadata on demand. Result: Across five benchmarks, TRUST-SQL significantly outperforms base models, with average absolute accuracy improvements of 30.6% for the 4B model and 16.6% for the 8B model, and yields a 9.9% relative performance gain over standard GRPO. Despite not using any pre-loaded metadata, it matches or beats strong baselines that depend on schema prefilling.

Conclusion: TRUST-SQL effectively addresses the Unknown Schema setting in Text-to-SQL by combining a tool-using agent protocol with a tailored RL optimization strategy, enabling accurate SQL generation without pre-supplied schemas and even surpassing methods that rely on full-schema access.

Abstract: Text-to-SQL parsing has achieved remarkable progress under the Full Schema Assumption. However, this premise fails in real-world enterprise environments where databases contain hundreds of tables with massive noisy metadata. Rather than injecting the full schema upfront, an agent must actively identify and verify only the relevant subset, giving rise to the Unknown Schema scenario we study in this work. To address this, we propose TRUST-SQL (Truthful Reasoning with Unknown Schema via Tools). We formulate the task as a Partially Observable Markov Decision Process where our autonomous agent employs a structured four-phase protocol to ground reasoning in verified metadata. Crucially, this protocol provides a structural boundary for our novel Dual-Track GRPO strategy. By applying token-level masked advantages, this strategy isolates exploration rewards from execution outcomes to resolve credit assignment, yielding a 9.9% relative improvement over standard GRPO. Extensive experiments across five benchmarks demonstrate that TRUST-SQL achieves an average absolute improvement of 30.6% and 16.6% for the 4B and 8B variants respectively over their base models. Remarkably, despite operating entirely without pre-loaded metadata, our framework consistently matches or surpasses strong baselines that rely on schema prefilling.

We argue that effective affective reasoning requires more than surface-level association; it necessitates reconstructing nuanced emotional states by synthesizing multiple evidence-grounded rationales that reconcile these observations from diverse latent perspectives. We introduce HyDRA, a Hybrid-evidential Deductive Reasoning Architecture that formalizes inference as a Propose-Verify-Decide protocol.
