### [120] [Breaking the Chain: A Causal Analysis of LLM Faithfulness to Intermediate Structures](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16475)
*Oleg Somov,Mikhail Chaichuk,Mikhail Seleznyov,Alexander Panchenko,Elena Tutubalina*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies whether schema-guided reasoning pipelines in LLMs (that use intermediate structures like rubrics or checklists) actually causally determine final model decisions or just correlate with them. It proposes a causal evaluation protocol using tasks where each intermediate structure deterministically implies a unique correct answer, enabling precise tests via controlled edits to the structure. Experiments on eight models and three benchmarks show that models often fail to update their answers after the intermediate structure is perturbed, despite appearing self-consistent when structures are unedited. When the mapping from structure to answer is offloaded to an external deterministic tool, this inconsistency largely vanishes, and prompts that tell the model to prioritize the structure over the input do not fix the problem. The paper concludes that intermediate structures act more like influential context than reliable causal mediators in these pipelines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Schema-guided reasoning has become a popular way to increase LLM reliability: instead of directly outputting answers, models are asked to produce interpretable intermediate structures (e.g., rubrics, checklists, verification steps) that are supposed to govern the final decision. This is often assumed to improve faithfulness and interpretability because the final answer is expected to be derived from these structures. However, it is unclear whether these structures actually causally determine the model’s output or are merely a parallel artifact of the same latent reasoning. Without a way to test causal dependence, we cannot trust these pipelines for safety-critical or interpretability-sensitive use cases. The paper is motivated by this gap: providing a concrete, measurable protocol to test whether changes in intermediate structures causally drive changes in final outputs.

Method: The authors design a causal evaluation protocol centered on tasks where there exists a known deterministic mapping from intermediate structures to final decisions. They select or construct benchmarks where, given a particular schema (e.g., a scored rubric, a filled-out checklist, or structured verification data), there is a unique correct label or answer that can be mechanically computed. The protocol proceeds by first having the LLM generate intermediate structures and corresponding decisions, verifying apparent self-consistency. Then, the authors intervene on the intermediate structures via controlled edits (such as flipping checklist items or scores) that, under the deterministic mapping, should change the correct final output. They then prompt the same model to produce a decision conditioned on the edited structure, measuring whether the output changes in the expected way. They repeat this for eight different LLMs across three benchmarks. Additionally, they compare this to a variant where the final decision is not made by the LLM itself but by an external deterministic tool that maps structures to answers, and they test prompts that explicitly instruct the model to base its decision primarily on the intermediate structure rather than the original input.

Result: Empirically, across eight models and three benchmarks, the authors find that models are generally self-consistent when no interventions are made: the intermediate structure and the answer appear coherent with each other. However, under causal interventions where the intermediate structure is edited in a way that should force a change in the correct answer, models fail to update their predictions in a substantial fraction of cases—up to 60%. This indicates a weak causal link from intermediate structure to final decision: the model often continues to rely on its initial latent judgment or the raw input rather than the altered schema. When the derivation of the final decision from the intermediate structure is delegated to an external tool that deterministically implements the mapping, this fragility largely disappears: the system becomes reliably responsive to edits in the structure. Attempts to fix the problem by re-prompting the model to “trust” or prioritize the intermediate structure over original input do not materially reduce the inconsistency. These results collectively show that, as currently used, schema-guided intermediate structures do not robustly control model outputs.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that intermediate structures in schema-guided reasoning pipelines typically function as influential context rather than stable causal mediators of LLM decisions. While they can improve apparent interpretability and sometimes correlate with the final answer, the causal link is fragile: perturbing the structure does not reliably change the output. Consequently, these pipelines should not be assumed to provide faithful explanations or guarantees about how decisions are made, especially in high-stakes applications. To obtain robust causal control, it is more effective to use external tools that deterministically map intermediate structures to outcomes, treating the LLM as a generator of candidate structures rather than as the final decision-maker. The findings call for rethinking how interpretability and faithfulness are evaluated in LLM workflows, emphasizing explicit causal tests over surface-level self-consistency.

Abstract: Schema-guided reasoning pipelines ask LLMs to produce explicit intermediate structures -- rubrics, checklists, verification queries -- before committing to a final decision. But do these structures causally determine the output, or merely accompany it? We introduce a causal evaluation protocol that makes this directly measurable: by selecting tasks where a deterministic function maps intermediate structures to decisions, every controlled edit implies a unique correct output. Across eight models and three benchmarks, models appear self-consistent with their own intermediate structures but fail to update predictions after intervention in up to 60% of cases -- revealing that apparent faithfulness is fragile once the intermediate structure changes. When derivation of the final decision from the structure is delegated to an external tool, this fragility largely disappears; however, prompts which ask to prioritize the intermediate structure over the original input do not materially close the gap. Overall, intermediate structures in schema-guided pipelines function as influential context rather than stable causal mediators.

</details>

Extensive experiments on our newly released multi-modal expressway benchmark demonstrate that ExpressMind comprehensively outperforms existing baselines in event detection, safety response generation, and complex traffic analysis. ### [122] [Exploring different approaches to customize language models for domain-specific text-to-code generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16526)
*Luís Freire,Fernanda A. Andaló,Nicki Skafte Detlefsen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how to adapt small open-source language models for domain-specific Python code generation using synthetic datasets, comparing prompting (few-shot, RAG) to LoRA fine-tuning, and finds LoRA yields the best accuracy and domain alignment.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large proprietary LLMs generate strong code but are costly and often closed, while smaller open-source models are cheaper yet weaker, especially in niche domains that rely on specific libraries and workflows. There is a need for practical, cost-effective methods to specialize these smaller models for particular programming domains without massive real data, motivating an evaluation of synthetic training data and different adaptation strategies.

Method: The authors build synthetic programming-exercise datasets in three Python-related domains: general Python, Scikit-learn ML workflows, and OpenCV computer vision tasks. They then adapt smaller language models using three strategies: (1) few-shot prompting with in-context examples, (2) retrieval-augmented generation that supplies relevant domain examples or snippets at inference time, and (3) parameter-efficient fine-tuning via Low-Rank Adaptation (LoRA), which updates a small number of additional parameters. They evaluate outputs with standard benchmark-style accuracy metrics and similarity-based measures that quantify how well generated code matches domain-specific patterns and APIs.

Result: Prompting-based methods (few-shot and RAG) improve how domain-appropriate the generated code is, and they do so without training the model, but they only modestly affect traditional benchmark accuracy. In contrast, LoRA fine-tuning produces consistent and larger gains in both benchmark accuracy and domain-specific alignment across most of the evaluated tasks and domains.

Conclusion: Prompting techniques like few-shot and RAG are flexible and inexpensive ways to steer smaller LLMs toward domain-specific code but offer limited improvements on strict accuracy metrics. LoRA-based fine-tuning, while requiring additional training compute and setup, delivers more substantial and reliable gains in both correctness and domain adherence. Practitioners choosing how to specialize small models for programming tasks must weigh the trade-offs between cost, flexibility, and performance, with LoRA emerging as the most effective approach when higher accuracy is required.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have demonstrated strong capabilities in generating executable code from natural language descriptions. However, general-purpose models often struggle in specialized programming contexts where domain-specific libraries, APIs, or conventions must be used. Customizing smaller open-source models offers a cost-effective alternative to relying on large proprietary systems. In this work, we investigate how smaller language models can be adapted for domain-specific code generation using synthetic datasets. We construct datasets of programming exercises across three domains within the Python ecosystem: general Python programming, Scikit-learn machine learning workflows, and OpenCV-based computer vision tasks. Using these datasets, we evaluate three customization strategies: few-shot prompting, retrieval-augmented generation (RAG), and parameter-efficient fine-tuning using Low-Rank Adaptation (LoRA). Performance is evaluated using both benchmark-based metrics and similarity-based metrics that measure alignment with domain-specific code. Our results show that prompting-based approaches such as few-shot learning and RAG can improve domain relevance in a cost-effective manner, although their impact on benchmark accuracy is limited. In contrast, LoRA-based fine-tuning consistently achieves higher accuracy and stronger domain alignment across most tasks. These findings highlight practical trade-offs between flexibility, computational cost, and performance when adapting smaller language models for specialized programming tasks.

</details>


### [123] [Designing for Disagreement: Front-End Guardrails for Assistance Allocation in LLM-Enabled Robots](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16537)
*Carmen Ng*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a procedural design pattern for how LLM-enabled robots should prioritize scarce assistance among multiple users in social settings, emphasizing transparent, constrained, and contestable allocation rules.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In social environments where LLM-enabled robots allocate limited assistance (e.g., only one person can be helped at a time), there are pluralistic human values about who ought to be prioritized, and LLM behavior can vary unpredictably with prompts and contexts. Existing user-facing guardrails for real-time, multi-user allocation are vague, raising risks of opaque or unfair prioritization and hidden value assumptions.

Method: The authors introduce a design pattern called "bounded calibration with contestability." This pattern: (i) restricts prioritization decisions to a pre-approved set of admissible allocation modes determined through governance processes; (ii) ensures the currently active mode is clearly communicated to users in terms that matter at the moment someone is deferred; and (iii) offers a way for users to contest specific outcomes without reopening the entire global rule set. They ground the pattern in a vignette involving a robot in a public concourse and sketch an evaluation agenda rather than conducting a full empirical study.

Result: Conceptually, the paper shows that treating value pluralism and LLM uncertainty as persistent conditions leads to a middle-ground design: neither hidden default rules nor fully customizable value sliders for users under time pressure. The bounded calibration with contestability pattern structures how prioritization logic is constrained, displayed, and challenged. The authors map out key evaluation dimensions—legibility, procedural legitimacy, and actionability—and identify potential failure modes such as automation bias and unequal access to contest mechanisms.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that for LLM-enabled robots managing scarce, real-time assistance, procedural safeguards should focus on transparent, governance-bounded prioritization modes and accessible, outcome-level contestation. This approach can mitigate hidden value biases and reduce the cognitive and moral burden on users, while acknowledging persistent disagreement and LLM unpredictability. Future work should experimentally assess how well such patterns support user understanding, trust, and fair use of contest channels, and how they interact with human tendencies like automation bias.

Abstract: LLM-enabled robots prioritizing scarce assistance in social settings face pluralistic values and LLM behavioral variability: reasonable people can disagree about who is helped first, while LLM-mediated interaction policies vary across prompts, contexts, and groups in ways that are difficult to anticipate or verify at contact point. Yet user-facing guardrails for real-time, multi-user assistance allocation remain under-specified. We propose bounded calibration with contestability, a procedural front-end pattern that (i) constrains prioritization to a governance-approved menu of admissible modes, (ii) keeps the active mode legible in interaction-relevant terms at the point of deferral, and (iii) provides an outcome-specific contest pathway without renegotiating the global rule. Treating pluralism and LLM uncertainty as standing conditions, the pattern avoids both silent defaults that hide implicit value skews and wide-open user-configurable "value settings" that shift burden under time pressure. We illustrate the pattern with a public-concourse robot vignette and outline an evaluation agenda centered on legibility, procedural legitimacy, and actionability, including risks of automation bias and uneven usability of contest channels.

### [124] [BenchPreS: A Benchmark for Context-Aware Personalized Preference Selectivity of Persistent-Memory LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16557)
*Sangyeon Yoon,Sunkyoung Kim,Hyesoo Hong,Wonje Jeung,Yongil Kim,Wooseok Seo,Heuiyeen Yeen,Albert No*

TL;DR: The paper introduces BenchPreS, a benchmark to test whether LLMs apply stored user preferences appropriately or suppress them when context makes them unsuitable, revealing that current LLMs over-generalize preferences as always-on rules.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs gain persistent memory for user personalization, there is a growing risk that learned preferences will be inappropriately applied in contexts governed by social or institutional norms (e.g., workplace, formal communication), potentially causing norm violations or undesired behavior. There is a need to systematically evaluate whether models can treat preferences as context-dependent signals rather than rigid rules.

Method: The authors design BenchPreS, a benchmark consisting of diverse communication scenarios where some user preferences should be applied while others should be selectively suppressed based on context. They define two key metrics: Misapplication Rate (MR), measuring how often a model wrongly applies a preference when it should not, and Appropriate Application Rate (AAR), measuring how often it correctly applies a preference when it should. They then test various LLMs, including frontier models, under different prompting and reasoning settings to assess context-sensitive preference application.

Result: Experiments show that even advanced LLMs perform poorly in distinguishing when to apply or withhold user preferences. Models that are better at following preferences overall tend to over-apply them, leading to higher misapplication rates. Enhancing reasoning via chain-of-thought or adding prompt-based defenses mitigates but does not eliminate inappropriate preference application.

Conclusion: Current LLMs largely treat stored user preferences as globally applicable constraints rather than context-conditioned norms, resulting in frequent over-application in settings where preferences should be suppressed. The findings highlight a key limitation of current personalization approaches and suggest that future work must focus on more nuanced, norm-aware preference handling and memory mechanisms that can reason about when not to apply a preference.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) increasingly store user preferences in persistent memory to support personalization across interactions. However, in third-party communication settings governed by social and institutional norms, some user preferences may be inappropriate to apply. We introduce BenchPreS, which evaluates whether memory-based user preferences are appropriately applied or suppressed across communication contexts. Using two complementary metrics, Misapplication Rate (MR) and Appropriate Application Rate (AAR), we find even frontier LLMs struggle to apply preferences in a context-sensitive manner. Models with stronger preference adherence exhibit higher rates of over-application, and neither reasoning capability nor prompt-based defenses fully resolve this issue. These results suggest current LLMs treat personalized preferences as globally enforceable rules rather than as context-dependent normative signals.

  <summary>Details</summary>

This mismatch means model predictions can quickly become outdated, especially when facts change erratically or periodically. There is a need to systematically measure and understand how well VLMs handle time-sensitive knowledge and how effectively their knowledge can be updated across both text and visual inputs.

Method: The authors design V-DyKnow, a Visual Dynamic Knowledge benchmark focusing on time-sensitive factual knowledge. Result: Experiments show that VLMs often produce outdated facts that mirror the older data snapshots seen during pre-training. The factual reliability of models decreases when moving from textual prompts to visual stimuli, even when the models correctly recognize entities in images. Furthermore, existing alignment and updating techniques fail to reliably and consistently refresh the models’ knowledge across modalities.

Conclusion: V-DyKnow reveals fundamental limitations in how current vision-language models acquire, store, and update time-sensitive knowledge across text and image modalities. The benchmark demonstrates that VLMs are prone to outdated predictions and that current alignment, editing, and RAG-based approaches are insufficient for robust, cross-modal knowledge updates. The authors provide the benchmark, code, and evaluation data to support further research on dynamic, temporally-aware vision-language systems.

Abstract: Vision-Language Models (VLMs) are trained on data snapshots of documents, including images and texts. Their training data and evaluation benchmarks are typically static, implicitly treating factual knowledge as time-invariant. However, real-world facts are intrinsically time-sensitive and subject to erratic and periodic changes, causing model predictions to become outdated. We present V-DyKnow, a Visual Dynamic Knowledge benchmark for evaluating time-sensitive factual knowledge in VLMs. Using V-DyKnow, we benchmark closed- and open-source VLMs and analyze a) the reliability (correctness and consistency) of model responses across modalities and input perturbations; b) the efficacy of knowledge editing and multi-modal RAG methods for knowledge updates across modalities; and c) the sources of outdated predictions, through data and mechanistic analysis. Our results show that VLMs frequently output outdated facts, reflecting outdated snapshots used in the (pre-)training phase. Factual reliability degrades from textual to visual stimuli, even when entities are correctly recognized. Besides, existing alignment approaches fail to consistently update the models' knowledge across modalities. Together, these findings highlight fundamental limitations in how current VLMs acquire and update time-sensitive knowledge across modalities. We release the benchmark, code, and evaluation data.

### [126] [Runtime Governance for AI Agents: Policies on Paths](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16586)
*Maurits Kaptein,Vassilis-Javed Khan,Andriy Podstavnychy*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a formal framework for runtime governance of AI agents based on evaluating their execution paths against probabilistic compliance policies.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: AI agents based on large language models exhibit non-deterministic, path-dependent behavior that cannot be fully controlled at design time. Organizations need a principled way to balance task success with legal, security, and reputational risks when deploying such agents, especially in light of emerging regulation like the AI Act.

Method: The authors conceptualize the AI agent's execution path as the central object of governance and define compliance policies as deterministic functions that map the agent's identity, partial execution path, proposed next action, and organizational state to a probability of policy violation. They situate prompt engineering, system prompts, and static access control as restricted special cases of this more general runtime evaluation framework, and outline a reference implementation and policy examples.

Result: They show theoretically how various existing control mechanisms (prompts, access control) fit as special cases within their path-based policy framework and demonstrate how runtime policy evaluation can express path-dependent constraints inspired by regulatory requirements such as those in the AI Act.

Conclusion: Runtime, path-based evaluation of AI agent behavior is both more general and necessary for enforcing path-dependent compliance policies. The paper provides a formal foundation, illustrative policies, and an implementation sketch, while highlighting unresolved challenges like calibrating risk probabilities and understanding the fundamental limits of enforceable compliance for non-deterministic agents.

Abstract: AI agents -- systems that plan, reason, and act using large language models -- produce non-deterministic, path-dependent behavior that cannot be fully governed at design time, where with governed we mean striking the right balance between as high as possible successful task completion rate and the legal, data-breach, reputational and other costs associated with running agents. We argue that the execution path is the central object for effective runtime governance and formalize compliance policies as deterministic functions mapping agent identity, partial path, proposed next action, and organizational state to a policy violation probability. We show that prompt-level instructions (and "system prompts"), and static access control are special cases of this framework: the former shape the distribution over paths without actually evaluating them; the latter evaluates deterministic policies that ignore the path (i.e., these can only account for a specific subset of all possible paths). In our view, runtime evaluation is the general case, and it is necessary for any path-dependent policy. We develop the formal framework for analyzing AI agent governance, present concrete policy examples (inspired by the AI act), discuss a reference implementation, and identify open problems including risk calibration and the limits of enforced compliance.

### [127] [When AI Navigates the Fog of War](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16642)
*Ming Li,Xirui Li,Tianyi Zhou*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper evaluates whether large language models can meaningfully reason about an ongoing war in its early stages, using a carefully time-grounded benchmark built around the 2026 Middle East conflict.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Assessing LLMs’ geopolitical forecasting and reasoning is hard because most historical conflicts are already part of their training data, causing leakage and hindsight bias. The authors want a way to test how models analyze and forecast an unfolding war *as it happens*, under uncertainty and incomplete information, to better understand the strengths and limits of AI in high-stakes, real‑time geopolitical analysis.

Method: They select the 2026 Middle East conflict, which postdates the training cutoff of current frontier models, and construct a temporally grounded benchmark. This consists of 11 key temporal nodes (distinct moments in the unfolding conflict), 42 verifiable node‑specific questions, and 5 broader exploratory questions. At each node, models only get information that would have been publicly available *up to that time*, so they must reason without access to later developments. The authors then systematically analyze models’ answers for strategic depth, accuracy, and how narratives change over time.

Result: They find: (1) state‑of‑the‑art LLMs often show notable strategic realism, going beyond official rhetoric to reason about underlying incentives and constraints; (2) performance is uneven—models do better in domains with clearer economic or logistical structure and are less reliable in messy, politically ambiguous, multi‑actor contexts; and (3) as the conflict progresses, model narratives shift from expecting quick containment to describing a more entrenched regional conflict with slow, attritional dynamics of de‑escalation.

Conclusion: Temporally grounding evaluation around an ongoing conflict effectively reduces training‑data leakage and hindsight bias, providing a more valid testbed for AI reasoning under the “fog of war.” The study shows that LLMs can exhibit sophisticated strategic reasoning but with domain‑specific weaknesses and evolving narratives over time. The authors present their work as an archival snapshot of model behavior during an active crisis, intended to support future, more hindsight‑informed analyses of AI geopolitical forecasting and decision support.

Abstract: Can AI reason about a war before its trajectory becomes historically obvious? Analyzing this capability is difficult because retrospective geopolitical prediction is heavily confounded by training-data leakage. We address this challenge through a temporally grounded case study of the early stages of the 2026 Middle East conflict, which unfolded after the training cutoff of current frontier models. We construct 11 critical temporal nodes, 42 node-specific verifiable questions, and 5 general exploratory questions, requiring models to reason only from information that would have been publicly available at each moment. This design substantially mitigates training-data leakage concerns, creating a setting well-suited for studying how models analyze an unfolding crisis under the fog of war, and provides, to our knowledge, the first temporally grounded analysis of LLM reasoning in an ongoing geopolitical conflict. Our analysis reveals three main findings. First, current state-of-the-art large language models often display a striking degree of strategic realism, reasoning beyond surface rhetoric toward deeper structural incentives. Second, this capability is uneven across domains: models are more reliable in economically and logistically structured settings than in politically ambiguous multi-actor environments. Finally, model narratives evolve over time, shifting from early expectations of rapid containment toward more systemic accounts of regional entrenchment and attritional de-escalation. Since the conflict remains ongoing at the time of writing, this work can serve as an archival snapshot of model reasoning during an unfolding geopolitical crisis, enabling future studies without the hindsight bias of retrospective analysis.

### [128] [Domain-Independent Dynamic Programming with Constraint Propagation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16648)
*Imko Marijnissen,J. Christopher Beck,Emir Demirović,Ryo Kuroiwa*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper integrates constraint programming-style propagation into dynamic programming to prune states and transitions, improving DP’s efficiency on several scheduling and routing problems.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Model-based approaches for combinatorial optimization are largely split between dynamic programming (state-based) and constraint programming / MIP / SAT (constraint and domain-based). Each paradigm has complementary strengths, but there is a gap in how they can be combined: DP explores explicit states systematically but often lacks powerful pruning, while CP offers strong constraint propagation and pruning but may not exploit DP-like structure. The authors aim to bridge this gap to get the best of both worlds: DP’s structured search with CP’s propagation power.

Method: They extend a Domain-Independent Dynamic Programming (DIDP) framework so that each DP state and transition is filtered using constraint propagation provided by a general-purpose CP solver. Essentially, as DP generates states and possible transitions, the CP solver is invoked to propagate constraints and prune inconsistent or dominated states/transitions. The method is evaluated within a heuristic search over DP state space on three benchmark combinatorial optimization problems: Single Machine Scheduling with Time Windows, RCPSP, and TSP with Time Windows. Performance is measured in terms of the number of state expansions, solved instances, and runtime, with and without propagation.

Result: Constraint propagation within DP substantially reduces the number of state expansions on all three problems. For Single Machine Scheduling and RCPSP, the integrated approach solves more instances than a pure DP solver. For tightly constrained TSPTW instances, similar improvements are seen. Runtime experiments show that, for constrained instances, the pruning benefits from propagation more than compensate for the overhead of calling the CP solver, though for other cases the overhead can be significant.

Conclusion: Integrating CP-style constraint propagation into a generic DP framework is both feasible and beneficial: it can markedly reduce search effort and improve the practical power of DP solvers on constrained combinatorial optimization problems. The study demonstrates a concrete, model-based way to combine the DP and CP paradigms and highlights that further optimization of propagation overhead could yield even better performance. This work positions constraint propagation as a valuable component within dynamic programming solvers and a promising direction for future hybrid methods.

Abstract: There are two prevalent model-based paradigms for combinatorial problems: 1) state-based representations, such as heuristic search, dynamic programming (DP), and decision diagrams, and 2) constraint and domain-based representations, such as constraint programming (CP), (mixed-)integer programming, and Boolean satisfiability. In this paper, we bridge the gap between the DP and CP paradigms by integrating constraint propagation into DP, enabling a DP solver to prune states and transitions using constraint propagation. To this end, we implement constraint propagation using a general-purpose CP solver in the Domain-Independent Dynamic Programming framework and evaluate using heuristic search on three combinatorial optimisation problems: Single Machine Scheduling with Time Windows, the Resource Constrained Project Scheduling Problem (RCPSP), and the Travelling Salesperson Problem with Time Windows (TSPTW). Our evaluation shows that constraint propagation significantly reduces the number of state expansions, causing our approach to solve more instances than a DP solver for Single Machine Scheduling and RCPSP, and showing similar improvements for tightly constrained TSPTW instances. The runtime performance indicates that the benefits of propagation outweigh the overhead for constrained instances, but that further work into reducing propagation overhead could improve performance further. Our work is a key step in understanding the value of constraint propagation in DP solvers, providing a model-based approach to integrating DP and CP.

</details>

Inspired by the story of Pinocchio in ``Le avventure di Pinocchio - Storia di un burattino'', this thesis proposes a pipeline that addresses the problem of developing norm compliant and context-aware agents. Building on the AJAR, Jiminy, and NGRL architectures, the work introduces \pino, a hybrid model in which reinforcement learning agents are supervised by argumentation-based normative advisors. In order to make this pipeline operational, this thesis also presents a novel algorithm for automatically extracting the arguments and relationships that underlie the advisors' decisions. Finally, this thesis investigates the phenomenon of \textit{norm avoidance}, providing a definition and a mitigation strategy within the context of reinforcement learning agents. Each component of the pipeline is empirically evaluated. The thesis concludes with a discussion of related work, current limitations, and directions for future research.

  <summary>Details</summary>

This taste is used constantly by editors and funders but has never been formalized or automated. The authors aim to determine whether this evaluative capacity can be extracted from institutional records and encoded into AI models to help triage the growing volume of scientific work whose quality is hard to verify ex ante.

Method: The authors fine-tune large language models on historical journal publication decisions in management (and separately in economics). The models’ performance is compared to baseline frontier LLMs (both open and proprietary) and to panels of human experts (journal editors and editorial board members). They also assess calibration and generalization by examining model confidence, transfer to pairwise comparisons, and performance on one-sentence summaries.

Result: Across eleven frontier models, accuracy on classifying research pitches into four quality tiers hovers near chance (31%). Human expert panels achieve 42% accuracy. Fine-tuned models trained on historical publication decisions significantly outperform both baselines, with the best model reaching 59% accuracy in management and 70% in economics. The fine-tuned models show strong calibration, achieving 100% accuracy on their most confident predictions, and their learned evaluative signal generalizes to related tasks (pairwise pitch comparisons and one-sentence summaries) without additional training.

Conclusion: Scientific 'taste'—the ability to recognize which novel ideas merit pursuit—can be extracted from historical publication decisions and embedded in language models. These fine-tuned models not only surpass generic frontier LLMs but also outperform panels of experienced editors, and they exhibit reliable confidence calibration and cross-task generalization. This suggests a scalable, domain-transferable mechanism for triaging scientific work in fields where quality is hard to verify in advance, implying that institutional records contain a latent, machine-learnable representation of evaluative judgment that can augment human decision-making in research evaluation.

Abstract: Artificial intelligence matches or exceeds human performance on tasks with verifiable answers, from protein folding to Olympiad mathematics. Yet the capacity that most governs scientific advance is not reasoning but taste: the ability to judge which untested ideas deserve pursuit, exercised daily by editors and funders but never successfully articulated, taught, or automated. Here we show that fine-tuning language models on journal publication decisions recovers evaluative judgment inaccessible to both frontier models and human expertise. Using a held-out benchmark of research pitches in management spanning four quality tiers, we find that eleven frontier models, spanning major proprietary and open architectures, barely exceed chance, averaging 31% accuracy. Panels of journal editors and editorial board members reach 42% by majority vote. Fine-tuned models trained on years of publication records each surpass every frontier model and expert panel, with the best single model achieving 59%. These models exhibit calibrated confidence, reaching 100% accuracy on their highest-confidence predictions, and transfer this evaluative signal to untrained pairwise comparisons and one-sentence summaries. The mechanism generalizes: models trained on economics publication records achieve 70% accuracy. Scientific taste was not missing from AI's reach; it was deposited in the institutional record, waiting to be extracted. These results provide a scalable mechanism to triage the expanding volume of scientific production across disciplines where quality resists formal verification.

### [132] [IQuest-Coder-V1 Technical Report](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16733)
*Jian Yang,Wei Zhang,Shawn Guo,Zhengmao Ye,Lin Jing,Shark Liu,Yizhi Li,Jiajun Wu,Cening Liu,X. Ma,Yuyang Song,Siwei Wu,Yuwen Li,L. Liao,T. Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduces IQuest-Coder-V1, a family of code LLMs trained with a multi-stage, code-flow-centric pipeline to achieve state-of-the-art code intelligence and agentic capabilities, including an efficient recurrent variant.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing code LLMs mostly rely on static code snapshots and single-stage or loosely staged training, which limits their ability to reason about evolving program logic, operate as autonomous coding agents, and scale efficiently under deployment constraints. There is a need for models that better capture the dynamic flow of software development, support long-context repository reasoning, and balance performance with resource usage.

Method: They design a code-flow multi-stage training paradigm. First, they pre-train models on code facts, repositories, and completion data. Second, they introduce a mid-training stage that injects reasoning and agentic trajectories using 32k-context and repository-level 128k-context data to build deep logical and long-context foundations. Finally, they perform post-training specialized into two branches: a thinking path using reasoning-driven reinforcement learning for deep problem solving, and an instruct path for general coding assistance. Additionally, they create a "Loop" variant with a recurrent mechanism to improve efficiency-capacity trade-offs. The full chain of checkpoints from base to final models is released for research.

Result: The IQuest-Coder-V1 models (7B/14B/40B/40B-Loop) achieve state-of-the-art performance among competitive code LLMs on benchmarks that probe agentic software engineering, competitive programming, and complex tool usage. The recurrent Loop variant demonstrates that recurrent architectural modifications can meaningfully improve the trade-off between model capacity and deployment footprint.

Conclusion: A code-flow, multi-stage training pipeline that explicitly models evolving program logic and long-context reasoning, combined with specialized post-training for thinking and instruction, can substantially advance code intelligence and agentic capabilities. The recurrent Loop design offers a practical path toward more deployable yet high-performing code LLMs. By releasing a transparent sequence of checkpoints, the work aims to catalyze further research on autonomous code intelligence and real-world software agents.

Abstract: In this report, we introduce the IQuest-Coder-V1 series-(7B/14B/40B/40B-Loop), a new family of code large language models (LLMs). Moving beyond static code representations, we propose the code-flow multi-stage training paradigm, which captures the dynamic evolution of software logic through different phases of the pipeline. Our models are developed through the evolutionary pipeline, starting with the initial pre-training consisting of code facts, repository, and completion data. Following that, we implement a specialized mid-training stage that integrates reasoning and agentic trajectories in 32k-context and repository-scale in 128k-context to forge deep logical foundations. The models are then finalized with post-training of specialized coding capabilities, which is bifurcated into two specialized paths: the thinking path (utilizing reasoning-driven RL) and the instruct path (optimized for general assistance). IQuest-Coder-V1 achieves state-of-the-art performance among competitive models across critical dimensions of code intelligence: agentic software engineering, competitive programming, and complex tool use. To address deployment constraints, the IQuest-Coder-V1-Loop variant introduces a recurrent mechanism designed to optimize the trade-off between model capacity and deployment footprint, offering an architecturally enhanced path for efficacy-efficiency trade-off. We believe the release of the IQuest-Coder-V1 series, including the complete white-box chain of checkpoints from pre-training bases to the final thinking and instruction models, will advance research in autonomous code intelligence and real-world agentic systems.

</details>


### [133] [Differential Harm Propensity in Personalized LLM Agents: The Curious Case of Mental Health Disclosure](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16734)
*Caglar Yildirim*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how user personalization signals—especially mental health disclosure—affect how LLM agents complete harmful vs. benign multi-step tasks, finding small protective but fragile effects that vanish under simple jailbreaks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most safety work on LLM agents evaluates them in non-personalized settings, even though real deployments often condition on user profiles and persistent memory. Mental health-related context is both realistic and sensitive, and it is unclear whether it makes agents safer (more cautious) or more dangerous (more easily exploited or more helpful in risky behaviors). The authors aim to systematically quantify how such personalization affects harmful task completion and refusals in agentic, tool-using setups.

Method: They extend the AgentHarm benchmark of multi-step malicious and benign tasks. Multiple models (frontier and open-source) are evaluated under controlled prompting conditions that vary user-context personalization: (1) no bio, (2) bio-only, and (3) bio plus explicit mental health disclosure. They also introduce a lightweight jailbreak injection. For each condition, they measure harmful task completion rates, refusal rates, and effects on benign tasks, using statistical tests with multiple-testing correction to assess the reliability of observed effects.

Result: Harmful task completion remains non-trivial for all tested models. Frontier models such as GPT 5.2, Claude Sonnet 4.5, and Gemini 3-Pro still complete some harmful tasks, while an open model (DeepSeek 3.2) completes substantially more. Adding a non-mental-health bio generally lowers harm scores and raises refusal rates. Adding explicit mental health disclosure typically further nudges models toward less harmful behavior and more refusals, but these effects are modest and not always statistically robust after correcting for multiple comparisons. Increased refusals also occur on benign tasks, revealing a safety–utility trade-off where personalization induces over-refusal. Simple jailbreak prompts significantly raise harmful completion and can negate or overpower the mild protective effect of personalization signals.

Conclusion: Personalization, including mental health disclosure, can slightly reduce harmful task completion in LLM agents, but the effect is weak, inconsistent, and easily disrupted by minimal adversarial prompting. Moreover, personalization may degrade utility by increasing refusals even for benign requests. Therefore, safety evaluations and deployed safeguards must explicitly account for user-context personalization and be robust to both personalization cues and simple jailbreak attempts, rather than assuming that sensitive context alone will reliably improve safety.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly deployed as tool-using agents, shifting safety concerns from harmful text generation to harmful task completion. Deployed systems often condition on user profiles or persistent memory, yet agent safety evaluations typically ignore personalization signals. To address this gap, we investigated how mental health disclosure, a sensitive and realistic user-context cue, affects harmful behavior in agentic settings. Building on the AgentHarm benchmark, we evaluated frontier and open-source LLMs on multi-step malicious tasks (and their benign counterparts) under controlled prompt conditions that vary user-context personalization (no bio, bio-only, bio+mental health disclosure) and include a lightweight jailbreak injection. Our results reveal that harmful task completion is non-trivial across models: frontier lab models (e.g., GPT 5.2, Claude Sonnet 4.5, Gemini 3-Pro) still complete a measurable fraction of harmful tasks, while an open model (DeepSeek 3.2) exhibits substantially higher harmful completion. Adding a bio-only context generally reduces harm scores and increases refusals. Adding an explicit mental health disclosure often shifts outcomes further in the same direction, though effects are modest and not uniformly reliable after multiple-testing correction. Importantly, the refusal increase also appears on benign tasks, indicating a safety--utility trade-off via over-refusal. Finally, jailbreak prompting sharply elevates harm relative to benign conditions and can weaken or override the protective shift induced by personalization. Taken together, our results indicate that personalization can act as a weak protective factor in agentic misuse settings, but it is fragile under minimal adversarial pressure, highlighting the need for personalization-aware evaluations and safeguards that remain robust across user-context conditions.

### [134] [MedCL-Bench: Benchmarking stability-efficiency trade-offs and scaling in biomedical continual learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16738)
*Min Zeng,Shuang Zhou,Zaifu Zhan,Rui Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They introduce MedCL-Bench, a unified continual-learning benchmark for medical language models that evaluates forgetting, transfer, and compute cost across many tasks and update strategies.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Medical language models need continual updating as medical evidence and terminology change, but naive sequential fine-tuning leads to catastrophic forgetting; there is no standardized, task-diverse, compute-aware benchmark to evaluate continual learning methods in this domain.

Method: They create MedCL-Bench, a benchmark that streams ten biomedical NLP datasets from five task families, defines standardized continual-learning protocols, and evaluates eleven continual learning methods over eight different task orders while tracking metrics such as retention, forward/backward transfer, robustness to task order, and GPU-hour cost.

Result: Sequential fine-tuning causes strong catastrophic forgetting across backbones and task orders. Different continual learning strategies trace distinct trade-off frontiers between performance retention and compute cost: parameter-isolation methods offer the best retention per GPU-hour, replay-based methods provide strong protection from forgetting but are more compute-intensive, and regularization-based methods yield relatively small gains. Forgetting patterns depend heavily on task type, with multi-label topic classification being most susceptible and constrained-output tasks more stable.

Conclusion: MedCL-Bench serves as a reproducible, compute-aware evaluation framework for auditing continual updating strategies for medical language models prior to deployment, highlighting that naive sequential fine-tuning is unsafe and that method choice should balance retention, compute budget, and task characteristics.

Abstract: Medical language models must be updated as evidence and terminology evolve, yet sequential updating can trigger catastrophic forgetting. Although biomedical NLP has many static benchmarks, no unified, task-diverse benchmark exists for evaluating continual learning under standardized protocols, robustness to task order and compute-aware reporting. We introduce MedCL-Bench, which streams ten biomedical NLP datasets spanning five task families and evaluates eleven continual learning strategies across eight task orders, reporting retention, transfer, and GPU-hour cost. Across backbones and task orders, direct sequential fine-tuning on incoming tasks induces catastrophic forgetting, causing update-induced performance regressions on prior tasks. Continual learning methods occupy distinct retention-compute frontiers: parameter-isolation provides the best retention per GPU-hour, replay offers strong protection at higher cost, and regularization yields limited benefit. Forgetting is task-dependent, with multi-label topic classification most vulnerable and constrained-output tasks more robust. MedCL-Bench provides a reproducible framework for auditing model updates before deployment.

### [135] [Is Conformal Factuality for RAG-based LLMs Robust? Novel Metrics and Systematic Insights](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16817)
*Yi Chen,Daiwei Chen,Sukrut Madhav Chikodikar,Caitlyn Heqi Yin,Ramya Korlakai Vinayak*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper evaluates and stress-tests conformal factuality filtering for RAG-based LLMs, showing that while it can give statistical factuality guarantees, it often harms usefulness, is fragile under distribution shift and distractors, and that lightweight entailment verifiers can be more efficient and equally or more effective than LLM-based confidence scorers.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs hallucinate often, which is problematic for knowledge-intensive tasks. Existing fixes like RAG lack statistical guarantees of correctness, while conformal factuality offers guarantees but may hurt informativeness. There is little systematic understanding of how well conformal factuality actually works in realistic RAG settings, especially regarding the trade-off between factuality and usefulness, robustness to distribution shift, and computational cost. This gap motivates a thorough analysis to guide the design of reliable and practical RAG pipelines.

Method: The authors evaluate conformal factuality filtering applied to RAG-based LLMs along several axes: (1) generation: how responses are produced with retrieval; (2) scoring: comparing different claim-level factuality scorers, including lightweight entailment-based verifiers and LLM-based confidence scorers; (3) calibration: using conformal prediction on held-out data to set thresholds guaranteeing a target factuality level; (4) robustness: testing behavior under distribution shifts and presence of distractor information; and (5) efficiency: measuring FLOPs and computational cost. They introduce new informativeness-aware metrics that jointly measure factuality and usefulness of filtered outputs, and they run experiments across three benchmarks and multiple model families.

Result: Empirically, they find: (i) to reach high factuality guarantees, conformal filtering tends to yield vacuous or nearly empty answers, drastically reducing usefulness; (ii) the conformal factuality guarantees break down under distribution shift and in the presence of distractors, indicating that conformal calibration is highly sensitive to mismatches between calibration and deployment data; and (iii) simple, lightweight entailment-based verifiers can match or even outperform more expensive LLM-based confidence scorers for conformal filtering, while being over 100× cheaper in FLOPs. These results quantify a strong trade-off between factuality and informativeness and expose the fragility of current conformal-filtering-based reliability schemes for RAG.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that current conformal factuality filtering is not a complete solution for reliable RAG systems: it faces a fundamental factuality–informativeness trade-off and lacks robustness to dataset shifts and distractors, even though it offers formal guarantees under ideal conditions. Lightweight entailment verifiers are a promising, efficient alternative to LLM-based scoring. The authors argue that future reliability work should explicitly optimize for robustness and usefulness, not just formal guarantees, and they provide practical guidance for designing RAG pipelines that are both statistically grounded and computationally efficient.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) frequently hallucinate, limiting their reliability in knowledge-intensive applications. Retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) and conformal factuality have emerged as potential ways to address this limitation. While RAG aims to ground responses in retrieved evidence, it provides no statistical guarantee that the final output is correct. Conformal factuality filtering offers distribution-free statistical reliability by scoring and filtering atomic claims using a threshold calibrated on held-out data, however, the informativeness of the final output is not guaranteed. We systematically analyze the reliability and usefulness of conformal factuality for RAG-based LLMs across generation, scoring, calibration, robustness, and efficiency. We propose novel informativeness-aware metrics that better reflect task utility under conformal filtering. Across three benchmarks and multiple model families, we find that (i) conformal filtering suffers from low usefulness at high factuality levels due to vacuous outputs, (ii) conformal factuality guarantee is not robust to distribution shifts and distractors, highlighting the limitation that requires calibration data to closely match deployment conditions, and (iii) lightweight entailment-based verifiers match or outperform LLM-based model confidence scorers while requiring over $100\times$ fewer FLOPs. Overall, our results expose factuality-informativeness trade-offs and fragility of conformal filtering framework under distribution shifts and distractors, highlighting the need for new approaches for reliability with robustness and usefulness as key metrics, and provide actionable guidance for building RAG pipelines that are both reliable and computationally efficient.

</details>


### [136] [Nonstandard Errors in AI Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16744)
*Ruijiang Gao,Steven Chong Xiao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Study tests whether multiple cutting-edge AI coding agents, given identical data and hypotheses, produce consistent empirical results, and finds large variation (nonstandard errors) in their methodological choices and outcomes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As AI coding agents are increasingly used for empirical research and policy analysis, it is crucial to understand whether they deliver stable and replicable results, or whether their outputs vary substantially across runs and model variants, similar to researcher degrees of freedom among humans.

Method: The authors deploy 150 autonomous Claude Code agents to independently test six hypotheses about market quality trends using NYSE TAQ data for SPY (2015–2024). They compare agents’ choices of measures (e.g., autocorrelation vs. variance ratio; dollar vs. share volume) and estimation approaches across two model families (Sonnet 4.6 vs. Opus 4.6). They then introduce a three-stage feedback protocol: (1) baseline independent analysis, (2) AI peer review via written critiques, and (3) exposure to top-rated exemplar papers, and measure how dispersion in empirical estimates changes.

Result: AI agents show substantial agent-to-agent variation—nonstandard errors—due to differing analytical choices, resembling human researcher heterogeneity. The two model families display distinct and stable empirical styles in terms of preferred measures and methods. AI peer review alone barely reduces dispersion, but showing agents exemplar papers dramatically reduces the interquartile range of estimates (by 80–99%) within converging measure families. Convergence arises both from more tightly estimated results within a chosen family and from agents switching to the exemplar’s measure family.

Conclusion: State-of-the-art AI coding agents do not naturally converge on a single empirical answer; instead, they exhibit considerable methodological heterogeneity that induces nonstandard errors, echoing concerns about researcher degrees of freedom. Structured feedback using exemplar work can force strong convergence, but largely through imitation rather than genuine methodological understanding. This has important implications for the reliability and interpretability of AI-driven empirical research and automated policy evaluation, especially when different AI runs or models might yield systematically different conclusions.

Abstract: We study whether state-of-the-art AI coding agents, given the same data and research question, produce the same empirical results. Deploying 150 autonomous Claude Code agents to independently test six hypotheses about market quality trends in NYSE TAQ data for SPY (2015--2024), we find that AI agents exhibit sizable \textit{nonstandard errors} (NSEs), that is, uncertainty from agent-to-agent variation in analytical choices, analogous to those documented among human researchers. AI agents diverge substantially on measure choice (e.g., autocorrelation vs.\ variance ratio, dollar vs.\ share volume). Different model families (Sonnet 4.6 vs.\ Opus 4.6) exhibit stable ``empirical styles,'' reflecting systematic differences in methodological preferences. In a three-stage feedback protocol, AI peer review (written critiques) has minimal effect on dispersion, whereas exposure to top-rated exemplar papers reduces the interquartile range of estimates by 80--99\% within \textit{converging} measure families. Convergence occurs both through within-family estimation tightening and through agents switching measure families entirely, but convergence reflects imitation rather than understanding. These findings have implications for the growing use of AI in automated policy evaluation and empirical research.

Conclusion: Survey-based cultural alignment metrics generalize from proprietary to open-weight LLMs, confirming that cultural bias and the usefulness of cultural conditioning are not model-specific. Abstract: Culture shapes reasoning, values, prioritization, and strategic decision-making, yet large language models (LLMs) often exhibit cultural biases that misalign with target populations. As LLMs are increasingly used for strategic decision-making, policy support, and document engineering tasks such as summarization, categorization, and compliance-oriented auditing, improving cultural alignment is important for ensuring that downstream analyses and recommendations reflect target-population value profiles rather than default model priors. Previous work introduced a survey-grounded cultural alignment framework and showed that culture-specific prompting can reduce misalignment, but it primarily evaluated proprietary models and relied on manual prompt engineering. In this paper, we validate and extend that framework by reproducing its social sciences survey based projection and distance metrics on open-weight LLMs, testing whether the same cultural skew and benefits of culture conditioning persist outside closed LLM systems. Building on this foundation, we introduce use of prompt programming with DSPy for this problem-treating prompts as modular, optimizable programs-to systematically tune cultural conditioning by optimizing against cultural-distance objectives. In our experiments, we show that prompt optimization often improves upon cultural prompt engineering, suggesting prompt compilation with DSPy can provide a more stable and transferable route to culturally aligned LLM responses.

  <summary>Details</summary>

The paper aims to build agents that can plan and act coherently over multiple steps in realistic computer-use and tool-use scenarios.

Method: The authors propose TraceR1, a two-stage reinforcement learning framework. Stage 1 performs trajectory-level RL where the agent predicts short-horizon action sequences and receives rewards enforcing global consistency across the whole predicted trajectory before execution. Stage 2 conducts grounded reinforcement fine-tuning where the model is refined using execution feedback from frozen tool agents, focusing on improving step-wise accuracy and executability of the previously anticipated trajectories.

Result: Across seven benchmarks that span online computer-use, offline computer-use benchmarks, and multimodal tool-use reasoning tasks, TraceR1 shows large gains in planning stability, robustness of execution, and generalization compared to reactive agents and single-stage RL baselines.

Conclusion: Anticipatory trajectory reasoning—explicitly forecasting and optimizing short-horizon action sequences before acting—is crucial for building multimodal agents capable of reliable reasoning, planning, and acting in complex real-world environments. The two-stage TraceR1 framework effectively instills such capabilities, leading to more coherent long-term behavior than reactive and single-stage approaches.

Abstract: Recent advances in multimodal agents have improved computer-use interaction and tool-usage, yet most existing systems remain reactive, optimizing actions in isolation without reasoning about future states or long-term goals. This limits planning coherence and prevents agents from reliably solving high-level, multi-step tasks. We introduce TraceR1, a two-stage reinforcement learning framework that explicitly trains anticipatory reasoning by forecasting short-horizon trajectories before execution. The first stage performs trajectory-level reinforcement learning with rewards that enforce global consistency across predicted action sequences. The second stage applies grounded reinforcement fine-tuning, using execution feedback from frozen tool agents to refine step-level accuracy and executability. TraceR1 is evaluated across seven benchmarks, covering online computer-use, offline computer-use benchmarks, and multimodal tool-use reasoning tasks, where it achieves substantial improvements in planning stability, execution robustness, and generalization over reactive and single-stage baselines. These results show that anticipatory trajectory reasoning is a key principle for building multimodal agents that can reason, plan, and act effectively in complex real-world environments.

*Rui Ge,Yichao Fu,Yuyang Qian,Junda Su,Yiming Zhao,Peng Zhao,Hao Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces LEAFE, a framework that trains language-model agents to better use environment feedback and recover from mistakes in long, interactive tasks, improving both single-try and multi-try success rates over existing post-training methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing outcome-driven post-training methods for large language model agents (e.g., RL with verifiable rewards) mainly optimize for final success signals such as whether a task is completed, but they underuse the rich, intermediate feedback provided by interactive environments. This leads to distribution sharpening: the policy gets better at repeating a narrow range of already-successful behaviors without truly improving its capacity to handle errors, explore alternatives, and expand its problem-solving abilities in long-horizon tasks. There is a need for a training approach that explicitly leverages environment feedback to build recovery and reflection capabilities, so that agents can more robustly handle complex, multi-step interactions within fixed interaction budgets.

Method: The authors propose LEAFE (Learning Feedback-Grounded Agency from Reflective Experience). During exploration in an environment, the agent interacts as usual but, instead of treating the trajectory only by its final outcome, it summarizes intermediate environment feedback into actionable experience. The agent then backtracks to earlier decision points, uses the summarized experience to reflect on what went wrong or what could be improved, and explores alternative branches with revised actions. These experience-guided corrections—essentially demonstrations of how to recover from or avoid mistakes—are collected and then distilled into the model via supervised fine-tuning. This process trains the policy to internalize recovery strategies grounded in environment feedback, rather than only optimizing for final rewards.

Result: Using a fixed interaction budget, the authors evaluate LEAFE on a variety of interactive coding and agentic tasks. LEAFE consistently improves Pass@1 performance over the base model, indicating better one-shot success. It also achieves higher Pass@k than outcome-driven RL baselines such as GRPO and experience-based methods such as Early Experience, with improvements up to 14% on Pass@128. These results suggest that LEAFE not only enhances immediate performance but also expands the diversity and robustness of successful behaviors across multiple attempts.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that explicitly learning from reflective, feedback-grounded experience enables language-model agents to develop stronger recovery capabilities and more effective long-horizon problem-solving. By summarizing environment feedback, backtracking, and exploring alternative actions, LEAFE converts rich interaction signals into supervised training data that improves both Pass@1 and Pass@k. This approach outperforms standard outcome-driven RL and simpler experience-based baselines, demonstrating that leveraging reflective experience is a promising direction for advancing autonomous LLM agents under realistic interaction constraints.

Abstract: Large language models are increasingly deployed as autonomous agents that must plan, act, and recover from mistakes through long-horizon interaction with environments that provide rich feedback. However, prevailing outcome-driven post-training methods (e.g., RL with verifiable rewards) primarily optimize final success signals, leaving rich environment feedback underutilized. Consequently, they often lead to distribution sharpening: the policy becomes better at reproducing a narrow set of already-successful behaviors, while failing to improve the feedback-grounded agency needed to expand problem-solving capacity (e.g., Pass@k) in long-horizon settings.
  To address this, we propose LEAFE (Learning Feedback-Grounded Agency from Reflective Experience), a framework that internalizes recovery agency from reflective experience. Specifically, during exploration, the agent summarizes environment feedback into actionable experience, backtracks to earlier decision points, and explores alternative branches with revised actions. We then distill these experience-guided corrections into the model through supervised fine-tuning, enabling the policy to recover more effectively in future interactions. Across a diverse set of interactive coding and agentic tasks under fixed interaction budgets, LEAFE consistently improves Pass@1 over the base model and achieves higher Pass@k than outcome-driven baselines (GRPO) and experience-based methods such as Early Experience, with gains of up to 14% on Pass@128.

</details>


### [143] [SocialOmni: Benchmarking Audio-Visual Social Interactivity in Omni Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16859)
*Tianyu Xie,Jinfa Huang,Yuexiao Ma,Rongfang Luo,Yan Yang,Wang Chen,Yuhui Zeng,Ruize Fang,Yixuan Zou,Xiawu Zheng,Jiebo Luo,Rongrong Ji*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces SocialOmni, a benchmark to evaluate omni-modal large language models on social conversational interactivity rather than just static accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing omni-modal LLM benchmarks focus mainly on static, accuracy-based tasks and do not capture models’ abilities to handle dynamic, socially interactive dialogue cues across audio, vision, and text. There is a need to measure how well models manage real conversational behaviors like identifying speakers and making natural interruptions.

Method: The authors design SocialOmni, a benchmark with three evaluation dimensions: (1) speaker separation and identification (who is speaking), (2) interruption timing control (when to interject), and (3) natural interruption generation (how to phrase interruptions). The benchmark contains 2,000 perception samples plus 209 carefully curated interaction-generation instances with strict temporal and contextual constraints. It also includes controlled audio-visual inconsistency scenarios. They then evaluate 12 state-of-the-art omni-modal LLMs on these tasks.

Result: The evaluation reveals large differences among 12 leading omni-modal LLMs in their social interaction abilities. The authors find that high perceptual accuracy (e.g., correctly recognizing content or speakers) does not necessarily imply good performance in generating appropriate, context-sensitive interruptions, showing a decoupling between perception and interaction capabilities.

Conclusion: Traditional understanding-focused metrics are inadequate to characterize social conversational competence in omni-modal LLMs. SocialOmni provides more diagnostic, interaction-oriented evaluations that expose the gap between perception and interaction, and it offers actionable guidance to improve future OLMs so they can better handle socially interactive conversations.

Abstract: Omni-modal large language models (OLMs) redefine human-machine interaction by natively integrating audio, vision, and text. However, existing OLM benchmarks remain anchored to static, accuracy-centric tasks, leaving a critical gap in assessing social interactivity, the fundamental capacity to navigate dynamic cues in natural dialogues. To this end, we propose SocialOmni, a comprehensive benchmark that operationalizes the evaluation of this conversational interactivity across three core dimensions: (i) speaker separation and identification (who is speaking), (ii) interruption timing control (when to interject), and (iii) natural interruption generation (how to phrase the interruption). SocialOmni features 2,000 perception samples and a quality-controlled diagnostic set of 209 interaction-generation instances with strict temporal and contextual constraints, complemented by controlled audio-visual inconsistency scenarios to test model robustness. We benchmarked 12 leading OLMs, which uncovers significant variance in their social-interaction capabilities across models. Furthermore, our analysis reveals a pronounced decoupling between a model's perceptual accuracy and its ability to generate contextually appropriate interruptions, indicating that understanding-centric metrics alone are insufficient to characterize conversational social competence. More encouragingly, these diagnostics from SocialOmni yield actionable signals for bridging the perception-interaction divide in future OLMs.

</details>
